“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
The Intercolonial Press Agency publishes “ The
Dis-Honourable,” a novel by J. D. Hennessey
(boards, 35.), which has all the elements of popu-
larity. Plot and style are commonplace, but the
beat of commonplace. The story has interest;
the treatment is capable and uniform ; and the
characters are not all melodramatic puppets. The
scene is laid at Brisbane ; and the author gives
incidental pictures of the floods of ’93, of the
land-booming politicians who ran the Govt, at
that epoch—a life-like and unflattering sketch,
and of a fairly typical dissenting brotherhood
whose portraits are done with vigor and insight.
Mr. Hennessey has produced one of the most pro-
mising Australian books printed for a long time.
He observes well; describes well ; and has a real
talent for characterisation. “ The Dis-Honor-
able ” is too conventional in essence to have per-
manent value; but some of the figures and inci-
dents are treated with a breadth and originality
that give hope of better things.
H. B. Marriott-Watson is a writer of quaint,
flavoury, old-fashioned prose, all melody and
picturesqueness, which would give distinction
even to ordinary ideas. As Mr. Watson’s ideas
are generally good, his sketches and stories are
uncommonly well worth reading. The episode of
“The Lady’s Chamber,” in the Now Review for
May, for example, is a bit of delicious art which
only “ Q.,” of contemporaries, could parallel.
And in “At the First Corner,” Mr. Watson’s
new book (cloth, 4s. 6d.), out of nine short stories
only the first and last do not impress—the one
merely unpleasant, the other mainly uncon-
vincing. The others range from fine to very fine);
and even their affectation touches the appreciative
palate as a subtle grace.
“ Gallia,” by Mene Muriel Dowie (paper, 3s. ;
cloth, 45.), is another “advanced” book made
around the idea of “ A Superfluous Woman ” that
modern girls do not live by husband alone, but
principally by baby. The husband is a rather
tiresome accessory before the great fact of the
goo-goo machine. The book iias some interest
and no value.
Kidd’s “Social Evolution” is published in a
cheaper edition (cloth, 6s. 6d.), and at that price
is worth buying. The book is suggestive, and a
notable contribution to the literature of revolt
from reason ; though the argument has already
neon described as dogmatic assumption supported
my the merest shell of evidence.
Lovers of Walter Scott should purchase the
'. harming new edition of his works, now pub-
i.fishing in 48 vols. (2s. 6d. each). The edition is
i reprint of the Author’s Favorite Edition, with
all the original plates; and size, type, paper and
i.inding are delightful. Vols. 1 and 2 ( Waverley)
are.to hand; and the others will follow at bi-
monthly intervals.
Grant Allen’s “ Woman Who Did ” (cloth, 4s.
6 1.) is as dull as most books with a purpose. The
surprising vogue it has achieved is due partly to
judicious putting, but chiefly to the attractive im-
propriety of the heroine. Mr. Allen’s statement
that the book was written '* wholly and solely to
satisfy his own taste and conscience” titillates
the reader into curiosity which broadens to
amusement as the chapters pass. The author’s
painful earnestness makes his story solemn ; but
it is not without interest, and it furnishes as a
whole a ponderous anticlimax to his well-meant
dogma.
Lord Wolseley’a “Decline and Fall of Napo-
leon ” (cloth, 3s. Ild.) is a compact and interesting
account of the campaigns from the invasion of
Russia to Waterloo, disfigured by truly British
cant and prejudice.
Allan Menzies’ “ History of Religion ” (cloth,
ss. 6d.) gives “a sketch of primitive religious
beliefs and practices, and of the origin and char-
a -ler of the great systems.” The book is lucid
and comprehensive, and is unusually free from
theological bias.
“The Infant,” by Frederick Wicks (Reming-
ton ; cloth, 6s. 6d.) is a novel of manners and char-
acter which only needs to be half as long and
twice as bright in order to be readable.
Lady Jeune is a plodding essayist who labors
with more or less success to extract suitable morals
from topics of the day. She is not at all a New
Woman ; and her “ Lesser Questions ” (Reming-
ton ; cloth, 45.) is a commentary respectable,
solid, and rather dull.
“ Bimetal!sm,” by H. D. MacLeod (cloth,
6s. 6d.), is mainly an historical review of currency,
with a summing-up in favor of monometallism.
The other side hardly gets fair statement; but the
book has value.
Ouida’s “Views and Opinions ” (paper, 35.;
cloth, 45.) is a weighty and interesting set of
essays on many various subjects. Ouida has
vigor and style, and is full of suggestion.

chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken

and a complete cure is certain.

assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub

PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
itizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
Jr the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
,wm the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in - bold relief- against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but it the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
never cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. . For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

Various Verses.
AT THE WATERFALL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Down in a deep, delightful glen,
On soft, dead leaves I lie—
The loveliest spot I yet have seen
Beneath the southern sky.
I look into dark and dreamy eyes,
And think of their witching power
To make me forget the flight of time
As I idle away the hour.
I’ll describe to you the delightful spot,
And_ I think I hear you say :
“ ’Tis just the place with a charming girl
To loiter away the day.”
There’s a wall of rugged red-brown rocks
O’er which limpid waters flow,
As they hurry along with a life-like leap
To their rest in the pool below ;
And the spray, as soft as a summer mist,
Is caught on the ferns around,
Where fancy sees a water-sprite
Whose hair with pearls is bound.
There are giant trees whose massy trunks
Attest the flight of time.
And lovely flowers to weave a wreath
Around a hat to twine.
As I lie at ease on the leaves and moss
And hold as soft a hand,
I ask no more for an idle hour
And sigh for no fairer land.
There’s an echo like to the vesper bell
I have heard in Italia’s clime —
Tho’ a stranger here I have learned to love
The bell-bird’s liquid chime ;
In varying tones its cadence rings
Through the lazy summer air,
And Music’s soul can claim than this
No melody more rare.
As the evening shadows grow in length
And the golden-rose lights fade,
I think of many as fair a scene
And of many a lovely maid.
I dream of eyes of the deepest blue
And of hair with a golden sheen—-
Of the loving looks and the warm caress
Received from my heart’s first queen.
And then I think of that Spanish girl
With hair like the raven’s wing,
And the bell-birds echo the passionate words
I heard the dark beauty sing;
While the jackass there, with his mocking laugh,
Who sits on the branch above,
Seems to ask me why to that beauty dark
I foolishly spoke of love.
I think of many a fairy scene,
Of ballrooms bright and gay,
And I wish for a dip in Lethe’s stream
To banish thought for aye.
For the golden beauty pined and died
And the dark turned out a shrew.
Ah ! many a sorrow deep I trace
To women. Do not you ?
I heard one breathe the tenderest vows
When she was wishing the while
For a ride with Tom, on an Arab horse
Down that darling “Ladies’ Mile.”
I have looked into eyes and truly felt
That Heaven’s whole truth was there,
When she was thinking “ was that blue dress
Becoming enough to wear?”
But a truce to this ! lam wiser now
And bask in a sunny smile,
Without enquiring whether the girl
Is thinking of me the while.
I like very much for a little hand
To caress and arrange my hair, ,
But I look on a woman and treat her just
As a flower rich and rare.
And as here I lie, on the soft dead leaves
And list to the bell-bird’s song,
I truly hope that my fate may not
Tie me to a woman long.
Sydney.
E. B. PARNELL.
A VISION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A cursed dream it was of light and dark,
Of ice-bound bodies retching sheets of flame,
Of fair souls shivering with secret shame ;
And others sudden stripped of splendor, stark,
Disclosed black hell-hounds whose thin hideous
bark
Ran trembling through the ink-depths as it came
Waking wild imprecations on a name,
Till shudd’ring Silence gasped an awful “ Hark !”
And Terror labored in that pregnant time,:
While Chaos leashed his thunders in his’hand—
Waiting a signal. Then, in whisper shrill,
“Life, Sin, or Death?” I questioned; but the
slime
Of horrors vanished from the wakening land—
Leaving its problem damned unanswered still.
N.S. Wales.
HESKETH.
A COUNTRY MAID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
. I meb a dainty country lass ;
The wind had kissed her cheek to flame ;
3he stepped, aside to let me pass,
- And dropped her eyes in maiden shame.
“ A pretty maid in lonely wood
Is sweet temptation to the blood.’’
Her age was eighteen years, she said.
Too young to wander all alone !
Ah. no, the rugged pathway led
Unto her native village soon,
, “ A pretty maid in lonely wood
Is sweet temptation to the blood.”
She blushed and lingered, sweetly grave,
I praised her eyes, and sighed adieu—
A merry, mocking smile she gave,
And down the forest swiftly flew.
“ A pretty maid in lonely wood
Is sweet temptation to the blood.”
Maoriland.

W IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
E beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-out No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. I,’’ have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THREE CASTLES ” and “ CAPSTAN.”
We have declined to. conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Whales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
II ills’ “Three Castles’ ’ and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
Tliree Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HETDE, TODMAN & CO. [Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

NOTICE.
By sending- direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—

Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for

SATURDAY, JULY 20, 1895.
Shoddy.
The great soul of the Victorian Assembly
has been roused to hysteria by the discovery,
or alleged discovery, that much of the
tweeds, flannels and other so-called woollen
goods of local manufacture are shoddy of
the shoddiest description. The press and
.the people of Melbourne and other towns
joined in the commotion, and for many days
and nights the shoddy upheaval raged with-
out ceasing. The Argus denounced the im-
position as another consequence of the blast-
ing influences of Protection, and pointed
out with much eloquent raving that when
local industries were fostered by a tariff such
results were inevitable. The Single-tax
organ published photographs of various
alleged samples of local flannel, said to be
nearly one-half cotton, and drew the same
lame and strabismused inference. Murray
Smith’s little band of Freetraders laid down
on the table of the House specimens which
they said they had been told were of local
workmanship, and which they stated, on
their own unsupported authority, to be
largely bogus and cotton ; and were of
opinion that, as these were the results
of Protection, Protection ought to be
abolished. Sundry weak and thick-headed
Protectionists admitted that their fiscal
view's were very much modified by
these painful revelations. And finally
the Assembly, to express its horror at
shoddy, and to prevent the continuance of
the evil, made a considerable reduction in
the duty on woollen goods generally. All
of which is a fine specimen of the stupidity
and logical incapacity of the average legis-
lator, and his ability to make a fool of
himself at the shortest notice.
« * «
Nothing at all was proved in the course of
all this discussion. About the most terrify-
ing collection of samples was produced by a
Freetrade member who stated that he had
procured the dreadful evidence of crime from
a firm of Melbourne importers. No absolute
proof was advanced that they were really of
Victorian manufacture, save the statement
of a biassed politician that a firm with
a special interest in destroying the local in-
dustry had told him so ; and there was no
re: 1 evidence of the alleged adulteration save
that the same member had been informed of
it by the same unreliable authority. Another
excited politician came down with another
awful example in the shape of a garment
which, he asserted, he had found marked
“all wool” in a warehouse. The fact that
he had found it there, that it was a local
production, and that it was so labelled, all
rested on his own bald assertion, and the
House was too badly excited to notice that
there was no positive evidence of its quality,
and that, even if everything else was granted,
it was the dishonest retailer who was directly
responsible for the label upon it, and no one
had even attempted to prove that the manu-
facturer sold it for anything save what it
really was, whatever that might be. Hardly
any of the samples were supported by any
authentic evidence as to their origin, or
their real composition, and the proceedings
were a confused howl rather than an inquiry.
# # •
But despite this want of reliable evidence
to begin with, it may safely be assumed that
many of the Victorian woollen products
commonly so-called, are largely shoddy. It
may be so assumed—-firstly, because the fact
has been known for many years, and,
secondly, because they couldn’t be otherwise
under the circumstances. An enormous pro-
portion of the goods imported from England
and elsewhere are shoddy of the worst des-
cription. There are among them blankets
made of 90 per cent, cotton and one-tenth
wool, alleged tweeds of the same composi-
tion, alleged flannels that are 70 per cent,
vegetable fibre, and other deceptions. These
are exceedingly cheap because they are made
by people who work long hours for low wages,
and because the cost of the raw materials is
small ; and they are permitted to be sold in
Victoria, as pure woollen goods, by any
fraudulent importers and dealers who feel so
inclined. Other cheap varieties, however,
are sold without any false pretence at all.
There is a demand for many different grades
of material at different prices, and many of
these materia’s are not pure wool, for the
simple reason that they were never in-
tended to be. If the Victorian mills deal in
wool only, then the supply of nearly a
dozen different varieties of flannels alone
must be left entirely to the importers. The
importers first created the demand, and the
demand being there the local manufacturers
naturally endeavor to supply it. To assume
that they don’t is absurd, and to profess
that they shouldn’t do it is simply to
profess that a woollen manufacturer should
be- something too pure and spotless fur this
earth. Also, the aspect of righteous in-
dignation assumed by the little knot of
Freetraders was altogether misplaced ; there
would be just as much reason for horror at
the discovery that a Victorian furniture-
maker supplied ugly and umesthetic-looking
pine tables to meet the demand for cheap
and nasty furniture, instead of turning
out only high-class goods in walnut and
mahogany.
* « •
It may also be assumed that some of the
shoddy which is produced by the Victorian
mills is sold by the dishonest wholesale and
retail dealer as pure material. Considering
that the same wholesale and retail dealers
notoriously sell much imported shoddy for
what it is not, they are hardly likely to do
otherwise with the native material. And
considering that the importers of Melbourne,
like those of other cities, consist largely of
dealers in bogus goods, persons who have
been convicted of notorious frauds against
the Customs, ex-directors of fraudulent
boom-banks, and people who have com-
pounded with their creditors for one fink
in the £ —in other words, thac their standard
of commercial morality is much the same as
that of other people—the alleged samples of
local shoddy which they furnished to various
Foreign-trade M.’sP. are just as unreliable
evidence as the testimony of any other in-
terested parties of doubtful character. Also,
the little party of Foreign-trade members on
whose statements the furore mostly rested
are probably just as reliable as other poli-
ticians—whatever that may amount to.
And, furthermore, the Victorian woollen
manufacturers have probably just about as
much honesty as other manufacturers—a
doubtful quantity at the best. But the mere
fact that they produce shoddy amounts to
absolutely nothing, unless it can be proved
that their inferior goods are sold under false
pretences, and that they, and not the
dealers, are responsible for these false pre-
tences. Neither of these points was proved
in any legal sense.
# m •
The attitude taken up by the Murray
Smith party and the Argus was practically
this : (1) It is not a fraud, nor even a matter
to be mentioned with regret, that the worst
description of shoddy should be made in
England under a Freetrade system, and
imported into Australia, nor does that
trade call for repression, or even inquiry.
(2) It is, however, an offence of the worst
description that a somewhat less objection-
able class of goods should be manufactured
in Victoria under a protective policy. (3)
The fact that there is a demand for certain
goods which are invariably made of a mixture
of wool and cotton counts for nothing ;
for some inscrutable reason these goods
must not be made in a Protectionist
country, and this branch of trade must
be supplied by importers only. And (4)
even the fact that no proof w is advanced
of any fraud on the part of the manufacturers
themselves makes no difference. The busi-
ness of a protected manufacturer, according
to the bright lexicon of the Murray Smith
party, is to pay his workpeople nearly twice
the wages paid in England ; to work them
much shorter hours ; to sell pure woollen
goods at prices as low as those charged for
imported articles that are 80 or 90 per cent,
cotton ; and to be denounced as a curse to
the community if he does these things, and
as a commercial swindler if he dues not.
* • •
Possibly if anybody had been sufficiently
calm to search forany real evidence against the
said manufacturer it would have been proved
that he —or a considerable part of him at all
events —is a commercial swindler. Protec-
tion doesn’t even profess to make men honest
any more than does Freetrade or revenue-
tariffism, and no one in his senses ever
dreamt that it would do so. If the protected
manufacturer finds a favorable opportunity
to swindle the public, and the swindle looks
sufficiently profitable, he is as likely to avail
himself of it as any other man. If it is to
his interest to make his alleged woollen goods
wholly out of cotton, and there is rio law- to
send him to gaol for so doing, and there is
any reasonable chance to escape detection, he
will most probably do it. But, even if he was
proved to have done so, it is not in any
sense an argument against Protection, and it
no more shows that that fiscal system should
be abolished . than the many recent bank
frauds show that ban king should be abolished,
or than the Customs frauds of which so many
importers have been convicted show that
importing should be wholly prohibited. If
the fact that a manufacturer who exists in a
protected country is dishonest in his dealings
is a reason why Protection should be done
away with, then the fact that a banker is dis-
honest in a Freetrade community is an
equally convincing proof that Freetrade
should be put down at once and for ever.
Also, the fact that anybody defrauds any-
body else in a country where revenue is
raised by a land-tax is a similarly re-
liable evidence that the land-tax re-
quires to be wiped out. Meanwhile
Premier Turner has been so much im-
pressed by the recent “ revelations ” that a
bill is shortly to be introduced prohibiting
the sale of shoddy unless it is distinctly
marked and branded as such. This measure
will be wholly admirable if it deals in abso-
lutely equal measure with the importer and
the manufacturer—if it scoops in the person
who sells high-class Victorian goods as
English, and low-grade foreign goods as
Victorian, or vice versa —and if it is so framed
as to exercise an equally careful supervision
in every case. But a great effort will be
made to apply its restrictions principally, if
not wholly, to the local factories, and any
such attempt needs to be closely watched
and promptly suppressed. It is not the dis-
honest shoddy ot local manufacture which
wants to be extinguished, but dishonest
shoddy in general.
The Ethics of Oscar.
The Bulletin has received many letters from
intelligent persons who deny the right of the
community to gaol Oscar Wilde for an offence
which, in order to “depolarise ” it for purposes
of debate, may be called galvanised-iron. The
argument may be shortened by excluding from
consideration whether brilliant abnormality (of
the Wilde type) stands on a higher footing
than dull normality.; whether a community,
some of whose members commit unhindered
possibly worse offences than galvanised-iron, is
justified in “punishing” galvanised-iron dealers;
and whether, supposing the community’s action
justified, the best or fairest treatment for gal-
vanised-iron dealers is per gaol, or per asylum,
or per some other means of community-protec-
tion or individual-reformation. The questions
for settlement are, therefore: Is galvanised-
iron an anti-social act ? Has the community an
ethical right to deal as it pleases with those who
commit anti-social actions ?
« * *
To the first question, the obvious answer is
that the social character of any act must, in
the last resort, be determined by the com-
munity ; and, as galvanised-iron has been
determined to be an anti-social act, to all com-
munity-intents and purposes it is an anti-social
act. But, going deeper, the community’s
decision is justified. Could it be proved that
the results of galvanised-iron were confined to
Wilde himself and his accomplices, and sup-
posing these accomplices to be of sufficiently
mature age to warrant the community in
leaving them to take the responsibility of their
acts, it might be held that the community is
wrong. But no such proof was given ; and the
weight of presumption is all the other way.
Individual experience justifies the belief that
in such cases precept and example have a strong
influence; historical experience justifies the
belief that galvanised-iron is a manifestation of
the degeneracy which saps the life of a nation.
In other words, the effect of galvanised-iron
could not practically be confined to Wilde and
his responsible assistants—if they were all
responsible; and galvanised-iron ' has a per-
nicious influence on the community. An in-
duction of the facts on which this conclusion is
based cannot here be made; but probably few
students will disagree,
# # *
The second question will bear more extended
argument. It is premised that no notice need
be taken of the “ morality ” or “ immorality,”
in the cant sense, of galvanised-iron. There,is‘
no absolute standard of morality. Says a
recent writer: Probably “there is-no crime,
recognised by us as such, which has hot some-
where and at some time .found its plane in the
catalogue of virtues, and no virtue which has
not been officially condemned. The,murderous
Fijian’s only fear is lest he should not be active
enough in slaughter to win the approbation of
his gods ; with the Egyptian, lying is honor-
able ; while the Turkoman’s code of moral
injunctions prescribes theft. Polygamy, wrong
in Europe and America, is right and proper in
China, India, and Turkey ; while infanticide, a
practice that we hold in utter abhorrence, was
not only common in Greece and Rome, but was
even justified by the greatest ethical teachers
of antiquity, Plato and Aristotle, who also
held views concerning the relations of the sexes-
which we should look on as revolting.”
• • *
What is moral and immoral is practically
fixed for every man by the majority-sentiment
of the community into which he happens to be
born. Ned Kelly, for example, was hung for
conduct which would have won him esteem
from the freebooters of William the Norman.
At any time, for any individual, the measure of
rectitude or turpitude in his actions is practi-
cally the measure of the majority which ap-
proves or condemns them. In other words,
might is right—practically.
• • »
But might is not right ethically, absolutely.
A thing is not essentially wrong because at any
given time a majority of people in Australia,
or America, or Europe, believe it to be wrong.
For we have only to change place and time to
be nearly sure of finding a country where a
majority of people deem that thing right and
lawful. That is to say, a majority-opinion is
no absolute test of ethical quality. The morality
of any given majority is doubtless moral for the
individuals who compose that majority, but it
is immoral for the minority, and it may be
immoral for the majority in the same country
at another time, or in another country at the
same time.
* * *
The only rational standard of ethics is well-
being. What helps or benefits or pleases the
individual is right for him : what injures or dis-
pleases him is wrong. And to every individual
must be conceded the right to know what he
wishes or does not wish. Similarly, what helps
the community is right for the community :
what hurts it is wrong. And to the community
or its majority must be conceded the right to
know what it wishes or does not wish.
* * #
Ethically, every man may be considered from
two points of view : first, in relation to himself;
second, in relation to others. As an individual,
he has the right to act as he pleases ; though as
a member of society, if the society is to exist,
he can be permitted to act as he pleases only so
long as his actions are not prejudicial to the
society. The quality of morality or immorality,
right or wrong, cannot inhere in any act of the
individual considered as an isolated integer.
Morality is a matter of relationships to time,
place, and circumstances ; and the individual
per se has no relationships. The Bible says
that with Adam came sin into the world : it
would be more correct to say that sin came in
with the community. The first man could not
possibly commit a crime—even against himself;
since his own will and pleasure was the sole,
standard of right and wrong which existed..
The last man will be in similar case—the only
measure of the morality of his acts will be his,;
power to act; and, as he cannot act where he- :
is impotent, it follows that, in kingly phrase,
he “ can do no wrong.”
# # •
Thus, considering the individual as a member •
of society, he is immoral only when he is anti-
social. From the individual’s point of view, he ■
need not be immoral even then, and ethically
there is no certainty that he is. Practically,.
however, the force of the majority leaves the;
individual no room for debate ; and it is true ■
that, if the individual has the right to defend I
himself against the community, the community
has at least as much right to defend itself
against the individual, and, if necessary, to-
attack the individual. The sole justification)
which it needs is proof that the individual is-j
doing something dangerous to its well-being..
• « »
Thus the answer to the second question is
also affirmative. Summed up: There is no
fixed cognisable standard of absolute right.
The best cognisable standard is that of well-
being. Every individual or community has the
right to fix his or its own standard of well-
being. When the standard of the individual
conflicts with that of the pommunity, the pre-
sumption is that the community is at least as
ethically right as the individual. Therefore,
the community has just as much ethical right
to enforce its standard as the individual has to
enforce his. And as, practically, the com-
munity is stronger than the individual, when
there is conflict the individual generally goes
under. Oscar Wilde has gone under.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Kanakaland Contest.
Up North, the Boodlewraith party is working
for the coming elections with the vigor and
ingenuity of the Devil himself. The contest
cannot be deferred beyond the beginning of
’96 : it is more likely to come at the end of this
year. Whenever Boodlewraith feels fittest,
and judges its opponents most unfit, the poll
will be sprung upon them. During the past,
nine months the rolls have been steadily “ bul-
cocked ”--f.e., the electors Rave been canvassed
one by one, the tendencies and antecedents of
each noted and registered, and every possible
dodge,' legal and illegal, has been tried to dis-
■ fraupiuS'c known or suspected opponents of the
'The work of enrolling “good men,”"
witliVor without qualification, has continuously
gone forward; and the “bad men” have
found officials strangely forgetful or strongly
hindering when they pressed their claims,
for a vote. ..The Govt, is run as a party-
electoral organisation, and “the authori-
ties ” preside over the property league
which strives; by hook or crook, to dis-
franchise every laboring elector. Democrats
qualified by 20 years’ residence in the same
spot have received official demands to make
oath anew to their qualifications—for no ap-
parent reason but the whim of an electoral
registrar, behind whom the National Ass is.
ambushed. Fat-men enrolled in the Fat alliance -


have suddenly discharged employes who, finding
no work handy, have had to tramp ; while their
places have been filled by men from another part
of the province. Thus two sets of possible anti-
boodle voters are disfranchised for six months,
until a residence qualification is again acquired.
Every labour-elector who can be “ moved on ”
is being “ moved on ” —to lose his vote ; while
those who can’t be “ moved on ” are mys-
teriously “moved off”—off the electoral-roll.
This policy has already been so successful that
Boodlewraith has high hopes of returning to
office. It is simply a question of the number
of honest men in the province ; and the Govern-
ment believes that a majority are either
naturally dishonest or can be bribed or coerced
into dishonesty. The democrats have only one
chance —a coalition between the Labour-party
and the Opposition. As long as the Labour-
party persists in its policy of extremes it will
achieve nothing. When it drops theory for
practical utility, when it is content to take
what it can get instead of waiting for what it
wants, it should succeed—as the Labour-party
has succeeded in Maoriland. “ Socialism — i.e.,
Collectivism—in our time ” is a chimera. In
pursuing it the Q. Labour-party sacrifices, and
will continue to sacrifice, substance for shadow.
The N.S.W. Fiscal Issue.
The present fiscal issue in New South Wales
can be put in a few w'ords, somewhat as
follows : The province had nearly a century of
Freetrade. The Freetrade epoch covered the
great land-sale period when all the eastern
districts were disposed of. It covered the
great borrowing period, when the loan-money
was rolling in at the rate of from .£1,000,000 to
£5,800,000 per annum, and the date of repay-
ment was still far off. It included the great
gold era, when the annual output was occasion-
ally over £2,000,000, instead of about one-
quarter of that amount as at present. And it
extended over the silver-boom days ; and the
era of private borrowing, when foreign capital
was still coming in fast, and the interest
thereon had not become so great a burden
as it now is ; and the era of high prices, when
the province’s exports of raw material fetched
in London from 20 to 60 per cent, more
than they now do. Then the province had
three years of partial Protection. The first,
1892, was the year when the first large
loan fell due and when the London financiers
flatly refused to lend, for about the first time in
Australia’s history. The second, 1893, was the
year of the great financial collapse. And all
three were the years when Australian exports
realised all round the lowest prices in London
ever recorded. Also it was in this period that
Japan began to annex the foreign coal-trade of
Newcastle, and Victoria began to be indepen-
dent of New South Wales supplies, and the
great Broken Hill strike took place. And
the case against Protection is that it did
not produce much better results in these direful
years, and after only 36 months’ trial, than the
Foreign-trade policy produced after it had
existed for a century, through all the halcyon
days of the province’s history. This is the
New South Wales fiscal question put in the
very smallest space up to date.

Some Dreary Statistics.
Sydney Telegraph has invented a new and
very inaccurate correspondent, Mr. J. A.
Stanley Adam, evidently an Englishman, and
apparently a new-chum, who writes a long
letter almost every day on the horrors of Pro-
tection in New South Wales. The following is
a sample :
How do the number of manufactories in existence and
the number of hands employed in 1894 compare with the
figures for the years prior to the imposition of the Dibbs
tariff? According to the Government statist, there were
2647 works in existence in New South Wales last year,
against 2583 in 1891, and 3541 in 1887. As compared with
1891 there was an increase of 64 works of one kind or
another; the total number of hands employed in these
factories was 42,751 in 1894, against 46,135 in 1891, a drop
of 3384. But for the rapid development of the boiling-
down, tanning, and wool-scouring industries, which
owe nothing to the tariff, the drop would have
been much greater. In 1891 there were only 273
hands engaged in the boiling - down establishments
against 1715 in 1894 ; 897 were engaged in the tan-
neries in 1891, against 1130 last year ; and 842 in wool-
scouring, against 1246. The total number of hands
engaged in the boot factories last year was 3420, as com-
pared with 2806 in 1891, an increase of 614. Curiously
enough, however, the smaller number of hands turned
out more pairs of boots in 1891, under Freetrade, than the
larger number did in 1894, under 10 per cent. Protection.
The output in the former year was 2,634,254 pairs, and in
the latter 2,611,700. As regards hats and caps—protected
by a 10 per cent, duty—there were only 10 hands engaged
in the manufacturing industry last year, against 74 in
1891. Ever since the duty was imposed the number of
hands employed in this industry has fallen year by year,
the numbers being—lß9l, 74; 1892,26 ; 1893, 14 ; and 1894,
10. As to the ready-made clothing trade—also protected by
10 per cent—Mr. Coghlan shows that while it afforded
work to 2698 persons in 1891, it could only maintain 1466
in 1894. In 1891, the biscuit factories employed 504
hands ; last year—with a duty of 2d. per lb.—the number
fas 479. In 1890 there were 497 hands employed in
making confectionery—duty, 2d. per lb.—and 359 in 1891;
last year the figures were 380. The wire-workers in 1891
totalled 170, and 169 in 1894. Furniture-making gave
■ work to 1064 people in 1891, and to 716 last year. Coach-
I builders represented 1486 workers in 1891, but only 1224
;in 1894. There were 483 hands employed in making
, saddlery and harness in 1891, and 324 in 1894.
Figures don’t lie, but a great deal depends on
how they are exhibited. The following is
.practically the same batch of figures the other
- way up : In 1885 there were 3541 “ factories or
-works” in New South Wales, and by 1891 the
inumber had decreased to 2583, nearly 1000
\works having closed up in six years of Free-
'trade. In 1894, after three years of Protection,
they had increased to 2647, or an advance of
64. There has been, since 1891, a decrease of
3384 in the number of employes in factories,
but the statement that the enormously in-
creased employment in tanneries “ owes
nothing to the tariff” is unabashed lying. The
business in this line remained almost stationary
for many years, until the 10 per cent, duty was
imposed in 1891, since which date the imports
of leather have decreased by £32,000 and the
exports have increased by £90,000. Also, the
boom in the boiling-down industry, which em-
ployed 273 hands in 1891 and 1715 in 1894, is
partly owing to the improved local market
for hides created by the protective tariff. The
decrease of 3384 in the number of employes is
chiefly accounted for by the fact that 3216 less
people are employed in six industries which
were unaffected by the tariff changes of 1891,
brickmakers, joiners, plasterers, and railway
workshop employes accounting for most of the
decline. This collapse in the freetrade indus-
tries of the province is significant. The number
of employes in the hat and cap trade fell, as
stated, by 64 in three years of Protection ; it
also fell by 67 in two previous years of Free-
trade—in fact, it has fallen continuously since
1885. Probably it wasn’t sufficiently pro-
tected. As Mr. Stanley Adamsliow's, the confec-
tionery industry has improved materially since
1891, though it has very little to do with the
case, the duty being an old one of many years
standing. In 10 important industries which
were protected by the ’9l tariff', employment
has increased very largely. The first year of
Protection showed an increase of 700 em-
ployes; the third (1894) showed an in-
crease of nearly 4000, the largest ever
recorded in New South Wales. In between
there was an awful drop of 8000 during the
bank-smash year of ’93, but Protection is no
more responsible for that tremendous laud-boom
collapse than Freetrade is for Jabez Balfour’s
bank. Yet, despite this unforeseen disaster,
the protective period compares very well, on
the whole, with the Foreign-trade period which
went before it. Out of the 45 New South
Wales industries enumerated by Coghlan,
there was, during the Foreign-trade period
1885-91, a decrease of 5890 employes in 25
separate industries, and nine others had a total
increase of 37 hands among them. The only
increases of importance were in Government
employes, gas - works employes, builders,
engineers and printers, who are not exposed
to foreign competition, but in almost every
competitive industry there was decay and a
general fading away.
An Accusing Voice.
The following letter from Mr. Arthur Grif-
fith, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), speaks for itself; —
Parliament House, Sydney, 12th July, 1895.
Editor Bulletin,—l! you see fit to use all your influ-
ence, at the present juncture, to injure the cause of Land
taxation and the Referendum, by continually throwing'
mud at the men who are fisfhtinjt for these measures, that
is your own business. But even this liberty must be sub-
ject to some sort of limitation, and, when in your anxiety
to besmirch some political party you pass into the region
of deliberate mis-statement, it is time for protest.
I am referring now to your statement that Crick’s Bill
to place Kanakas, Japanese, etc., under the same restric-
tions as the Chinamen, was oast out by 38 votes to 20
the majority being composed of Ministerialists and a
portion of the Labor-pirty. This statement is a lie. It
is not even a cleverly constructed falsehood, but a dull,
ponderous, stupid lie, worthy of the Star, and not at all
up to Bulletin standard !
Of course you know perfectly well that Crick’s Bill was
never Before the House—that even the resolution asking
permission to introduce it was not reached on the business
paper, and that the 38 to 20 division was on a mere catch-
penny motion of adjournment asking the House to throw
out the Govermnentfor simply compelling Mr. Crick tO'
place his Bill among those of other private members.
I have not the least hope that you will publish this
letter, but I do expect that you will at least have the
grace to withdraw your mis-statements in this matter.
1 am, &c., Arthur Griffith.
In reply to which The Bulletin distinctly re-
fuses to withdraw anything, and denies Mr.
Griffith’s assertions from first to last. The
situation is as follows: In October, ’94, the
New South Wales Assembly declared by a solid
majority that steps should be taken to exclude
colored labor. The Government has ignored
the instruction up to date, despite alarming re-
ports about the nigger influx in the Northern
districts. Under these circumstances, Crick,
M.L.A., submitted a bill in three clauses to
put the black alien under the same restrictions
that have worked so well in the case of the
Chinaman, which measure could, with Govern-
ment assistance, have been passed through tlm
Assembly in a few hours. It was blocked for
w’ant of that assistance, and when the dissolu-
tion was announced, and all chance of
carrying it this session without Ministerial aid
was over, Crick brought his measure before
the House in the only way which the rules and
regulations permitted. There was no question
about throwing out the Ministry ; the Reid
Cabinet has been defeated over and over again,
notably over the Local Government Bill, and
hasn’t gone out one inch. The Bill might not
be formally before the House, but it was
there informally—as much there as circum-
stances would allow. In reply to Crick,
Premier Reid denounced his action as a “ dirty
political move,” and refused to assist his
measure in any way, and yet gave no promise
that the Government would take the matter up
within any reasonable period, and, at the divi-
sion, he was supported by a strong Freetrade
and Labor majority, which practically voted
for the continuance and extension of black
labor so long as it suited Reid’,s convenience.
Under these circumstances, The Bulletin
declines to retract anything, despite Mr.
Griffith’s truculent attitude in the matter.
The rest of that gentleman’s accusation is that
this paper refused to tell glozing lies, or to
meanly suppress the truth, about Reid’s ruinous
fiscal policy, and his shameful manipulation of
the finances, and his Loan Bill, and other
matters, because he advocates a land-tax—in
other words, that because he promises a good
thing in the remote future, it should approve of
all the bad things he is doing in the immediate
present, and encourage him to keep on doing
them. A year or two ago another politician
remonstrated in the same strain, his plea being
that, for the sake of Protection, this paper
should say nothing about Dibbs’s title, and the
Broken Hill trials, and Lyne’s scheme for sell-
ing the State railways, and various other
matters. The Bulletin hasn’t got sufficiently
low down to oblige either of these gentlemen,,
and it declines to buy Mr. Griffith’s approval
on such terms. Moreover, as that gentleman
twice voted on the Local Government Bill
against the Reid Ministry, thereby endangering
the land and income tax, the referendum, local
government, and the Ministry itself, he is the
very last windy person who has a right to strike
ridiculous attitudes, because The Bulletin
does much less than he did and for a much
better reason.
The Insensible Abrahams.
Six years ago the Vic. Full Court quashed the
conviction of one Fitzgerald, a perjurer, upon
the ground that the Judge, whilst absent from
tbe court, had communicated with the jury on
a matter material to one of the issues being
tried. The jury seat a message asking advice,
and his Honor’s answer repeated what he had
previously said in open court, but inasmuch as
this communication didn’t pass in the presence
of the prisoner and his counsel it was held to
be fatal to the old trial, and a new one was
ordered. Justice Higinbotham dissented from
the finding of four colleagues (Justice Williams
among them) on this law* point, but one dis-
sentient in a Full Court is the least that can be
expected. Five Judges are seldom unanimous.
The decision of the large majority in this case
was unobjectionable from all points of view. A
Judge ought not to direct any portion of a trial
from somewhere round the corner, and nobody
should be away from court at a moment when his
absence would provide grounds for one of those
hollow objections by which justice is delayed
or upset. Clearly, there was a lesson sticking
out from the case of the accused Fitzgerald,
in April, 1889. It showed to the Law the
necessity for laying down a hard and fast rule
which would prevent the possibility of a similar
accident occurring again—a rule ordering the
clerk, or the Judge’s associate, or the crier, or
any other minor official, to see that everybody
who should be in court was actually there at
given times. But since such a rule would close
one little avenue of approach to new trials and
more costs it wasn’t demanded by the lawyers,
neither did the public demand it, because they
are, generally speaking, appalled by legal
fiddlededee and incapable of thinking for them-
selves. So the case of Fitzgerald was adopted
as a convenient precedent, and stowed away in
the dark recesses of legal crookedness until
somebody might want to use it to quash another
conviction. The long-waited-for opportunity
came last week, when four brothers Abrahams
were convicted of conspiracy to swindle the
gun-buyers of Melbourne. The Abrahams
quartette took to fainting and being carried out
of Court so often that at last one of them
wasn’t present when something “ material to
the issue ” occurred in Court. Therefore,
counsel Gaunson, on behalf of another Abra-
hams, bided his time for mentioning that the
trial had gone bung. The presiding Judge—
the same Justice Williams of 1889—feebly
remarked that his attention ought to have been
called to the absence of an Abrahams when
the trial was in danger of degenerating into a
farce, although he had only the Law to blame
for not having appointed somebody to that.
duty. An appeal to the Full Court against the
Abrahams’ conviction seems bound to be up-
held. Judge Williams helped to hatch the
chicken of precedent, and the ridiculous bird is
just now roosting on his wig.
A Comprehensive Flogging Clause.
The flogging clause proposed by Cooke,
M.L.C., to be added to the Victorian Gaols Act
Amendment Bill, provides that the visiting
justices may sentence “ any male prisoner,
aged 16 or upwards, convicted of an assault
upon or attempt to do any bodily injury to any
officer of any gaol ” to be “once or twice pri-
vately whipped.” Each whipping is to consist
of not more than 25 lashes. The whole con-
cludes with this very comprehensive and sug-
gestive clause :
For the purposes of this section the word officer shall
include and refer to the Inspector-General and any deputy
or assistant Inspector-General, and even' governor,
gaoler, keeper or officer in charge of any gaol or hulk,
and every medical officer, overseer, warder, storekeeper,
matron, nurse or person whether permanently, tempor-
arily or occasionally employed in or about any gaol or
hulk, and any member of the police force, and any person
engaged in the conveyance of any persons or goods to or
from any gaol or hulk, and any justice visiting any gaol
or hulk.
If this unspeakable amendment becomes law,.
there is hardly a breach of prison discipline
that cannot be brought within its octopus
grip. The excited prisoner on his way
from the court to the gaol may shake his
fist (legally an assault) at a policeman, and
thereupon find himself liable t 0.50 lashes ! The
semi-delirious gaol-hospital patient may attack
his nurse, and thereupon find himself liable to
50 lashes. The careless prisoner shovelling
stones into a dray may accidentally land a
shovelful upon the head of the ungaoled driver,
and thereupon find himself liable to 50 lashes.
The unlucky wretch cleaning up on a balcony
above a corridor may inadvertently drop a floor-
cloth on a supercilious visiting-official’s head,
and thereupon find himself liable to 50 lashes.
These suggested possibilities are not far-fetched,
for it must be remembered that the prisoner is
powerless against his keepers. They only have
to get him “ set,” and then, upon offering the
least pretence, he is in danger of all the vials of
■official vengeance. If the “ cat ” is of the vials,
then he is in danger of that. The justices
generally do exactly what the officials require.
Prison punishments should be rigidly defined
and confined to the narrowest limits. If any-
thing beyond them is considered necessary the
alleged offender should be taken into the open
courts of justice.
Federation; A Protest.
Dear Bulletin, —What’s the use of continually
whipping the moribund horse—Federation ?
Outside a few professional mouthers, a sec-
tion of politicians and press which sees
in the subject material for heroic plati-
tudes, or a more crafty section which
finds it an opportune red-herring, and The
Bulletin, the people of New South Wales do
not care a rap about Federation —at pre-
sent. The bush folks, I know, do not, and the
city feeling on the recent border railway-rates
fiasco is one more evidence of how Sydney
commercialism views the matter. You know
and have set forth some of the material diffi-
culties, amongst which the financial adjustment
looms large, but I think you scarcely realise
the strong provincial jealousy in the Australian
character which will not be fully manifested
till the practical details are discussed, or
till Federation is consummated and the
strain of new conditions is felt. As you
will probably admit, an immediate Federation
could only be a partial one, and a partial
Federation would be peculiarly liable to dis-
ruption, and how difficult would it be to again
federate after a re-action ? The motive circum-
stances surrounding the only Federation to
which ours has any analog3 r —the American
yUniou—are entirely wanting. There is no per-
sonality now to the fore which can appeal to
all the Australias and surmount the tremendous
difficulties which confront union, and not till
we have an ideal Democracy with ideal machi-
nery can such a leader be dispensed \tith. Fot
an occasion like this, one commanding pet-
sonality is more forceful than a score of medio-
crities. There is no outside pressure —no “ us
against the world ” to raise the white-heat
of patriotism which would annihilate our
petty, but deep-rooted, provincial prejudices.
No menace to rouse the fundamental in-
stinct of self-preservation ! The great factor
in the Canadian Federation is also lacking—we
have no America alongside. We know that
Federation must come. That it is a desirable
consummation we can agree, hut —is it desirable
in the present state of Australian feeling, and
is it safe with our present class of politicians
and “ leaders ? ” Note the ennobbled crowd who
are pulling the strings, and think of the
MTlwraith and Republican Dibbs, and ■
answer ! With such a gang in power —and
they would be, for though the constituencies
be altered the constituents are the same—we
should have an era of Imperial jingoism and
militarism. With subservient Ministries of
Canadian and Australian Federations, and
Cecil Rhodes as Emperor of South Africa, the
Imperial wire-pullers would, with tiie arts so
successful in the past with the same men, con-
summate that Imperial Federation which is to
bulldoze the colonies into shoring up the decay-
ing greatness of England. Had we not better
“ mark time ” till we have a prospect of getting
the men who will voice the growing Australian
sentiment: “Let the dead bury their dead.
What is that to us !” Surely you are, for once,
a little out of touch in advocating so persistently
an immediate Federation. Missionise by all
means, educate us for the time when we can
“ take occasion by the hand”—but don’t assist
the “shelving” politicians in delaying those
many useful little things which are so momen-
tous in the aggregate and which we have some
immediate prospect of getting. Dangle the
carrots occasionally, but let the ass get a few
substantial thistles meantime. — Treble B.
The Disastrous Land-Tax.
The Australian Star publishes a lank and
foolish article whose burden is that in Maori-
land the cultivated area is diminishing, the
State revenue is declining, the population is
growing less, and the yield of agricultural pro-
duce is falling off, and that all these evils arise
through the land-tax. It, therefore, beseeches
New South Wales to be warned in time and
keep clear of land-tax and land-taxers. The
alleged decline of revenue is based on one
quarter’s returns, and the loss of population
rests on the fact that (apart from the rush to
Coolgardie) in one month —the month when the
regular exodus of shearers to Australia is on—
the departures were more numerous than the
arrivals. This method of using statistics re-
quires very little intelligence. It simply con-
sists in stating that there is, in some particular
country, a law or system, or an absence of one
of them, that you object to, and also that the
same country has a period when, in some re-
spect or other, it is less prosperous than it was
at some other period. Then it is only neces-
sary to shalre the two circumstances up
together. The cause ascribed may be Free-
trade, Protection, land-tax, want of a State
Church, “ godless education,” labor legislation
—anything, in fact; and as no country is always
more prosperous in every respect this year than
it was every previous year, or this day than
it was the day before, the same argument
always fits. If the Star can show that the
population of Maorilaud and its revenue and its
cultivated area have steadily declined for a
series of years since the land-tax was imposed,
or even that they have increased at a much less
rate than they did before, and that the down-
ward movement and the land - tax began
together, then it would go a long way towards
proving its case. But the mere fact that Maori-
land is apparently a shade less prosperous in
one year under the land-tax than it was in
another year under the same system, counts for
nothing, so long as the fact remains that the
province is richer in almost every respect than
it was in the pre-lamhtax period. No political
system ever invented will make fluctuations
absolutely impossible, and nobody save a Para-
guayan emigrant expects it to. Maoriland’s
present “ disastrous ” land-tax dates from 1891.
Since then the population has increased largely,
whereas there was a heavy decrease in all the
years from ’B6 to ’9l, and the Star’s alleged
falling off is a garbled affair which is arrived at
by taking one month only and tacitly assuming
that it is a fair sample of the whole year. Its
exports have reached their highest record in the
land-tax period ; its savings bank deposits
have grown to be the largest, per head of
population, in Australasia, the land-tax pro-
vince of S.A. alone excepted ; and its total
yield of agricultural and pastoral pro-
duce, in proportion to population, is now
also the largest with the exception of
Queensland only. If the present situa-
tion of Maoriland is owing to the land-tax.
as the Star alleges, then it presents a most
powerful argument in favor of that policy.
Unfortunately the old political hacks of the
Dibbs party have taken up an attitude of hos-
tility to the land and income tax simply because
it is an axiom of the degraded science of party
politics that they should be “ agin the Govern-
ment ” in all things. The fact that the present
N.S. W. Freetrade-party alsofoughtagainst these
two measures when it was in Opposition and the
Dibbs crowd’s political hacks advocated the
land-tax from the Ministerial benches, only
aggravates the immorality of the proceeding.
And the Star, which follows every change of
its owners’ policy, grinds out its tune to order,
just as it would grind out any other tune if so
instructed.

published. Nevertheless, there remains the
objectionable fact that the Age proprietor, who
has been virtually dictator of the vie. railway
policy, and who brought about the dismissal of
Speight and Allison Smith, did take a fourth
share in a certain patent truck built in
the hope that it might be adopted by

faith in their patent, rushed Mr. Syme into the

department invites a certain amount of suspicion

The Maoriland Government is confronted by
the fact that it must raise money somehow to
complete the disastrous Midland railway. It
was compelled to take over the line because,
under the imbecile agreement of the Stout-
Vogel Ministry, ail the public lands in Canter-
bury, Nelson and Westland were locked up
against settlement until the Midland Co. had
selected its giants out of them, and the grants
couldn’t be selected till the line was completed,
and the co. flatly refused even to make a pre-
tence of going on with it. The want of land
for settlement drove the Ministry into shutting
down on the co., and, having done so,
the white elephant must be finished, if
only to give it a chance of earning a
part of its keep. Now the proposal to
go on with the work has aroused a wild demand
for the completion of the long-neglected Otago
Central and North Island Trunk lines, and the
present signs are that Maoriland will ultimately
proceed to execute all the three, and the bor-
rowing policy will recommence in earnest.
The ghost of Vogel is abroad, and the
speculations that he left behind him keep his
memory green. And now, if the province
starts on another career of railway expansion,
it has to consider whether it can raise the
money by more taxation, or by the issue
of a State paper currency, or by local bor-
rowing, or by tbe old policy of applying
to the disastrous British capitalist. Probably
it could be done by a combination of the first
three devices, if Treasurer Ward is the financial
genius which he imagines himself to be. Bub
though Australasia has had many financial
geniuses they were, unluckily, all of one pat-
tern ; and Ward, it is to be feared, is of one
pattern also. For a Treasurer who will dis-
pense with foreign capital and who can make
something out of nothing locally, Maoriland
now offers about the finest opening on record.
There is no credit to be gained by finding
money when it can be got by paying interest
on it; the only kind of Treasurer who is of any
use in Australasia nowadays is the one who
can find money when it can’t be gob and get it
without interest.
From among the confusion of politics The
Bulletin’s dear old friend, “ Wiugen ” Abbott,
rises up once more to complain about State-
railway ownership. Says Abbott :
If the State had never made a mile of railway in New
South Wales private enterprise would before now have
supplied railways, where they were really required, with
freight charges at about half what they are now on our
State railways, so that producers would only have been
taxed to half the amount of the present freight tax.
Second, that New South Wales, in proportion to popula-
tion, has already more railways than America or any
other country, and more than the present population can
profitably use. And, third, that every railway system in
Australasia would have been insolvent and in the hands
of receivers 20 years ago if it were not for the system of
making up losses out of the general revenue of the
country, thus doubly taxing producers, and then concoct-
ing a balance-sheet which does not show in the capital
account of the railways the sums abstracted from general
revenue.
New South Wales, as it happens, has less rail-
way, in proportion to population, than Canada,
the United States, Victoria, Queensland, South
Australia, Westralia, Tasmania or Maoriland,
but Abbott can’t be, expected to know a little
thing like that. Poor Abbott, who seems to
have a personal grievance against the railways,
will probably go on in this dreadful fashion for
the rest of his life, and then he will take his
grievance away with him, and explain all about
the exorbitant freight charges of New South
Wales to the Watcher at the Golden Gate.
The man from Wingen’s burning mountain
couldn’t rest even in Paradise unless he had a
chance to read over these freight charges every
now and then to the archangels, and convince
them of the evils of State ownership. Mean-
while he never gets any further forward. Even
supposing he could prove that, if the railways
had been originally built by private enterprise,
they would have carried his goods for nothing,
he can’t get away from the fact that they
weren’t built that way, and as they can’t be built
that way now Abbott might as well write to
prove that, if George 111. had been born with
three legs, he would have had one leg more
than usual, or that, if he had never been born
at all, then he wouldn’t have existed. The
Bulletin now invites Mr. Abbott to write a
series of letters on the fact that, if there was no
such place as Australia, then we would most
likely all be somewhere else at the present
moment. The attention of the public has never
been properly drawn to this matter so far, and
a great many people have never even thought
of it.
This picture and that ! In the N.S.W. Dean
case (alleged attempt to murder) the Judge
re-called the jurymen (at 8.35 p.m. on Saturday
night) after they had been eight hours consider-
ing their verdict, and told them that if they
didn’t agree he would lock them up till Monday
piorning. That jury agreed in seven minutes.
In the Maoriland Dean case (baby-murder),
after witnesses and counsel had been heard, the
Judge told the jury, at the close of a Thursday
afternoon, that “he would defer his summing-
up till next morning, as he considered they had
had a sufficiently trying day.” Which attitude
is the fairer to a body of men entrusted with a
human life ? which the fairer to the prisoner ?
which the better for the attainment of justice ?

“Iii Town,” at Sydney Lyceum, is neither
better nor worse than described last week, but
The Bulletin’s opinion that little Fred Kaye, as
the aggressive and towzled call-boy, is unduly
eclipsed by the vast preponderance of Brad-
field, gains ground. The call-boy is a raucous,
cheeky brat with a great fund of hoarse
humor, and he is one of the few comedians
who is humorous when viewed from behind. In
fact the call-boy is even more humorous from be-
hind than he is in front, and the back aspect of
his pants is one of G. R. Sims’s ballads epitomised.
Also, he is remotely suggestive of Lawson’s poem
about how a man feels when his pants begin to go,
assuming that his pants go, not pathetically, but
in a rakish spirit of fog and gin. Kaye looks like
the one solid fact in a play that is otherwise
mostly gossamer and song and high kick, and this
paper waits anxiously to see him in a character
which will give him wider scope. And when it
does it will probably be disappointed. The Gaiety
kind of mummer is generally disappointing when
he gets too much scope and begins to have a mort-
gage on the centre of the stage.
* * * *
The Dacres are filling in their last week at
Her Majesty’s with “Man and Woman ” —a good
drama, which has been partially spoiled by the
authors’ anxiety to make it hold more characters
than there is standing-room for. They have
crowded it to such an extent that it is suggestive
of the Black Hole of Calcutta, and, in the effort
to make the dialogue go round all these people, no
single person has got enough to make him effec-
tive. Even Dacre, as the fraudulent cashier,
though he is allowed to weep considerably in the
middle of the stage, is a good deal obscured by
this rush of characters to the head, and Miss Amy
Roselle puts a vast amount of pathos an 1 ability
into a rather abbreviated part. There is not
much in “ Man and Woman,” save that the bank’s
trusted officer annexes the money for speculative
purposes, and loses it, and is then forgiven and
sacked by a fatuous-looking board, with a retreat-
ing chin. The fun is labored and disconnected,
and some of the sentimental passages are pain-
fully stupid. The Dacres do their best with the
materials on hand, and Messrs. Plimmer, Har-
court, Sweeney, and Inglis loom up, here and
there, to a considerable extent. Also, Miss Kate
Bishop makes a pleasant and pathetic mother to
the devastated cashier. The Bulletin, however,
feels an utter want of enthusiasm about “Man
and Woman,” which is built on the strictly con-
ventional theory that the man who speculates with
stolen money always loses it, and then, after a
period of adversity and repentance, during which
his girl sticks by him nobly, some grand old mer-
chant elevates him to a position of trust, and he
is last seen counting his benefactor’s cash with a
halo round his head and two wings sticking out
of his back. And, in these matters, this paper
suppresses its emotion with difficulty, and merely
remarks that Mr. Rignold will appear on Satur-
day as Frank Darlington, in that gorgeous spec-
tacle, “Youth.”

Hamilton’s Circus may be comprehensively
summed upas a show that is very like every other
circus, and contains everything which a well-
ordered circus could be expected to have. Almost
any kind of a circus can do a good business in this
horsey land, and as Hamilton’s is not any kind of
a circus by a very long way, it is doing a business
that is much above the average.

r emarkable. Rickards’ Co. at the Tivoli has been

** * *
“ Thoroughbred,” the new farcical comedy
at Melbourne Princess’, was intended to amuse
the patrons of Toole’s Theatre, London, “ where
it is now being played with immense success.”
Two distinct sets of Hinglish characters and one
casual Amurrikan gentleman provide the human
interest in “Thoroughbred,” whilst a race-horse
named Roast Chicken constitutes the plot. Roast
Chicken, although much talked about, doesn’t
appear during the evening, therefore the plot isn’t
visible. The members of the B. and B. com-
pany take their places on a drag, situated some-
where among the wilds of Ascot Heath, and cry
aloud that Roast Chicken is winning in a canter,
but if the noble animal were beaten cleverly by
three-quarters of a mile the effect on the audience
would be much about the same. A strange sense
of inane improbability is diffused by “Thorough-
bred ”in the first two acts. This alleged racing-
scene ot the last act appears hopelessly mad.
* * •
An opulent provincial mayor and his wife
Maria are the leading characters. They are sup-
ported by a daughter who speaks more correctly
than her parents, and a welsher of unknown ante-
cedents. A bankrupt lord, with a son and
daughter and a few other people of superior
manners in tow, fastens on to the mayor’s family.
The stray Amurrikan wanders round after the
lord’s daughter. The lord’s son borrows a blank
cheque from the mayor and buys Roast Chicken,
after which they all go to a villa residence at
Ascot. The wretched civic dignitary finds a
feeble excuse to flee from the police, accom-
panied by the welsher and the lord’s son.
The three fugitives disguise themselves, for
no conceivable reason, as Christy Minstrels,
and go through a certain form of being
funny because their faces are blacked. Here
endeth the third lesson, or act. “Thorough-
bred ” may have been written for the purpose of
introducing an impossible trinity in a state of
burnt cork. Thefperformance is not one of B. and
B.’s greatest achievements. Elton starts to play
the mayor as though he were impersonating a real
individual, instead of a ridiculous joke, and Miss
Jenny Watt Tanner, the awful Maria, is not at
home in low comedy parts; neither does Titheradge
Come reasonably near to an American. Boucicault,
Miss Faber, Miss Noble, arid Dorrington (in a
very minor position) are furnished with characters
that give them a chance to seem natural, more or
less, and Mr. Compton has a character which he is
peculiarly unfitted to adorn.
-* * * *
“A Million of Money” is a fine massive
success at Melb. Royal. The start for the Epsom
Derby is now, for the first time on any stage, a
machine-made spectacle, and White Stocking has
improved a good 141 b. in appearance since the
first night. Also, the victory of the favorite
excites wilder enthusiasm than ever, yet there is
still one thing wanting to complete the moral
lesson of Bland Holt’s Derby. He has for-
gotten to interpolate a “tote” into which
the multitude might pour their sovereigns
with the knowledge that they were getting fair
odds, less 8 per cent. A subsequent tableau,
moonlight on the downs, might show a heap of
bookmakers lying dead upon the course, with
revolvers clutched in their right hands and despair
written on their rigid countenances. These would
show the tote’s value as a turf-purifier, and the
Vic. Leg. Council would pass the forthcoming bill
by an overwhelming majority. “ A Million of
Money ” is certain to run another week or two, so
there will be time to adopt this suggestion.

Harry Rickards is again illuminating the
Opera House bill, but only for five nights. On
Monday next that invincible mezzo - baritone
vocalist and vigorous delineator of coster character
will tune his last lay prior to catching the steamer
for London. This occasion will probably be
marked by the finest gallery chorus on record.
The Gerards, Tudor and Friedman, and the rest
of the co. are for the time being suffering an
eclipse. Rev. Haskett Smith gathers in the silver
of the godly at the Athenaeum Hall, and the Town
Hall will be enlivened until Saturday by dissolving
views, Pepper’s ghosts, concerts, and many other
items, including light refreshments, in aid of the
Tucker Village Settlements.
** * *
Titheradge’s break-down at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, the other night, was a serious business for a
few minutes. Dr. Adams, who hurried down to
the stage from the circle as soon as he saw the cur-
tains drop, found Tith. in an icy perspiration
clinging to Boucicault for support. His heart had
struck work, and it was not until the doctor
straightened him out that Tith. ’s vital organ began
to make healthful music again. An abscess at the
root of a tooth originated this little drama.
Titheradge, a very nervous man, required
chloroforming by Dr. Neild before being
operated upon by dentist Oldfield; and the
combined effects of chloroform and abscess
knocked him out. There hadn’t been a long
enough lapse of time between Tith.’s afternoon
and evening performances. He was still cold and
tottery when he went on for the first act of
“Sowing the Wind,” and by about 8.30 he felt so
disposed to die that Australia nearly lost its old
favorite. This was Tith.’s first experience of his
heart suspending operations—a terrible shock for
a novice. One can get hardened to heart-stop-
pages, however, just as one can cultivate indiffer-
ence to bank failures.
m* * *
The Bishop of Melbourne took the chair
at Rev. Haskett Smith’s first lecture in Mel-
bourne, but he didn’t take it much. Having
introduced Rev. Smith in a small assortment of
well-chosen remarks, be sat in an armchair until
the lights were put out, a period of about four
minutes. As soon as darkness fell upon the audi-
ence Field Flowers Gqe fled. The head and
shoulders of him were visible against the magic-
lantern focus on the sheet as he moved noiselessly
across the platform, like a bishop escaping from
the wrath to come. Then he was gone. He had
prudently remembered an appointment elsewhere,
and crept away to keep it.
LYRICS OF THE STAGE-THE VILLAIN’S
ARREST.
Many scores of sensational dramas
We have taken to heart in our time :
We have wept when the sturdy young farmers
Got “ pinched ” for another man’s crime,
And rejoiced at the evidence hollow
By which all their wrongs were redressed j
But the one bit we never could swallow
Was the villain’s off-handed arrest.
The plots ot those dramas may vary,
Though virtue must triumph at last—
Some heroes are simple and hairy,
And some are clean-shaven and “ fast ”;
Then, the girl, who too blindly has trusted
The forger of grandfather’s will—
She sometimes gets mad and disgusted.
And sometimes she clings to him still.
And the murderers’ methods may differ,
As much as the victims they slay—
Some corpses, we notice, seem stiffer
Than others that fall by the way.
E’en the climax the author arranges
Is often in novelties dressed ;
But a fashion which time never changes
Is the villain’s off-handed arrest.
The police do not ask for a warrant;
They capture his nibs on the word
Of a stranger, who pours out a torrent
Of “ proofs ” primd-facie absurd.
Then he says : “The game’s up! I’m checkmated >’
His chin slowly falls on his chest;
And the gallery-boys are elated
At the villain’s off-handed arrest.
We like to find heroes asserting
Their virtue and truth on the stage ;
We like them to bring down the eurting
On baffled iniquity’s rage ;
But, in language severe and emphatic,
This journal will ever protest
Its contempt for the fiction dramatic
Of the villain’s off-handed arrest.

Dear Bulletin,— He the late Vi Varley’s secession from
the Opera Co. “Ah Pooh” misses the point. The little
woman never questioned the riaht of the Firm to cast
Marton as they pleased—that would have been manifestly
absurd. What she did feel sore about was that, after
eight years’ continuous service—in which she had proved
herself a valuable artiste—they should treat her with Such
a want of courtesy.—Ah Gib.
Fitzgerald Bros’, menagerie and circus is wintering on a
farm at Papatoetoe, near Auckland (M.L.), in charge of
the long-haired Capt. Sutton. There are nearly 70 horses
and ponies devastating the surrounding country, while
the lions, tigers, panthers, &0., may be heard roaring for
food several miles away. J. Burns, the lion, is now a
husband and a father, but the first litter of cubs were
eaten by their loving ma. Two beautiful tiger cubs are
growing up and are in training. Several litters of lion
and tiger cubs are expected before the return of Daniel
and Tomaso from London.
Walter Bentley is lecturing in his broadest Scotch
accent at Brisbane. When all else fails, the Scot and the
Irishman have a good card left in nationality-. The Eng-
lishman badly wants a brogue, too. Come to think of it,
he has one, but somehow it doesn’t take with the public.
“ When the Liliputians were playing in Kentucky, one
of the men in the company vastly pleased a lovely Mays-
ville girl. He stopped at the hotel where she was visit-
ing, and when she met him in the hall after the
she stepped up to him, patted him on the cheek, and then
imprinted one of those kisses that only a Kentucky girl
can dispense. ‘You dear, ’cute little thing!’ she ex-
claimed, as she lifted him for another smack, ‘how old
are you T ‘ Twenty-nine,’ he replied ;‘do it again.’ ”
Thus the London correspondent of the S.A, Advertiser,
writing on May 17 :—‘‘Mr. Mandeno Jackson, Who is
wonderfully well up in Anglo-Colonial musical gossip, and
most kind in letting us know items of interest, tells me
that Sims Reeves has signed and sealed for a tour of the
colonies i,his autumn. He is to receive 100 guineas a per-
formance. Mr. Jackson speaks of the great tenor with
bated breath, but, being a Philistine, I will say at once
that, whatever he may have been 30 years ago, Sims
Reeves is only a grand old wreck now, and I wouldn’t
(from a musical point of view) walk across the road to hear
him. Many colonists will, however, doubtless be glad to
have an opportunity of seeing the singer whose name has,
for half a century, been a household word all over the
world, and be pleased to welcome him to Australia and
Maoriland. But don’t expect Mr. Sims Reeves to sing.
He opens his mouth and articulates in a manner which, I
believe, is a liberal education to the musical tyro, but the
top notes that erstwhile witched the world have gone
never to return.”
Newton Griffiths, a remorseless tragedian who used to
lecture on Deeming and his victims at the Waxworks,
was performing “ Drink ” at the Exhibition Hall, Fitzroy
(Melb.) last week. The playbill concluded with " a beauti-
ful story of a Noble Youth ” who visited a famous states-
man in Washington and was pressed by the f.s. and his
fascinating wife to take one glass of wine. He refused.
The playbill narrative explains the picture of moral
grandeur. “No!” replied the heroic youth resolutely,
gently repelling the proffered glass. “No!” said the
noble youth, his voice trembling a little and his cheeks
flushed, “ I never drink !” (here he grew firmer) “I saw
Newton Griffiths last night in ‘Drink,’ and that wag a
settler! ” The Bulletin is inclined to believe this story.
Some people can’t eat for 48 hours after seeing Newton
Griffiths in anything.

At Ballarat once, a circus had just ended its first part
when the police seized all the performers, except the
clown, and took them to the lock-up for being illegally on
the ground. The clown escaped under the canvas, went
into the ring, and kept the audience amused till the per-
formers began to turn up as they were bailed out.

gentle violet.” And then comes this: “ The beauty of the
perfect blossom had-just been indicated by the loveliness
of the tender bud, never, alas ! to blossom here, but in a
happier world.” The dead deserve sympathy.

Pretty Ethel Haydon was much upset at the outset,of
her London engagement by the strict rule re the non-
admission of relatives or friends of artists in the dressing-
rooms or behind the scenes. Up till then she rarely
stirred out without her mother, who also acted as her
dresser. Now Mrs. Haydon has to leave her daughter at
the stage-door before the performance, and cannot go
near her till it is over.

Marshall Hail’s last musical composition was a failure.
It professed to be a dramatic scene, based upon a
passage from Tennyson’s “ Maud.” It was unmelodiqus,
dull, wearisome, and, in fact, an outrageously unmusical
assault upon a fine poem. It made one think of Gautier,
who printed on one of his books the notification : “Com-
posers are warned not to string notes to these verses.”
