Emile Girard is in Melb. again, appearing at the Opera
House with the comely wife whom he married in Hobart
six years ago, and afterwards took to Paris to acquire
French pantomime polish. Veteran Emile is the same
slim, easy master of his business as ever. He laughs,
and with reason, at the medical doctrine that a man’s
limbs lose their elasticity after the age of 35, and says
that a properly-trained knock-about artist, who makes no
flesh in his middle age, can preserve his stage youth for
three-score years and ten.

Arthur Sullivan is said to be patching up his quarrel
with W. S. Gilbert, and another comic opera collaboration
may result. Sullivan, who gets a taste for gambling from
the Hebrevy blood, and likes to be “in with the swells,”
has taken to the turf as a horse-owner, and has dropped his
jingling guineas thus far. The other day he had two
horses going for a race, sold one of them privately to
Blundell Maple and declared to win with the other.
Whereupon the discarded animal came in first, whilst the
esteemed stable companion finished second. Sullivan re-
tired to the saddling-paddock and composed a dirge.
The card-cutting episode in “The Masqueraders” is
rather suggestive of the gambling scene in Boucicault’s
“Led Astray.” There the husband requests his wife’s
lover to play a bogus game of cards with him, in order
that he may accuse the Lothario of cheating, fling the
cards in his face, and so provide an excuse for a duel
without dragging the lady’s name into the quarrel. The
late Charles Thorne, an American actor, made a great hit
in Boucioault’s scene, which, by the wav, was taken
from a French original. Thorne was the infuriated hus-
band, speaking in deadly whispers.

The most prosaic thing in this world is a policeman. In
England, a little while ago, a professional diver gained a
big advt. by jumping from the roof of a railway-carriage
as if crossed a bridge, and doing a dive of 150 ft. or so into
the river beneath. And when he got out of the water
amid tremendous applause, an absolutely unmoved con-
stable ran him in for “ leaving a carriage while it was in
motion.”

Religious paper,
Mdlle. Liane de Pougy, of the Folies
Eergeres, who is just now the vogue in Paris,
writes a Bulletin correspondent, wears more
jewels than perhaps any woman living. In
default of having any other place to show them
she has rows of splendid solitaire diamonds stitched
down the seams of her dress. She usually wears
a girdle of emeralds and diamonds, the centre
stone being as large as a five-franc piece. Recently
one of the jeunesse doree who wished to be asked
to one of her petits soupers paid for that privilege
a diamond necklet which cost him .£5OOO. At
Monte Carlo she had some words with another
swaggering and bejewelled lady of her class.
Next evening she came into dinner with the most
magnificent jewels all over her, and accompanied
by her maid, almost as bedizened as herself. She
flaunted past her rival triumphantly saying,
“ What do you think of this ? il y en a encore assez
pour trois femmes de chambre. Pah !” When at
Mentone la belle Pougy keeps two gens d’armes
outside her villa to guard her valuables. Her
virtue takes care of itself.

The land around Fremantle (W.A.) is
mostly held by a few old fossils, who flatly refuse
to sell for building purposes, or to build them-
selves, or to lease their land, or to do anything
save vegetate. Consequently, the progress of
Fremantle is practically stopped short; there is
not a house obtainable, and a large number of
people are living in tents outside the town. There
is no visible reason why the fossils in question
should refuse to sell, save their old, crusted
Westralian conservatism on which even a liberal
cash offer fails to make any impression, or else
their belief that the present boom is going to last
for ever, and that prices will rise steadily for
years to come. Meanwhile, the “young ini-
quities ” from the East are beginning to think of
building a new town somewhere else and leaving
Fremantle stranded. The immoveable W.A.
fossil is the province’s worst drawback.
A sample of cotton blankets, made in Ger-
many, and invoiced at 3s. 3d., was shown to the
Vic. Assembly last week by Zox, M.L.A. The
Turner Govt, had adopted the Tariff Board’s
suggestion of al5 per cent, duty on this ghastly
device for warming the poor man’s shoulders, and
it was J. B. Patterson, the freetrader’s friend,
who carried a resolution in favor of 25 per cent.
On Alfred Deakin’s motion, the House decided to
admit cotton blankets duty free when their true
character is branded on their imitation-woollen
faces. This may possibly improve matters.
The unfortunates led by poverty to pur-
chase the German stuff will get it at a low
price, and know it for what it is, and if the local
mills cannot soon tempt them to pay more for a
cheap woollen article, Lord help Victoria. But
until the Vic. mills are able to brand “all wool”
upon their flannelsthey will win norespect for their
blankets. When they have
manufactured a first-class
reputation for something they
will be in a position to talk
to the public.
The wife of N.S.W.
ex-Premier Parkes died on
16th inst. (since the para-
graph in another part of
this issue was printed). She
was aged only 38, and leaves
a still-young family. Some-
one has said that half
most politicians’ successes
are won by the social
influence of their wives.
The ex - Premier’s wife
gained no votes for her
husband, for “society”
cold - shouldered her
from the gilded circle to
to which she never willingly
aspired, and to which her
homely constant
rebuke. But the deceased
lady would have immea-
surably atoned for this, had
atonement been necessary,
by the rare and deep per-
sonal devotion which im-
pelled her, almost with her
dying breath, to urge her
husband to “No Surrender!”
in the election-contest which
he is now waging. The
veteran politician, now
past his 80th year and
twice a widower, has re-
ceived many splendid
testimonials, but none so
noble as the utter trust of
his dead wife in his talents
and his character. His
chequered life has been full
of pathetic phases, but it
has held none more touching
than that in which the bitter
enmity of the electioneering
arena was hushed into re-
spectful sympathy across
the open grave of the woman
who loved him.
Premier Reid’s proposition to reduce
Sydney pilotage-rates “means well, but doesn’t
know.” Port Jackson is unique in the matter of
exemptions, which can be obtained for .£5 by any
foreigner after a couple of visits to the port. This
is Freetrade witli a vengeance. The big French
boats enter Port Jackson free of pilotage, bub
when the little Australian-owned Birksgate paid
Noumea a visit, not long since, the owners had to
pay £49 pilotage. Mr. Reid proposed to reduce
the pilotage one-half, and said that, as regards
exemptions, the men had paid their money and
the Government could not go back on its con-
tract. But here he was wrong. The exorbitant
charges should certainly be reduced one-half, but
there would be no breaking of contract in
compelling all foreign-going ships to pay a
reasonable figure. The exemption is given
a man to enable him to bring his ship
in or out of the harbor without a pilot, but,
as regards the fees, they should be paid
whether a ship takes a pilot aboard or not. If a
master holds an exemption and likes to handle his
own ship, well and good; but that ship should
pay fair dues as in every other port of the
civilised world. Should the exempt ones take
exception to the payment of “ pilotage ” where no
pilot is used, then call the fees something else.
They would smell just as sweet in the Treasury
under any other name. The Govt, loses fully
£lO,OOO per annum on the four big companies
alone. Then, again, though the Government em-
ploys a staff of Harbor pilots, the Marine Board
allows vessels to pay a pound or so to a tug-
boat to move them about the Harbor—with no
pilot and no dues—though such action, in
any other port of the world, would draw a stiff
fine. The revenue derivable from Our Beautiful
Harbor under fair laws, properly administered, is
more worthy of consideration than Premier Reid
is aware of.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties haye been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

cure it with a mixture largely consisting of strych-
nine—which is Foreign-trade.
Times change. Here is the “great N.S.W.
Freetrade-party ” supporting the Republican
Arthur Rae, for the Murrumbidgee. It is not
Rae who has altered, but his enemies !
Shipway, of the legion of commonplace
legislative attorneys, should be easily thrust out
of Paddington by Jawbone Neild —a man who has
at his credit a long list of useful reforms.
“ The fashionable friends of Freetrade.”
But how about the fashionable friends of Labor ?
Just think of it —M‘Millan the ally of Sleath
and barrister Reid the friend of Arthur Rae !
Parkes; “ Why, the very water you drink is
supplied to you under my act of Parliament.”
The Late LADY PARKES.
Even the Deity, if addressing the electors, could
go but one step further. Not that a modest Deity
would.
Dr. James Graham, N.S.W. Freetrade
candidate : “ The collective capacity of the Upper
House is, I admit, greater than the collective
capacity of the Lower House.” True, its rent-
collective capacity !
The Bulletin would be sorry to see Dibbs
beaten at Tamworth by that dryasdust Foreign-
trader, Turkey Piddington, Junr. The Assembly
couldn’t stand two Piddingtons any more than
it could stand two Cullens.
A horrible electioneering announcement
from Pambula : “Major Samuel David Thomas
Augustus Mordina Woods (P.), of Boydtown,
Twofold Bay, has again announced himself as a
candidate for Eden-Bombala.”
The question of the N.S.W. election :
Which party is “working for the mortgagee”—
the Freetrade-party or the Labor-party ? Is Free-
trade going to swallow up Labor, or will Labor
swallow up the Freetraders ?
Premier Reid’s enemies can get little
change out of the fact that he opposed payment of
members. So did Dibbs and Parkes, and yet
Reid was for long one of the only two members
who refused to accept payment.
It is safe to say that M‘Millan (now absent
in England), who is to be returned for Burwood
with a view to his blessing Premier Reid, will, in
the long run, be found cursing him most heartily.
If the Reidites want a friend, let them throw
MMillan out.
Again The Bulletin says : Vote for no
man who is an alderman or a preacher. And
especially avoid mayors. Australian history
shows every alderman and every preacher to have
been a prosaic failure. And a mayor is an aggra-
vated alderman.
Parson Bennet-Anderson, who seeks to
enter N.S.W. Parliament, is a saintly twaddler
who should, and will, meet with the same fate as
B. Short, the righteous assurance-canvasser, who
seeks to oust (from Argyle) Rose, one of t£ie most
useful and intelligent members of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment.
Whether or not Fitzroy electors choose
M'Elhone, they must dismiss for ever as a legis-
lator that ghastlyfailure, grocer-warrior Chapman,
who should go back to his shop and cut bacon
with his sword. There should be no room in the
House either for fiery garter-merchants or for
bellicose pickle-vendors who are nothing and
deserve to be nothing but pickle-vendors.
Personally speaking, it is a pity that Regi-
nald Black, with his unique gift of making
figures attractive, should have to stand aside for
Willoughby in favor of anyone of the Cullen
order. But to publicly shake hands with the
Ass, as Mr. Black has done, is fatal to any poli-
tician.
The Bulletin hopes to see its old col-
league, Traill, the real Parliamentary author of
the N.S.W. One-man-one-vote system, returned
for Cook as against boot-importer Whiddon.
Traill’s great services in the cause of Australian
Radicalism are none the less real because they
have been done with pen instead of voice.
An alderman named Waine opposes Wise
for Flinders. Waine is a nonentity who should
be carefully biffed. His real opinions were re-
cently illustrated in his attitude on the municipal
day-labor versus contract question. As Brains,
not aldermen, are needed in Parliament, the
electors, in choosing between two Foreign-traders,
should act accordingly.
Should the ready but erratic John Norton
be returned for Waterloo, he will, as a parlia-
mentarian, be a hedgehog stuffed with dynamite.
No more aggressive and clever constitutional
authority or political manceuvrer has ever stood
on the Assembly floor; nor has the House seen
many more forcible or better-informed speakers.
Norton should be returned on his law-reform
programme.
The Australian Star last week reprinted
a glowing Protectionist anathema of “What
Freetrade has done for England,” sent to that
journal Deo. 28, several years ago, by Joseph
Cook, of Lithgow (N.S.W.). That Joseph Cook
(subsequently leader of the N.S.W. Parliamentary
Labor-party) is now Postmaster-General in Reid’s
Freetrade Government. Another curse come
home to roost.
Better far for the N.S.W. Protectionist
party to accept Freetrade at the hands of the Reid
Government, along with land and income taxes,
than to accept Protection from the puffed-up
senility of the Council, sans any direct taxation at
all. If the Foreign-trade party is to rule, let pure
and absolute Freetrade be established unto the
pinching of the workman’s stomach. The shortest
way to some men’s brains is through their
stomachs.
Queer that while ex-Treasurer See, a
strong Protectionist, represents a N.S.W. river-
constituency, Works-Minister Young, an equally-
pronounced Freetrader, should be so solid in a
neighboring electorate with precisely similar in-
terests. The explanation, of course, is that Mr.
Young is a person who, for some occult reason,
continually gets Ministerial office, and has power,
as the chief-spending Minister (Works), to provide
his voters with, to put it mildly, a fair share of
loaves and fishes.
If Mr. Walter Harcourt Palmer, who is
opposing O’Sullivan for Queanbeyan, happens to
be the Mr. Palmer who helped to promote the
Colo Valley grab, he should be thrown out with
such violence that people three blocks away will
be able, metaphorically speaking, to hear his skull
crack on the pavement. Likewise, Frank Cotton,
though one of the most brainy and useful men
who has been in the Assembly of late years,
should be summarily rejected for Newtown unless
he publicly swears off Colo,
The Broken Hill Labor-party, fearing no
opposition for the three seats locally held,
is spending all its money in the effort to dislodge
Speaker Abbott from Wentworth. The Speaker’s
opponent is one Scobie, a Menindie saddler, who
has found his way stony since he opposed “ Sir
Joseph” a year ago. Even if returned he will
not be a political star, though he will be a distinct
acquisition as knowing something about pastoral
and land questions at least—the weak point in the
party’s armor hitherto. A year ago he was beaten
by only 93 votes ; and as a canvass has been going
on all the time, in Scobie’s interest, the Speaker
is thought by many to have shown little discretion
in risking the seat again.
Henry Harris, of Harris and Ackman, has
no chance in Fitzroy. In addressing the electors he
denounced payment of members, and said (D.T.)
“if returned, his .£3OO would not.be a penny
benefit to him personally, but should be distri-
buted amongst the poor of the electorate.”
(Cheers.) Which (as the promise of the money to
charity is a benefit to him personally) was just as
indecent as the action of the barrister who, ap-
pearing for plaintiff in a libel-action, announced
that his client would hand to charity any damages
awarded him. The utmost Mr. Harris'can do is
to spoil the chance of M‘Elhone, the only Fitzroy
candidate with any political brains.
For the alleged “Catholic vote,” the only
uncertain quantity in King division, Premier
Reid made a most adroit bid when he said that
the brainy but heartless Parkes had acted with
black treachery to Edward Butler, one of the
noblest of men, who resigned the N.S.W.
Attorney-Generalship because Parkes had broken
his promise to give him the Chief Justiceship.
He (Premier Reid) was then in the (strangers’)
gallery, at the Assembly, and at once vowed (that
was long over 20 years ago ! how we do countour
chickens !) never to be a colleague of Parkes, who,
in saying that Butler “had not sufficient social
recognition to be given the Chief Justiceship,”
had revealed his true self. Yet Parkes, who had
always sneered at him (Reid), had never formed a
Ministry for the last 10 years without offering
him office.
Premier Reid’s statement that the N.S.W.
property-holders pay only about £BOO,OOO and the
wage-earners £1,900,000 per annum in taxes has
moved theS.M. Herald to a calculation, whereby
it endeavors to show that the property-holders
really pay £959,000 and the wage-earners only a
little over £1,800,000. In this calculation, it is
assumed that the propertied and professional
class drinks all the wine that is imported, that it
drinks 10 times as much imported fieer per head
as the wage-earner, that the wage-earner smokes
the same proportion of cigars as the other fellow,
that the 15 per cent, ad valorem duties—
which include such things as clocks, carpeting,
portmanteaux, chinaware and watches are
wholly paid by the propertied class, and that it
pays all the probate and stamp duties ! The
£959,000 which the Herald alleges to be paid by
the propertied class of the community isn’t much
even if it did pay it, and the particulars of the
calculation, which are recklessly supplied by Mr.
W. J. Lyne, prove that it doesn’t contribute any-
thing like that amount, and that a land-tax is
urgently wanted to make things a little more
even. The person with a bad case should avoid
details as he would a serpent.

no member shall be a shareholder or otherwise
interested in local steamship companies ; also, that
no member shall place himself under obligations
to shipping co.’s officials by getting billets for his
fneucls.

Society
Viscount Hampden, the N.S. W. Governor-
elect, and his noble brother have a creamery and
milk-run of their own at ’Ome, and make it pay.
This fact will be appreciated by Pint Pots — The
Bulletin means Potts Point. It establishes
H.E.’s claim to be considered an aristocrat of the
first water. When the Viscount arrives Sydney
society will consist mainly of rum-and-milk.
* * *
The majestic Acting-Governoress Madden
struck a new idea for her first deputy vice-regal
ball. After the opening set of lancers she re-
turned in state to the dais and gave reception to
nine debutantes. These favored spinsters, who
didn’t form a specially beautiful crowd, shook the
royal hand in turn, and were desired to take seats
adjoining Her Majesty, whence they were pre-
sently rescued by their partners for the next hop.
After which they mingled with the rest of the
company on even terms. Lady Madden’s
opinions concerning the beautiful innocence
of maidenhood are supposed to have been
partly responsible for this trotting out of the nine
debutantes, the balance of the reason being j, a, m,
jam. In her eyes there is something holy and
reverend about the virgin who is “ coming out ”
at Guvmentouse. Providence has just called the
timid fawn to take her place in “society,’’and Lady
Madden, accommodating her young friend with a
seat on the da* is Providence’s special agent.
THE MAORI LAND NEW WOMAN.-Scene No. 1.
The occasion is not quite so solemn as a baptismal
service, but it forms a sort of semi-secular link
between confirmation and marriage. ILady M.
might rig-up a suitable catechism for debutantes
a id have them “ prepared ” for the ordeal by a
dowager of acknowledged virtue.
• * *
A daughter of an arch-boomster cropped
up among the nine debutantes presented at the
Court of St. Smellbourne the other evening. She
appeared in a somewhat costly cream satin, also
her ma figured prominently in pink satin, and
papa wore a black suit d la nolle prosequi, let in
with tinsel tentacles and watered with depositors’
tears. What is it that Fritz Rimma says of Cap-
tain Coddington, in “In Town?” “ He’s very
thick ! ” The Bulletin is disposed to say the
same thing of the boomster who was only lately
whitewashed by Acting Gov. Madden (in his
judicial capacity), in consideration of having paid
nothing in the £. “He’s very thick,” is that
boomster. There were a good many thick ’uns at
this unblushing function (and several Judges
rubbing shoulders with them), but the areh-
“ banker ” struck one as being the thickest of all.
* * *
A Melb, daily paper mentions, re the
Madden ball at Guvmentouse, that most of the
women wore black, but “the debutantes, those
studies in white, supplied the high lights.”
Which calamitous displacement of internal organs
was no doubt consequent upon tight-lacing.
* * *
Twenty-two years ago a Victorian was
convicted on a charge of felony and sent to prison
for a term. On his release he didn’t seek his
young wife, who had gone back to her parents in
the meantime, but went far afield and married
another woman by whom he had two children. In
due course the original wife, having other arrange-
ments in view, spent £lOO in obtaining a divorce,
which involved a charge of bigamy against her
former spouse. He got two years for the bigamy,
and the poor woman he had married under false
pretences died of a broken heart. Time went on.
The divorced wife didn’t capture the other fellow
as she had expected, or perhaps she changed her
mind. So she set forth to find the bigamist, who
was now a free man again in all respects, and per-
suaded him to marry her once more. They got
along well together. Their second joining in
matrimony is a complete success, and the two
children of the other woman have found an
affectionate mother in their father’s twice-wedded
wife.
Now, here’s a pointer for Sydney matrimonial-
agencies. What are the odds that Dean and Mrs.
D. couldn’t be re-united ? After George’s meet-
ing with his wife the other day, The Bulletin
(which has considerably more respect for the
hero’s heart than for his head) puts them at about
(j to 4 against. According to the yarn in the
North Shore Times about the only paper
which believes that Mrs. Dean, in the words of
an asinine correspondent, has been “ used (most
cruelly and unkindly ” —Dean went to the house
in Little Collins-street, Surry Hills, where his
wife is staying, to get his bicycle. Dean
was slightly abashed at the meeting, and stood
with downcast eyes; but his wife was calm and
unemotional as a marble woman.” Soft v. tough,
apparently. “In answer to a question, civilly put,
Mrs. Dean produced the bicycle, and gave it to
Dean. . . . Our informant was an eye-witness
of the meeting, and says: ‘ Mary is looking
lovely—the roses are coming back to her che’eks,
and she’ll be the pride of Riley-street once more.”
And this paper bets that, with the slightest en-
couragement, Dean will come philandering around
in the same old fashion, heedless of what he has
gone through. Men are built that way—poor
wretches !

One of Acting-Gov. Madden’s daughters is
the best-looking girl ever seen in the vice-regal
box at Flemiugton during the last dozen years,
anyway. Also, the other daughter deserves favor-
able mention, and Mrs. Madden possesses features
of interest, whilst papa, the Judge, has a beautiful
skin and fine serio-comic appearance. Altogether,
the Madden family is a pictorial success, which
the Brassey crowd will certainly not eclipse, and,
for the sake of the pretty Miss M. aforesaid, The
Bulletin would prefer to leave vice-regal arrange-
ments in Victoria undisturbed.
* • •
“A Constant Reader” of The Bulletin
artfully, and in the guise of disinterestedness,
explains how Mr. Lees, Sydney’s Sunday-school-
superintendent-Mayor, came to give a wine-party
to the Gaiety girls :
The respected Mayor, who cannot from religious
scruples countenance public balls, seems to have, nolens
volens, attained some notoriety through the presence in
our midst of these charming creatures, the Geegees. It
is rather hard that, as a good Wesleyan, he should be
thought to have even dreamt of entertaining them. But
that greatness was thrust on him, for the true tale goes
thusly. The Geegees, on sight-seeing bent, invaded the
Town Hall, there to And the Mayor entertaining sundry
gentlemen per medium of wine and cake. Could even the
least gallant of men do other than ask such beauteous
visions to share the mayoral hospitality? It goes without
saying that the Mayor’s party helped to entertain them
with all the goodwill in the world.
Perhaps this correspondent will now oblige by ex-
plaining why Mayor Lees declined to preside at
the big Town Hall meeting to advocate the
appointment of a commission of enquiry into the
Dean alleged poisoning case.
# * *
A North Sydney divorce-case now on
the tapis will, if it eventuates, be one of the
most sensational suits ever heard in Australasia.
There are a few people who could, in several tries,
guess the name of one of the parties to the pro-
spective suit. But, so far, there aren’t many who
would dare to guess the name of the third party
out loud. From that case may come the solution
of a mystery.
* * #
Several Adelaide High Church clergymen
are already preparing for the coming of Evangeli-
cal Brewer Buxton by gradually lessening the
number of candles on their altars and otherwise
limiting the evidences of their Ritualism.
The Melbourne undertakers’ journal, the
Light, is not unworthy of a humorous profession.
Its telephone directory suggests the fatal character
of the little publication by furnishing the names
and numbers of about 150 doctors who may be
rung up. Then there is the advt. of a French
lady who supplies accomplished corpse care-takers
during the interval between death and interment,
and says—“ Rough—even if kindly—hands should
not be allowed to desecrate the remains of your
loved ones.” Finally, the advt. of Blank and
Sons, high-class undertakers, advises every pru-
dent citizen to order a heavy polished oak casket
while able. These desirable freehold residences
oan be purchased for £5 each, and the appeal of
Blank and Sons is irresistible. If you have one
ready to hand when required you are popped into
it, whereas if you die without having taken
thought for the morrow, “your executors will
perhaps consider the firm’s excellent 30s. coffin
good enough.” This warning, by the way, applies
to other post-mortem matters. If you don’t make
certain of a £lO tombstone, your executors may
be tempted to order a small cross at half the
money, and unless you buy your grave beforehand
you dunno where you are after your decease. The
Light is essentially a journal for anyone who
doesn’t feel well.

The latest S.A. public-service embezzler
was a cashier, long past middle-age, who was paid
under £3OO a year to handle thousands a day.
His predecessor received nearly twice the salary.
There is a tendency in S.A. Civil Service to emu-
late the (of course, Y.M.0.A.) youth who, when
asked “What salary do you want?” replied,
“None, sir; so long as I’m cashier I’ll look out
for myself ! ”
# * #
The latest Melb, horror is the coffin-can-
vasser, who, at all hours of the day and night,
places before everyone he meets the advantages of
his special brand of “corpse-casket ” over the old
and familiar kinds. The Melb. householder is, so
tar, powerless against this ghoul, who expatiates
by the yard on the comfort a corpse will enjoy by
reposing in the patent air-tight, damp-proof
receptacle (of which a miniature sample is shown)
manufactured by his firm.
# * *
The Queensland Attorney-General, in advo-
cating his Gambling-Suppression Bill, denies that
church-bazaars are gambles. Right. They cer-
tainly are not games of chance. The church gives
no chances.
• • •
H. : “ Apropos the Melb. clergy’s anti-
gambling furore, let it be pointed out again that
the Scriptures, to which they refer as the infallible
rule, have not a single word which can even be
tortured into a condemnation of gambling ;
whereas heaps of texts oan be construed on the
other side, such as those which urge the advantage
of being denuded of cash. The clergy are all on
the ungospelly Thomas Binney racket—How to
Make the Best of Both Worlds. Christ’s doctrine
is that it is a good thing for a man to lose his
money.”

Prior Vaughan gave himself away in the
worst fashion in one of his recent speeches
Said that R.C. potentate;—
The British empire, of which it was their boasted
privilege to be loyal subjects, was the greatest country
in the world. . . . There were 29,000,000 people iii
England, of whom 2,000,000 were Roman Catholics.
England is the centre of the British empire. And
the recipe for making the greatest country in the
world is 27,000,000 Protestants and unbelievers
and 2,000,000 Roman Catholics.

* # »
One of the most amusing sights of Melb.
is the photo, of a very fat boy in the show-case of
a Swanston-street photographer. The youth,
scarcely past the quince-hunting stage, is attired
in a gorgeous military costume, all epaulets and
gold lace, and a scowl of vast dimensions illumines
his countenance as he withdraws his Brobdingnag-
nian sword, apparently with theintentionof slaying
the unoffending camera and scattering its innards
on the studio floor. The Vic. defence force is such
a comic institution that nobody outside the pro-
vince can properly appreciate its enormity. Some-
body should certainly drop W. S. Gilbert a line
about it.

Last week saw ice in a pond in Hyde
Park, Sydney, and snow upon the roofs of an
early-morning train that arrived from the West.
The wives of the Adelaide plutocracy have
already begun to canvass the local working women
in view of the general election next April. • They
drive round, ascertain if each woman’s applica-
tion for a vote has been sent in, and then plausibly
sing their little song : “My husband, you
know, clearly sees that this terrible depression is
entirely due to the Labor members. He will not
spend any money, and as his friends also lack con-
fidence work is scarce. If these dreadful socialistic
doctrines were not so prevalent in Parliament the
wealthy men would be more enterprising, every-
one would have work to do, and we should all be
better off.’’
* # *
The cost of maintaining prisoners in Pen-
tridge Gaol is =£3o 11s. 3d. per head per annum ;
11s. 9d. per week. There are scores of Melb,
boarding-house keepers who would be glad to
accommodate even prisoners at this figure, or
something less. The Govt, might try the experi-
ment. Living in a boarding-house is an un-
equalled deterrent.
# * *
The Yic. authorities, in the halcyon days
of political influence, did not too curiously inquire
into a billet-hunter’s educational qualifications —
provided his Parliamentary sponsons were all
right. A retired civil-servant now living in Rich-
mond (Vic.) once said to a fellow clerk, “How
do you spell income ? ” The latter, looking over
the shoulder of his questioner, saw that he had
written down “incurn.” “That’s wrong,’’ ex-
claimed the clerk. “So it is,’’ said the other. “ I
can’t tell how the devil I came to omit the ‘ b ’ at
the end.”
* * »
The other evening a girl appeared at a
Vic. public fancy-dress ball as “Innocence,”
dressed from head to toe in white. Within 21
hours the police had arrested her. She had, it was
alleged, stolen every article worn by her at the
ball.
* * *
The young Australian, said somebody or
other, is “ a casual cuss.” The other day a girl of
12 years was travelling in the Geelong (Vic.)
train, and somehow omitted to get out at the
right station, where her mother was waiting for
her. She discovered her mistake soon afterwards,
so she simply waited until the train was sprinting
along at 35 miles an hour past her house, which
was close to the line a mile or two further on, and
then she opened the carriage-door and casually
stepped off. She struck the ground, and then
flew into the air upside-down and struck the
ground again, and after that she gathered herself
up and walked home in the same casual fashion,
quite uninjured, and was casually playing the
piano when her astounded mother returned.
M'Lellan, M.L A.
has heard of a beauti-

the bun-and-banana fund ; ladies, Is. 6d. “Society”
likewise feels depression’s pinch. At Prahran
iown Hall, the other evening, Aoting-Gov. Mad-

Is this the land of drought and glare,
Where sunstroke takes the head that’s bare,
And bush-tracks burn the feet ?
Is this the Sunny South we know,
Where billies boil by lenses’ glow,
And verdure dies of heat ?
In Hyde Park, ice ; on train-tops, snow ;
The morning’s milk all frozen ; Oh !
And last week’s mutton’s sweet!

“The Sydney unemployed,” writes a Bul-
letin correspondent, “are allowed to change
their milk and rice (sarcastically called extras by
the Bureau) for tobacco and other articles, but the
contractor will not exchange any surplus bread
for meat; or sugar, of which some families use very
little, for cocoa, soap, starch, or any other useful
article. Also, on the ground of being property-
owners, many men who have paid through the
building society, &c., a considerable amount on
their homes are refused work for rations at the
Centennial Park.”

The other day a big
“surprise” party was arran-
ged in Slowbart, The sur-
prises havirig resolved that
the decorations and table fur-
niture should be right up to
the mark. One man lent his
plate, another a handsome
table-cloths, vases, &c. The
party went off swimmingly
until they started to pack up.
Then a grim person appeared
from below stairs, and said :
“ I forbid you to remove any
of those goods on any pretence
whatever. I’m in charge of
this place as bailiff for rent ”
—and he showed them his
authority. It was a lovely
surprise—especially as there
are. in the world so many
people who, while ever ready
to spend pounds in (inciden-
tally) liquoring-up a man,
wouldn’t spend pence in paying out a distress-
warrant for him.
# * •
The case of the Abrahams brothers, of
gun-fraud notoriety, is a pretty stiff warning to
defendants who may harbor the design of fainting
in Court. Two of the unhappy creatures appear
to have carried the starvation trick so far that, at
latest dates, they were suffering from brain-
paralysis. The first effect of gaol-hospital refresh-
ment upon all four black-fasters was a distinct
improvement in their condition, but brothers
Emanuel and Louis subsequently suffered a
relapse, after the manner of the unfed outcast who
is picked off a doorstep and regaled on soup at a
police-station. Perhaps the next prisoner who
faints in Court will recover consciousness to find
his mouth being held ajar by the learned Judge,
whilst counsel for the prosecution loads him up
with a mutton-chop and half-a-pint of stout.
** * |
The Victorian naval force can take a hard
seat close to the door after its recent experiences
with the Hilaria. When that kerosene-laden
vessel was in full blaze, arid it was found impos-
sible to tow it away from the wharf, a party of
marine cavalry from the alleged warship Nelson
undertook the duty of sinking it. They got a
121 b. breech-loading field-piece, fixed it on a barge,
and moored the barge within 50 yards of the ship.
Then they fired six shots in succession, and either
never hit it in a vital spot or else missed it alto-
gether. Then an officer of experience in the
torpedo branch seized on the occasion with both
hands. He got alongside in a boat, and solemnly
drove a nail in the side of the vessel, and on the
nail he hung a big canister of gun-cotton. Three
ineffectual attempts were made to explode this,
and finally the torpedo-man retired, amid the ap-
plause of the spectators. Finally, the department,
roused to desperation, brought along a great
quantity of dynamite, which went off, possibly by
accident, and blew a hole in the Hilaria that an
omnibus could go through. This shows that the
Vic. naval force can hit an enemy in ten tries, if
the enemy stands quite still and doesn’t offer any
resistance, and allows the force to drive nails in
him to hang the gun-cotton on. But, unluckily,
the bloated armaments of Europe don’t allow any-
body, to drive nails in them with impunity.

The cable driveller wires from London the
thrilling information that “ Mr. Agnew Ralston,
who was assistant private secretary to the Earl of
Hopetoun, late Governor of Victoria, has been
appointed by Lord Hopetoun as factor for his
Scottish estates.” “Factor” is Scotch for the
man who collects the rents and puts the bailiff in
when they are not paid.

heads of four branches of one Vic. public depart-
ment have, in order to preserve their own official
existence, left themselves with hardly any staff at
all. Apropos, a certain Yic. service mag-
nate, a cruel “crawler,” presented to the
bluffest and gamest member of the Berry
Ministry, just before Black Wednesday, a long
list of men who, he thought, could be done with-
out in one of the departments. “ Are you sure,”
enquired the Minister, “ that these can all be dis-
pensed with?” “Yes, sir,” said the head. “And
the country can do without you,” remarked the
Minister, adding on the chief’s own name.

At Melb. Methodist Union meeting, Way,
Fitchett, and all of them, skilfully avoided the
real crux. The obstacle is that the Wesleyan
Church has certain snug funds for the benefit of
the clergy. The question is whether the poor
disjuncted clergymen of the Church are to come
in upon them.
# # *
The very latest variety of kleptomania is
that of a Melb. man who was lately sentenced to a
year’s gaol for an elaborate attempt to acquire .£lO
worth of ladies’ underclothing by false pretences.
In an .appeal heard before Judge Walsh, it was
set forth by a number of witnesses that accused
was a man of the highest character, and possessed
of considerable means, and that the cause of his
lamentable falling-away was simply “a strange
desire to become possessed of the articles enumer-
ated.” He was too bashful and nervous to acquire
them personally, so he ordered them by letter to
be sent to a non-existent lady at the house of a
clergyman, and made arrangements for intercept-
ing the parcel. His intentions, however, were
strictly honorable ; he proposed to secretly
forward his cheque for the amount, and
only desired the goods that he might gloat
over them in secret. In fact, as his counsel
reiterated with much eloquence, his downfall was
wholly owing to a shy and blushful desire “to
become possessed of the articles enumerated.”
The Judge was so much staggered by this view of
the case that he reduced the sentence to three
months. This opens quite a new line of defence,
and henceforth the man who bolts with your wife,
or abducts your watch with violence, or disappears
with your cash out of the bank, will also plead
that he was overcome with a sudden and violent
desire to possess the articles enumerated. Come
to think of it, that same desire to possess the
articles enumerated has caused more crime than
any other desire on record.
* * #
One of the most pathetic things about the
fiscal issue is the distressed widow who writes to
the papers, or to politicians, heartrending stories
about the way she has suffered through the
iniquitous policy which that paper or politician is
opposed to. Lately, MacLaurin, M.L.C.
(N.S.W.), had a widow who was so peculiarly
situated that the land-tax would reduce her from
comfort to something approaching penury.
Sydney D.T. replies with a widow who was
left, in 1886, with a dead husband, a baby,
a helpless orphan sister, and a dying brother,
all of whom she supported from her own
earnings. The 5 per cent, ad valorem duties were
imposed, and the widow had to pay Bs. more in one
week for the necessaries of life, altogether apart
from clothing. Considering that bread, meat,
coal, firewood, milk, butter, vegetables, fruit, and
many other necessaries were unaffected by the
duties, and allowing that the retailer charged the
said duties three times over to the consumer,
The Bulletin figures out that this widow
spent over .£6 in that week for food alone and
quite apart from clothing, boots, rent, gas, &c.
And, as she earned her own living this paper
hereby records its opinion that she was a really
hard-working widow with enormous grit and
more muscle than a horse. Bring along the next
widow.

When a man comes out of gaol under
extraordinary circumstances, every
heavy, slow-witted Uncle Pumble-
chpok in the community thinks it his
duty to make a lengthy oration to the
tired victim, and advise him to be
sober and virtuous, and cultivate a
contented mind, and wear flannels
next to his skin, and not get his
feet wet, and to always avoid indi-
gestible food, and be careful to wind
up the clock and put the cat out be-
fore going to bed. And then the
victim is expected to express himself
deeply grateful for the advice, and to
promise that he will follow it impli-
citly. All of which is a particularly
sickening advt. for the orator, whose
object is generally to keep himself
before the public with a view to get-
ting into Parliament.

A Maorilander, charged recently with being
drunk in a public place, pleaded it was no offence.
Beak looked up the Act, and found he was
correct. It seems you must charge the culprit
with being found drunk. Beak said straight:
“The offence is in being found out.” They laid a
fresh information,

Remittance received on May 10....M0nt.: “F.’s”
citation of the host of issues involved in the “mee-
jum-of-exchange ” problem incidentally explains why,
in word-of-mouth debate, the better man so often
cuts the worst polemical figure. The more comprehensive
his intellect, the more complications he perceives in a
question which, to a narrower intelligence, appears to
admit of a dogmatic “yes” or “ no.” The ready debater,
in fact, is, as often as not, the outcome merely of mental
circumscription, and the commonly-observed anomaly
that a man may be as ineffective, vied voce, as he is
powerful on paper, is due to the fact that, in oral dis-
cussion, he is—like the better-armed but clumsier ship—
liable to be “ raked ” by an antagonist he could simply
blow out of the water if with the pen he could bring his
broadside to bear... Orari: Thin and trivial... Levuka :

good. If that prisoner w’ould, were he in Sydney general

champagne in the gaol-hospital, even if he had been 5000
times convicted. That is the view we’d take if we were a
gaol-doctor ; and that is the view for which the doctors
as a body should, and, we firmly believe, would fight.
The chemist business of the late Mr. Frank
Senior, senior, J.P., at 246 George-street, Sydney,
will now be carried on by his son, Mr. Frank
Senior, J.P., who has managed the business for
the past 14 years. He is sole proprietor of Senior’s
Tonic and Liver Pills, which Australian Judges
are invited, in the name of Justice, to keep by
them always in order to ensure unlivered decisions.
Politicians, too, suffering from colds and hoarse-
ness consequent on hustings oratory and night air,
should take Senior’s Black Currant Balsam
regularly. Then their voices will become clear,
melodious and seductive.

July 16, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Mr. and Mrs. Alec. Marsh’s “at home” at
their hotel, last week, was a hugely-attended
function most admirably arranged. Miss Esty
-Mrs. Marsh') wore a flower-like combination of
lilac silk and pale-green velvet, and carried a
graceful shower posy, and Mr. Marsh, with a
button-hole like a beacon, backed her up admir-
ably in the pump-handle business. Delightful
vocal music, per medium of Miss Esty, Nellie
Young, Mr, Marsh (who sings as easily in a
crowded room hung with non-acoustic draperies
and pictures as in the Town Hall), and Mr. Cun-
ningham, ’bnade the afternoon pass like a dream
in Z flat; and Hair Vollmar, with his ’cello, was
responsible for much real melody. Afternoon-tea
circulated most persistently, and the decorations
were good.
Loud applause followed every musical item, and
I couldn’t help regarding these unstinted marks
of approval on the part of “society,” which
studiously avoided the reserved-seats at the Esty-
Marsh concerts, as being a very dreadful thing.
To have maintained its former attitude with
dignity, Potts Point, when invited to hear the
artists in private, should either have bucked away
from the invitation as it bucked away from the
box-office, or else have attended Miss Esty’s re-
ception (it is expecting too much of “ Society ” to
suppose it could forego a gratis entertainment),
but remained stonily uninterested when the sing-
ing began. To see people who had remained afar
off from the pay-concerts climbing over one
another to get at the artistes and splitting their
gloves encoring when a free show turned up, was
a perfect object-lesson in the hide-bound and im-
pecunious manners and methods of the Top Ten.
And another awful tableau was the band of “ pro-
fessional musicians,” who told you they were
determined not to sing or play or scrape, if re-
quested, but who looked crestfallen when nobody
asked them to, nevertheless. And then the
scurries of amateurs who hung round the piano
absolutely dying to be pressed to oblige the com-
pany ! It must be a most uneasy thing to be
“ musical.”
The “grand non-sectarian” charity ball at
Sydney Town Hall last week had the honor (or
the other thing) of presenting the most motley
assemblage I have ever seen on the floor of
Sydney’s leading public ballroom. A “crown”
dance is always heterogeneous, but then, it is
usually held in a small room and in a chastened
spirit. When you multiply it and spread it out
over a big floor-space the effect is simply frightful—
on the same principle that the common or bed-
room whatsisname, observed with the naked
eye can’t thus compare for horror with itself
as it appears when scrutinised through a
magnifying-glass. The decorations were very
good, the supper-tables neatly furnished, but
the music a trifle incoherent. As the various
Sydney firms responded liberally, and the hall
was lent for nothing, the hop should be a good
thing for impecunious Sydney.
A feature of this ball was the crowd in the gal-
leries, which certainly got a good show for its
money—for, even if Mr. and Mrs. Dacre hadn’t
kindly, but inaudibly, performed a short comedy-
sketch on the platform, the many human and side
shows visible would have kept anybody doubled
up with mirth for a week. The ball was plain
and fancy-dress sartorially—“ non-sectarian,” as
one might say—but the dressing was so generally
remarkable that one hardly knew t’other from
which. One woman, for instance, in a black dress
out half-way down her back, with off-the-shoulder
sleeves and Bacchanalian shoulder-straps of crim-
sqp roses, evidently had no idea she was there in
character as “lady off a cigar-box.” But the
funniest group in'the room was a “bride”—
orange-blossoms, veil, white satin and all—a sort
of troubadour on two bright blue legs, and a third
man in a,brick-dust-red toga with an ordinary
hard hat painted red on his skull. Whether it
was Charlotte Corday or an idealisation of a Dan.
O’Connor postal pillar was not apparent. Some of
the fancy costumes were not so aggressive—nurses
in linen frocks—Gainsborough girls grabbing at
the chance to powder their noses in the flagrant
fancy-dress manner—and so on.
The Gaiety Girls turned up most desperately
frescoed. lam afraid the habit theseoharmershave
of “ clapping a little on occasionally,” as Codding-
ton says, is steadily undermining their reputation
for enduring lorveliness, for if beauty is merely a
matter of grease-paint inches deep and vegetable
rouge, “who is so poor that would not be a
beauty?” as Brutus almost said. Maud Hob-
son in porcelain-blue brocade (but with no
very astonishing diamond display)—perhaps she
has read and still believes in all the Botany Bay
convict stories—had evidently come straight off
the stage without waiting to wash her face. Ethel
Selwyn, in cream brocade, also bore evidence of
the same omission. The lady who loves to do the
Block with an Ophelia expression about her back-
hair and a snake in her hand, wore pink and
black and lota of purohaseable complexion ; but
Florence Lloyd and Miss Kearney, both in crow-
black gowns, were apparently guiltless of make-
up. Other professionals present were Miss Alice
Esty, m pale-blue brocade, with trimmings of fine
lace and silver passementerie, who came with Mr.
Marsh; Mrs. Dacre, in black and silver, her
willowy husband, and one or two more; but
“ Society ” pure and simple was not hugely repre-
sented at the “grand charity ball.” Acting vice-
royalty posed on the dais, with Miss Darley in
pink; but the attendance tapered away very
rapidly into “who is it?” after that. The fact
was that a lot of people had no fellowship with
this sudden dance, which almost bumped against
the date fixed upon, so long ago as May last, by
another ball-committee.
On dit that there is more than a storm in
a tea-cup in certain sectarian circles over the
action of an (also sectarian) official, who thinks
his road the shortest cut to the jasper gates, and
considers the other track a mere cul-de-sac. But
it is in the highest degree both unbecoming and
unofficial for a public man, supposedly represent-
ing the citizens as a whole, to abstain from giving
the whole weight of his patronage (whatever that
may be worth) to any charitable or benevolent
public object whatsoever. Mayor Lees’ latest is
to avoid lending the full light of his countenance
to one of the largest and best charity-balls of the
year—held to assist the Tempo refuge for unfor-
tunate women—just because the institution hap-
pens to be conducted by nuns ? I sincerely hope
that never again will Sydney be afflicted with a
civic head whose “conscience” is so vulgarly
inelastic as to prevent him from helping forward
with all his resources every truly charitable move-
ment, whether run in the name of God or of
Joss. The narrowness of the Mayor (surely the
head of the city should be no less liberal
and tolerant than the Acting - Governor and
his wife have shown themselves to be !) will, I pre-
dict, find little sympathy on the present occasion,
inasmuch as there is no Protestant, lady or gentle-
man, who fails to recognise and admire the unsel-
fishness of the works of the R.O. Sisters of Mercy.
Sydney Liedertafel’s last concert as an unre-
lieved male society (they have skirts in the recipe
after this), at Sydney Town Kail, was a regular
dying-swan song—very sweet to finish up with,
don’t you know. The society, framed in ferns,
was in excellent voice and splendid tune in the
region of the orchestra, and altogether I don’t
think I have ever heard a better-sung lied than
“ The Sowers,” in which the nuances were finely
presented ; while, for a less sentimental number,
“ The Way to Build a Boat ” was very exhilarat-
ing. The Liedertafel orchestra’s performance of a
classic adagio was also most soothing. A big
audience as bright as a scatter of beads pervaded
the hall, headed by the Acting-Governor, and a
regular scoop of a party.
Miss Edith King, Miss Evelyn, Mr. Weir and
Kowalski “assisted” the society, which is so
strong _ in itself that it scarcely needs much
bolstering-up. I don’t think the Liedertafel
should make common concerts of these little
affairs—it spoils their novelty. Miss King, who
made her debut as far as I was concerned, wore a
white dress with big white sleeves, and when she
breathed over her shoulders (a bad trick she’s got)
the effect was just like a pigeon making up its
mind to fly, but thinking better of it. However,
Miss King has such a sweet, full, dramatic mezzo-
soprano that one forgives a mannerism here and
there. Miss Evelyn, in pale-pink and green,
proved to have a sweet contralto voice, which will
be heard to greater advantage when the lady gets
into her platform stride, as it were, and when she
doesn’t lay on the vocal color quite so thickly. A
songstress who renders a simple song with a whole
tragedy of meaning in her tones is almost as
irritating as a woman weeping in a black-bordered
handkerchief over the remains of a pet poodle.
Among a parcel of new music recently sent to
me is the “Gaiety Waltz,” by E. Lon Pitt. It is
a fairly lively and well-named musical effort, but
it does not perpetuate any of the tunes in the
Gaiety Co.’s repertoire, as most people would
imagine by its title ; and it is also written in five
flats—a key which breaks up the common or gar-
den waltz-player, “The Katoomba” is quite the
latest local waltz to reach me. It is by a young
girl—Miss Maguire—who dedicates her first
printed effort to Acting-Governor Darley and his
wife —a very suitable dedication, considering that
the A.G. is the little tin-god of that lovely moun-
tain resort (advt.), The waltz is mostly written
with a pretty tune and the beat well sustained,
and there is also a certain sequence of musical
ideas and flow from start to finish that should
command success. *
I have also received a song, “Beauty’s Lul-
laby,” composed by Miss Louise Clarke, a Yic.
professor of music, and dedicated to Grace
Palotta, whose portrait is thrown in on the cover.
The song is French-English and is certainly very
plaintively strung together, the music being of the
old-fashioned school and framed to suit words
alleged to be written four hundred years or so ago.
It is altogether an oddly-attractive lullaby—the
only disturbing thing being an accent tearing in
the wrong direction over the final e in “ beaut A”
Another Melbourne melody is a song “In an Old
Wardrobe,” words from the Argus, music by
W. F. Turner. The music is quite harmless—
and, perhaps, as the whole thing is stated on the
cover to be in memory of poor, pretty Violet
Varley, a portrait of whom adorns the front page,
it is put beyond the pale of criticism. But I rather
fancy both poet and composer, if they contemplate
their work six months hence, will regret the
impulse which led them to rush into print
and wed music to mere bathetic doggerel
descriptive of poor Violet’s theatrical ward-
robe, from Patatout’s little sword to “Marten’s
folded skirt.” A new comic duet in Sydney is
“The New Woman and the New Man,” words by
Tucker, and music by Lyndon. It has been sung
with great success at the Tivoli, and is every bit
as good as the comic songs which come out
stamped with the approval of the London’alls.
Which, you may say, is a rather left-handed
compliment.
The final appearance of the Esty-Marsh co., in
connection with enterprising photographer Eden’s
popular concerts at Sydney Town Hall, attracted
thousands of people again last Saturday. The
programme was compiled to suit what is under-
stood to be the public taste, and so the most
classic event was Mr. Alec Marsh’s dramatic sing-
ing of the “Fagliacci” overture; though Miss
Esty (who wore white and silver brocade with a
lovely pair of sleeves of the latest Parisian cut,
curving in to the figure, and much more elegant
than the big, abrupt, prize-pumpkin sort of abnor-
mality we think is quite le dernier mot) gave the
Jewel Song, from “ Faust,” delightfully. But so
many amateurs have murdered this charming
seena, in the sturdy tones of a steam-whistle, that
it has got to be regarded as a sort of vocal
“ Battle of Prague ” when taken outside the opera,
and so is decidedly “popular,’ Miss Esty had
the triumph of the evening after it—flowers by
the truck-load and loud shrieks of approval from
a crowd which was only pacified by a triple
encore. Mrs. C. A. Edwards assisted the com-
pany with a very soft and tender rendering of
Behrend’s “The Gift, ” for which she was bouqueted
and encored —indeed, the encoring was dreadful,
and should certainly be checked when it turns
14 numbers into 26 !
People have no consciences—and though this
appreciation is always welcome, still, killing by
kindness is a recognised slaughter, and audiences
should take the price into consideration when they
are clamoring for more. At one shilling for 14
items you get your music at a fraction less than
one penny per number, and when you stretch it
into 26 at the same figure you are getting a musi-
cal “ blow-out ” for less than a half-penny per
plateful! All the members of the 00, suffered.
Mr. Cunningham had to give a trifle more—and
Mr. Marsh, who sang the “Toreador” song as
only he can sing it, made it the last number but
one of the programme, and wrestled with the
same clamor at even that late hour. Even Herr
Vollmar, with the unpopular ’cello, got a recall,
and altogether the Esty-Marsh Co. wound up
amidst a scene of much enthusiasm. To-night
(Saturday) Mr. Weir (tenor) and la femme incom-
jprise—Madame Enriquez—are to appear at the
musical Garden of Eden.
This month finishes like a pawnbroker, in a
festive sense, for three balls are billed in it. Tempe
ball committee has wisely altered the date of
its popular fixture in Sydney Town Hall to 30th
inst., as on the first date (24th) wild politi-
cians will have the town by the throat in a general
election. Against the attractions of a first-class
poultry-supper donated by the committee ladies,
and which I am requested to particularise, change
of date (generally rather superstitiously feared by
ball committees) has little power. By the way,
Mrs. Prendergast and Mrs. Brennan are hon.
secs. The annual Masonic ball, which assumes a
gorgeous aspect now-a-days, comes off on 31st
inst., at the Town Hall, and will take some dress-
ing for on the women’s part, for, when the lords
of creation are trimmed with silk and jewellery,
the women have a task to avoid being snuffed out.
Messrs. G. H. Marsh and F. Harvey have most of
the worry getting the A. M. ball staged. The
third dance is provoked by the East Sydney
Rowing Club—the aquatic youths one sees spurt-
ing about the harbor, apparently equipped for the
. burlesque stage, in sleeveless jerseys. Their ball
is at West’s rooms on the 19th.
Acting-Gov. Darley has begun to spend part of
the V.R. screw in society entertainments. His
first festivity after taking up residence at
Guvmentouse was a dinner to mixed politicians
and pastoralists. It doesn’t apparently require a
genius to fill the Y.R. shoes—at any rate H.E.
evidently doesn’t mean to get out of the conven-
tional groove, judging by his invitation-list. Same
old “representative” mob with no alleviating
fringe to it; seemingly “ Wellington to meet Nel-
son,” and vice-versa. Really, history is merely a
circumstance in the repetition line compared with
Australian upper-crust tea-parties. But still
these stereotyped alleged entertainments coat
money. In Lord Jersey’s latest letter to Parkes,
which the handy press-reporter was just in the
nick of time to see, he says that “ filling the posi-
tion of Governor had cost him .£7OOO per annum ”
(understood to mean over and above his salary.)
Vice-royalty, by the way, has a habit of moaning
in the distance over its receipted caterer’s bills
that ought to be decidedly embarrassing to the
former recipients of its hospitality.
Madame Henry’s last reunion musicale of the
Cerole Frangais, at her rooms, Pitt-street, near
Park-street, on Monday evening, was very plea-
santly arranged. The artistically-decorated room
was crowded with appreciative listeners, who
preferred the crisp farce of “ Le Parapluie”—with
Miss Agnes Mowle letter-perfect as a soubrette,
Miss King natural as Miss Gibson, and Mons. de
Yilleval absolutely starring as the lover—to the
more imposing “La Matinee d’une Etoile,” in
which Mrs. Lumsdaine performed the disillu-
sioned star with the air of one who longs to be
play-acting something fearsomely dramatic with
poison in it. Dr. Roth did all he could
to lighten the atmosphere with a touch-and-go
sketch of “un offloier” (his exit being the signal
for much applause), but his comedy was over-
whelmed. However, both pieces went brightly
enough. Madame Henry (who looked regal in a
black velvet bodice striped with gold lines over a
black silk skirt trimmed with jet), besides teach-
ing a very charming accent to her cercle, chooses
piquant little plays for them which one hears
nowhere else.
It seems that Sydney has not seen the last of
the popular Esty-Marsh concert co., now trying
its luck again in Melbourne. Mr. J. E. Sykes,
the genial and well-known Sydney musician,
has arranged for a series of seven operatic
recitals in the Centenary Hall, beginning on
Saturday, August 3rd. Three old favorites and
two operas new to Australia—" Gabrielle ” and
“ Ivanhoe”—will be exploited in excerpt form,
and subscription tickets for the course (and trans-
ferable) will be half-a-guinea—a low fee for hear-
ing these distinguished artistes in the very best
portions of their repertoire. And, talking of high-
class music, Clifford Halle and Miss Ribbentrop
are billed to open at the Y.M.O.A. Hall to-night
(Thursday).
The last piece of Sydney hospitality offered to
the Gaiety Co. en masse was a midnight trip to
Sydney Observatory to count the twinkle-twinkles
through the big telescope. Stars looking at stars
and winking at one another ! Venus counting
the spots on Venus with the unfair advantage of a
strong spy-glass ! What an apt festivity !
Another cockatoo-story. An Australian matron
possesses a bird which she has trained to call out
“ mother !” in the most realistic manner possible,
with all the nuances of a spoilt child howling for
its parent. One day a visitor called j and th«
cockatoo lady was surprised to see her guqst get-
ting more and more uncomfortable as the visit
progressed, until at last she rose abruptly to take
sudden leave. In the hall they met pretty
cooky, screaming as usual, whereupon the visitor’s
mien altered on the instant. “Oh,” she said,
with a relieved expression, “I thought it was your
poor little child crying for you all the time, and I
wondered how you could sit there 1”
In a recent scandal concerning a doctor and
a barmaid, the allegedly-injured wife was, so
goes the story, told by her husband that ho
intended marrying Flossie if adivoreeoame about.
“ Humbug !” said she. “ How can a medical man
afford to fly in the face of society ? The day I
leave you sees you a ruined man. My dear sir,
please remember that you cannot afford the luxury
of a barmaid for a wife.”
Which strikes me as being the most business*
like reply on record under the circumstances. Nc
tears, no shrieks, no reproaches—simply cold
horse-sense. And yet it Was possibly a long
course of common-sense, unrelieved by a ray of
emotional weakness, which sent the erring doctor
on a voyage of discovery after a less stoical female
companion.
“It isn’t asking much,” chirrups DecimaMoore,
in the “Gaiety Girl,” “that everyone’s a lady
who behaves herself as such ”—though opinions
differ as to what constitutes a perfect lady and a
vulgar person, nowadays. At a recent M.L.
fancy fair, I’m told, the art stall was run by a
number of haw-haw young women in the attire of
Greek maidens. To.these pets of society came a
male outsider vvho, elated by winning a picture,
offered to act as whipper-in to his brother out-
siders. His offer was ardently accepted with
many thanks for his kaindness, don’toherknow,
and the raffle business boomed, and the classic
damsels were so condescendingly gracious that the
galoot thought his social fortune was made. The
idea so inflated him that at parting he proffered
his paw to the presiding genius, and so brought
about his downfall. Drawing herself up, “No,
thank you,” she said, icily, “ I really don’t know
you !” Evidently the vice-regal example of indis-
criminate pump-handling is thrown away on the
uppercrust in the land of the moa.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Samoa has just held its first cotoner’s
inquest. The world moves. It is not so very
long ago that the practice of holding coroners’
inquests would have interfered considerably with
the fresh-meat supply of the Samoan chiefs.
At Liverpool (Eng.) recently, a Crimean
veteran, aged over 70 years, found a shelter for
his old, ignoble bones in the workhouse. At next
meeting of the “vestry,” or Board of Guardians,
or whatever the authority is called, it was seriously
proposed to eject the ancient soldier on the
ground that “it wasn’t right to encourage that
sort of people.” The workhouse gruel isn’t a
very lavish form of encouragement, but even that
was regarded as excessive. Next time The Bul-
letin hears Charles Arnold singing the joyous
song of “ Tommy Atkins ” —especially that part
where he speaks of T.A. as his country’s special
pride—it will think of that Liverpool Board if
Guardians, and the battered hero in the work
house who ought not to be “ encouraged.”
Phil May gave an ex-
hibition of his drawings in
London, the other day. He
intended to welcome pat-
rons with a bashful, but
not too bashful smile (like
this), when, as recited in a
recent Bulletin, he was
thrown from his horse and
broke his jaw. The humor-
ous account says that
“ Mr. May was not even
riding at a gallop at the
time of the accident, but
was walking along quietly
when his horse shied at a
newspaper.” If the newspaper was an old Bulle-
tin containing Hop’s picture of Phil May on
horseback, the incident would be intelligible.
At time of writing the bailiff siege of
Sydney Truth still continues. All sorts of
dodges are said to have been tried to gain admis-
sion. The other day a well-dressed man went to
the office and interviewed editor Taylor through
the bars, saying he wanted to have a little
private conversation—would Taylor let him in for
a few minutes? He was an intending candidate
for Parliament and wished to consult the editor
about his election-address. “ Very sorry,” said
Taylor, ‘‘to hear that you have been in gaol.
However, the cleverest of us are found out now
and then.” “But I haven’t been in gaol,”
shrieked the suspected bailiff. “Goulburn
gaol, did you say? ” said Taylor—"it’s a deuced
cold place in winter.” Then the “candidate,”
who had begun to froth at the mouth, was led
away by his friends. Here is a slab from editor
Taylor’s solemn-warning (apropos of an alleged
threatened battering-ram assault at his offict-
door)
“Our .house is our oastle,” and we will riddle with
bullets every man that behaves as a burglar and attempts
to break in. To the officers of the law, acting under
lawful process, we offer no active resistance ; but the first
private detective, or detective’s bravo, that puts unlawful
hands on our castle will sleep with his fathers— pour
encourager les autres. P.S.-The Staff will meet for
Revolver Drill after Church Parade to-morrow.—By
order. J
As Taylor declines to endorse any arrangement
which would still leave to plaintiff the right of
putting defendant Willis into the debtors’ prison
for a year, negotiations for a settlement have
failed.

Personal Items.
Old man Parkes’s wife, sad to relate, is
dying of cancer.
N.S. Wales should forcibly administer the
teetotal pledge to several of her stipendiary
magistrates.
Macnamara, the lately-released N.S.W.
banking prisoner, has been operated on for epi-
thelial lip-cancer.
General Sir Andrew Clarke, who has been
drawing a big pension from Victoria for 40 years,
will be 71 on J uly 27.
Photo. by Tuttle.
Mr. J. CONLON, Champion Bowler.
Theo. Fink, M.L.A. (Vic.), “cycles” now
“as a relief from politics.” Rotatory motion in
■ each case—but the cyclist does, at least, progress.
It is not generally known that Premier
Reid js a brother-in-law of “Jimmy” Ewan, of
the firm of John Fraser and Co. —Mr. Ewan
having married Miss Reid.
That clever little Sydney girl, Louise M.
Mack, well-known to Bulletin readers both as a
poet and as a writer of short stories, has had a
novel accepted by Fisher Unwin.
Baron Yon Mueller, who celebrated the
70th anniversary of his birth on Sunday, July 1,
spent the earliest portion of his Australian career
as a chemist’s assistant in Adelaide.
Two popular skippers on the Howard
Smith s.s. line are named Joy and Pain. Not only
in name are they the two extremes, for one is
short and stout, while the other is tall and thin.
Murderer Bertrand, at latest, was in
America, studying modern improvements in den-
tistry. As it is 28 years since he practised his
profession, theonce-skilful Bertrand has a lot to
learn.
Had Oscar Wilde got off he meant to come
to Australia with one of the Douglases, and live
“somewhere in trie wild bush ” until the memory
of his exploits had faded. But it was ordered
otherwise.
Henniker Heaton has again been returned
unopposed for Canterbury, England, but the
general Australisn expectation that he would be
the new British Premier seems to have been dis-
appointed.
In his first public speech at Adelaide,
Bishop Harmer used the personal pronoun “ I ”
59 times in three-quarters of a column of S.A.
Advertiser. The reporter magnanimously sliced
some of them out, too.
Miss Melb. Punch Murphy has been made
a member of the British Society of Authors. The
Austral Salon confidently expects that she will be
presented to Queen Victoria before long as '“the
first Australian lady journalist.”
“Jim” Brunker, son of the N.S. Wales
Colonial-Secretary, has been asked to contest Dur-
ham with H. H. Brown. Once, when the matter
was mentioned to Brunker, sen., he replied: “One
fool in the family is quite enough.”
The next Australian knight, if he can
manage it, will be the pachydermatously humor-
less John Hotson, of Newport (Vic.) freezing
works, who has now apparently frozen himself
firmly on to the ear of the cableman.
Mrs. Caple has just died in Melb., aged
92. She was the widow of the Oollins-street book-
seller, precursor of Mullen and Robertson. Coppin
was in Caple’s shop when the news came of the
drowning of G. V. Brooke, in the London.
A N.S.W. legal luminary has (perhaps for
ever) renounced spiritualism. A very fashionable
coterie of spiritualasses lately invited him to
illuminate their circle with his hard smile, but he
declined. His reply is said to have been a
stinger.
Judge Docker (N.S.W.) is cynical enough
to be the proprietor of a “ record ” liver. The
other day he gave a prisoner five years’ gaol for a
big larceny. “You might as well sentence me for
life,” exclaimed the astonished thief. “I can’t,”
snuffled the Judge.
Hori Grey’s father was killed at Badajoz,
in the Peninsular War, in 1812. Grey himself
began his successful and marvellously-plucky sur-
vey of the Westralian coast in ’37, and he was
gazetted Governor of S.A. in Dec. ’4O. He has
surely earned rest.
Australia’s smallest policeman is on view
at Melb. Public Library. He recalls the little
old man who had the same post years ago, and
who single-handed captured the notorious “Capt.”
Melville. The captain was supposed to be armed
to the teeth. The little policeman waited for him
round a corner, tripped him up, and handcuffed
him in short order.
Mr. Wallace, the Australian chess-cham-
pion, played much more strongly in Melbourne
against Esling than he did some 18 months ago in
Sydney with ex-champion Crane, He is now to
visit Adelaide (S.A.), where, in the modest retired-
champion, Henry Charlick, a tough opponent
could be found for him.
The one-armed and hard-up Michael
Davitt, like the whole-souled man he is, will
devote the entire proceeds of his future Australian
lectures and meetings to supporting the election
fund of the Irish party. It is pathetic that such
sacrifices should be accepted at the hands of a man
with so splendid a record of self-abnegation and
suffering.
Since last year, and partly by the develop-
ments in the Dean case, Premier Kingston has
been converted into an opponent of capital punish-
ment. He lately spoke in S.A. Assembly in
favor of its abolition. It is unfortunate, however,
that the S.A. Labor-member who moves for the
abolition of the gallows also agitates for the
abolition of the Parliamentary refreshment-bar.
Sydneyites have sniggered vastly at the
Review of Reviews’ history oh N.S.W. Civil
Service Commission, prefaced by a gigantic por-
trait of the wild-eyed Mr. Percy R. Meggy, and
including a microscopic picture of that utterly un-
important person, Secretary Teece, of the trumpery
little A.M.P. Society. Editor Fitchett seems to
have a big grip of the relative sizes of things.
Matthew Davies, who is blossoming out
again as a social personage, now resides in Joli-
monb, and goes to church in a silk hat of brilliant
shinyness, frock coat of faultless cut, yellow dog-
skin gloves harmonising with his beard, and
patent-leather boots. The ever-eomely Matthew
is a vision of loveliness, and it is doubtful whether
any one of his late creditors cuts so impressive an
appearance.
Agent-General Playford lately enquired of
Lord Ripon “if it was necessary to appear in
Windsor uniform ’’at the Queen’s Birthday recep-
tion. Answer was “Yes,” and “ Honest Tom ”
didn’t go. “ They’ll have me in evening-dress or
not at all,” said Tom, some time ago, and he
seems to stick to it. At British official receptions
the most noticeable man is the American Minister,
who always wears plain evening-dress.
Mr. M. J. Conlon, the winner of the
N.S.W. bowling championship and gold medal
for ’94-5, is a native of Wollongong. Twice
previously he won the championship—in ’9l-2 and
’92-3 —and twice he was in the semi-final contest.
His career of triumph started long time ago.
He was runner-up and winner of second prize in
the “John Dawson trophy” matches, ’B5 ; winner
Glebe president’s trophy in ’9O; runner-up for
interprovineial champion medal, Victoria v. N.S.
Wales, ’B9 ; was one of the captains of the Glebe
club, which won the valuable trophy presented by

Mr. John Young (president of N.S.W. Bowling
Association); was one of the captains who won
the “ Crawford Shield ” for the Glebe club, ’9l-2 ;
captain of first champion rink against all clubs,
which won for the Glebe club in ’94; winner of
the Redfern president’s trophy, ’95 ; and was the
most successful captain in interprovineial matches
for N.S.W. against Victoria and Maoriland. Mr.
Conlon is a modest man, and wears his honors
worthily. For four years he was president of the
Glebe club. He now plays for Redfern.
Vic. ex-Premier Shiels has been absent from
Parliament this session by order of his doctor.
Heart-trouble is said to be the cause. His friends
fear that it will be impossible for him to take a
leading part in politics again. Shiels’ Casterton
programme was probably the most thoroughly-
comprehensive democratic bill-of-fare ever pub
forward by an Australian Premier; and his
Government ran away from it as soon as it was
enunciated.
The most exasperating variety of parson
inflicted on a suffering country is the weeping sin-
shifter. One Australian Legislative Council holds
a choice example, who used to also run a paper.
Too busy to prepare a full-time oration, he would
gently slide on to the story of two brothers, equally
gifted and well-trained, one of whom became an
utter reprobate, the other a shining light. Here
the preacher would break down completely and
blurt out the awful truth. He was the saintly
brother.
Dr. Harmer, the new S. A. Anglican
bishop, is a little man, with a clean-shaved, fat
face mounted with glasses. A plain, unimpres-
sive little fellow, who would have made his fortune
in the “Private Secretary.” The bishop’s legs
alone are a poem, especially when viewed from the
rear, and would be the especial delight of “Hop,
who has never been provided with a legitimate
successor to Pastor Jefferies. The new bishop is
a rapid speaker, and appears to have intellectual
and oratorical powers far above those of his pre-
decessor, Kennion.
Apropos the fact that comedian Wilfrid
Shine fainted in Melb. gaol immediately the un-
moved Buck walked on to the gallows to be
hanged, and so saw nothing of the actual specta-
cle : What a peculiar attraction morbid sights
appear to have for people who are too weak-nerved
to stand them ! Any regular attendant at execu-
tions will tell of the number of persons who
clamour for admission-tickets, and then, in terror,
turn their backs on the hideous show they have
taken so much trouble to attend.
G. Republican Dibbs was not always a mere
politician. In March, ’73, he was one of a party
dining at Sydney Exchange, when a man from
China began whooping up the Shanghai amateur
scullers, of whom he was on his own showing,
one of the best. Dibbs bet this gentleman,
Lindsay by nam ■, that he, a mere had-been, could
beat him for a dinner for twenty. Full as they
were of turkey and bitters, the pair and their
followers adjourned to the S.R.C. shed, borrowed
a couple of club skiffs and rowed the match round
Pinchgut, the “ long cove” winning easily.
Re Parkes’ well-known advocacy of Woman
Suffrage, the old man once presided at a large
public meeting of sympathisers in the cause, and,
having taken the various women under his wing,
gave forth an encouraging “Hear, hear,” at
frequent intervals, until one of the number, in
the manner peculiar to certain feminine orators,
became effusive on the question of sex morality,
and expressed the hope that, with the advent of
Woman Suffrage, the male politician of doubtful
morals would be completely extinguished. Then
a deep and awful silence fell upon that meeting.
