“It is not generally
known,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “that Joseph
Chamberlain, Secretary of
State for the Colonies in the
new Salisbury Government,
visited Australia nearly 40
years ago. While in Mel-
bourne he sought to take
back to England an Aus-
tralian wife. A daughter of
an eminent Sydney lawyer
(afterwards a Judge) was
then staying in Melbourne
at the house of Coroner
Youl. Mr. Chamberlain
met the fair one and was
smitten. Day after day and
night after night, for months,
he dropped in at the young
coroner’s and looked softly
through his eye-glass at the
Judge’s daughter. But after
a six months’ stay in Mel-
bourne and a look round
Sydney, Joseph returned to
England. The fair one after-
wards married a Melbourne
barrister, who deserted the
bar for the schoolroom. Mr.
Chamberlain has also mar-
ried since and married,
and married, arid his third
wife is a rich American.”
Louis Becke’s second collection of South
Sea stories will probably be issued in London in
two separate volumes in time for Xmas. Since
his “ By Reef and Palm ” appeared, London pub-
lishers have had quite a crop of South Sea yarns
offered them by various writers, none of which,
however, have been thoughtgood enough to take up.
Included in the first volume will be, with other
Bulletin stories of Becke’s, “The Cook of the
Spreeto Santo.” It will bednteresting to ,know
whether British decorum will stand the shook of
such a tale of beachcombing life’in Bully Hayes’
time.
The eye-trouble from which Trenwith,
leader of the Vic. Labor-party, has been suffering
for some months past v has become so serious that
he is now almost blind. *He is quite unable to
read print, even with a powerful glass. When he
wishes to quote a passage in Parliament, it has to
be read for him by a colleague ; and his son has to
read the newspapers to him daily. This trouble ia
likely to spoil a useful political career. Trenwith
is by far the most brainy member of his partft
and a certain first Minister of Labor, when *
Labor department shall have been established io
Victoria.
Baron Von Mueller’s presents on the
anniversary of his 70th birthday included enough
flowers to stock a florist’s, besides cakes, pin-
cushions, photos., books, and—a pet weakness of
his —a knitted comforter. The Baron is never seen
without at least two of them. He complained of
bad sore throat the day after : he had talked with-
out intermission from 8 a.m. till 11 p.m. He will
often spend an hour explaining in careful detail
why he hasn’t time to talk to you. Along with
these weaknesses the veteran scientist has perhaps
done more lo help Australian talent to the front,
and said less about it, than any other man.

Premier Seddon, of Maoriland, on a recent
visit to the West Coast, was waited on by a depu-
tation of stone-breakers, who complained that
their heaps of road - metal were not properly
measured by the official in charge. In proof
whereof they asked Seddon to come and measure
them himself. Thereupon the Premier went forth
through a drizzling rain with a tape measure in
his capacious red hand, accompanied by the
resident engineer and the road overseer and the
oldest inhabitant, and followed by all the muddy
small boys of the town, and personally measured
about two dozen heaps of road-metal. Then the
deputation expressed its thanks in a few well-
chosen remarks.
An influential N.S. W. politician, was in
the habit of borrowing from the late Judge
Josephson, at whose house he would present him-
self periodically to “square the bill” —with
another bill. “ But you have given me a bill for
.£3OO, and the amount is only a little over £200,”
tiie Judge would say. “Ob, never mind,” the
politician would retort, “ you can just give me a
cheque for the difference.” And so things would
go on until the Judge would say—at least, he did
say once, to his own cost —“ How on earth do we
stand ? Do you owe me money, or do I owe you
money?” “I’m blessed if I know,” replied the
great statesman, calmly, “but I suppose we’d
better call it level.” And so the roller was passed
over ,£BOO. Credat Judaeus!
The Abrahams quartette of Melb. gun-
forgers consists of Emanuel, about 30 years
of age, and Louis, Edward and Alfred,
ranging from 25 to 20; the three latter are so
strikingly alike as to be almost undistinguishable
when apart. The present writer was in court
during the whole trial of JO days and could not
now positively identify any of this trio. Indeed,
it is asserted that the contractor who built the
Abrahams mansion in Drummond-st., Carlton, had
to go without a number of his “extras” owing
to his inability to positively swear which of
the brothers ordered them. Physically, the
Abrahams are miserable little rats, all about
the same height (sft.) and build (Bat.), but, with
the exception of Emanuel, there is nothing strik-
ingly Semitic in their appearance.
William Creswell, Parramatta Asylum
insane patient, believed by many to be the real
Roger Tichborne, was well-known in the neigh-
borhood of Queanbeyan, N.S.W., nearly 30 years
ago. Even then there were vague rumors as to his
identity vyith the rauoh-talked-of baronet. He
married an hotel-keeper at Gundaroo, but the
marriage does not appear to have been a happy
one. Mrs. C., whose business had, been fairly
prosperous, once deputed her lord and master to
go to Sydney and make some necessary purchases,
entrusting him with a cheque for <£7oo for that
purpose. After being away for more than a fort-
night, and having bad a high old time while the
money lasted, he returned with nothing more to
show for his trip than a 15s. bonnet,, which he
tendered as a solatium to the outraged feelings of
his expectant better-half.

POLITICAL POINTS
“ Mirams made Berry.”— Melb. paper.
What a lifetime since Graham Berry
visited Melb. Trades Hall !
“ Prendergast and his respectable frock-
coaterie. ’ ’ — Melb. Trades-hall jibe.
6267 women have votes for S.A. Leg.
Council; and 53,603 for the Assembly.
Premier Kingston has sent a letter of
sympathy and encouragement to Premier Reid, for
whose success- against the Council he publicly
prays.
It is well to remember that the genesis of
the Parkesian Federal movement was the appear-
ance of a Protectionist majority in N.S.W.
Assembly.
One N.S.W. country candidate says frankly
he endorses the Council’s action in rejecting the
Land Tax Bill, because he is himself the possessor
of 4000 acres.
The Age rejoices over the alleged fact
that Socialism makes very little progress in Vic-
toria. It persistently refuses to report Socialist
meetings, ignoring both the local Fabian Society
and the Social Democratic Federation.
Melb. Chamber of Commerce considers
that Eight Hours’ Day should not be a public
holiday, although it “sympathises with the sub-
ject of the celebration.” Touching sympathy !
What big teeth you have, grandmother !
Reading a eulogistic note on Graham
Berry, in a Melb. Tory print, one reflects again
on all the last general election meant for him,
when he chucked his “ Sir ” into the fire, and
picked it out again—like Mrs. Ebbsmith with her
Bible.
The new Maoriland Immigration Bill pro-
hibits the importation of labor unless workmen
are unobtainable in the province ; and raises the
poll-tax on Asiatics to £lOO, while decreasing the
number in proportion to tonnage which a vessel
may bring.
Maoriland House of Representatives threw
out the Bill providing for a lottery of the B.N.Z.’s
estates by a majority of nearly four to one. The
pious element of the House had its eye partly on
the sinfulness of the proposal and partly on the
fact that all such lotteries have been a dead
failure of late years. Nothing aggravates the
good Presbyterian so much as being invited to
commit a sin which won’t pay.
Evolution and amalga-
mation in N.S. W. The Free-
trade party. The Labor
party. The Freetrade Labor
party. The Free Labor
Trade party.
A pious deputation,
headed by Bishop Goe, asked
Premier Turner for the re-
ferendum in order that the
opinion of the Vic. people
may be ascertained in re
Bible reading in State schools.
An extraordinary request
coming from a party that
declares the secular system
has already destroyed the
moral strength of the com-
munity. A verdict for the
continuance of the present
eystem would only be re-
garded by the parsons as a
melancholy demonstration of
its evil influence upon the
minds and morals of the
people.
Premier Nelson styles
the Q. Labor-party a faction
because it won’t follow the
“constitutional ” method of
unity with the Opposition,
and appoint a common leader
to confer with the Premier.
The incident is but one more
sign of the early break-up of
Government by Contradic-
tion. Browne, M.P., found
this as a definition of “ fac-
tion”:—“Any party which
abuses the power and public
authority with which it has
been entrusted.” The con-
cluding steps of the syllo-
gism were absurdly simple.
All Oppositionist Powers’ ability doesn’t
seem able to change the popular belief, well or ill
founded, that he lacks grit, “ backbone.” It is
this feeling which gave point to Murray’s
suggestion of an Upper House of ladies, with
Powers as president.
Parkes, though he has hitherto uniformly
gathered votes from diverse interests, will be
beaten badly by Reid, despite all the money which
will be spent in aid of the old man. Against him
will be arrayed, amongst other things, an almost
solid publicans’ vote. Many publicans are Pro-
tectionists, but ju«t as “ we are all Socialists,” so,
in matters directly affecting our livelihood, we are
all individualists. Reid has ever been the publi-
cans’ pet ; Parkes has uniformly toyed with the
teetotal saints, and, strange to say, even with the
social-purity brigade.
One good point about Premier Reid : he
is amenable to “ reason.” When you sing out to
him, “ Gee up, Bawley !” he sometimes fails to
respond instantaneously, but once you flourish the
bullock-whip in earnest, and knock a bit of hide
off him, he comes right up to yoke and pulls “ like
a little horse.”
N.S.W. Premier Reid, it may safely be
asserted, is far and away the best platform—
“mob”—orator in Australia. Not only this, but
he has of late exhibited so much astuteness of the
Parkesian order, that his veteran rival must now
feel himself in the position of the eagle stricken
by the arrow feathered from his own wing.
Brisbane Courier :
For another thing, the N.S. W. law which makes attempt
to murder a capital offence should be revised. Generally
to make attempt equal to completion is had law, and it is
especially bad where the penalty attached is irreversible.
It is certain that in the Dean case popular dissatisfaction
was aggravated by the fact that death punishment was
pronounced on a man for a living woman.
There are nearly 700 Japs in Torres Straits
and 200 more are coming. They run the boarding-
houses, ship-building slips, stores (about 15),
laundries, and almost monopolise and are ruining
the pearling industry. But there is no hope of
remedy, as powerful Q. politicians are intimately
related to people who are body and soul bound up
with the J ap system.

The row in the Victorian woollen-tiade
has produced one very important admission, viz.,
that thirty-odd qualities of flannel are manufac-
tured locally. “One of the chief causes of the
comparative failure of the woollen industry of
Victoria in the past has been the attempt of the
country mills to manufacture tweeds and every
class of flannels,” says Rawson, M.L.A.
Great Britain has paid off £108,000,000
of her national debt in the last 14 years, and has
reduced the liability of her people by £5 3s. 6d.
per head. All grovellers who think this country
should do as England does are requested to con-
sider these figures. But, unluckily, the Australian
groveller wants to follow England in everything
save the one matter where her 'example is most
worth following. In this one direction some sound,
reliable grovel would do a lot of good.

roads, &c. Hitherto the department has bei n in

It is goodbye to George Reid’s chances of a K.C.M.G.
after his injudicious and mistaken cablegram to fiipon.
After all, there is a certain amount of courtesy observed
in official life in this country, and people are not accus-

wonder why Mrs. Reid allowed him to commit such a
be Use.
There you have it. The English official creed is—

price is a title.
Ex-Republican Dibbs’ statement that the
British Govt, would, as likely as not, refuse to
pass an Enabling Bill for the amendment of the
N.S.W. Constitution, is the puny, grovelling cry
of a voice lost in an electioneering wilderness.
Britain is now—worse luck !-very wide awake as
to the value of the Australian connection. The
ultimate victories of the Chinese Restriction Act
and the Marriage with a Deceased Wife’s Sister
Act., &c., &0., are instances, also, that should
discourage even the blundering Dibbs from further
acts of Imperial worship.

The Q. Early-Closing Bill won’t apply to
chemists, confectioners, fish, vegetable, fruit and
refreshment vendors, restaurants, tobacconists,
booksellers, hairdressers, undertakers, or pubs.
Also, shops where there are no employes other
than members of the tradesman’s family are
excepted. Further, the Bill only works by local
option, and it won’t come into force in any dis-
trict unless 200 electors sign a petition in its
favor ; and this petition may be overset if a poll is
demanded by 200 other electors. With so many
exemptions, and so many difficulties put in the
way of enforcing the Act, the measure is plainly
a dead letter. The Govt., indeed, never intended
it otherwise; but it is thought the pretence of
popular legislation will catch some foolish votes
for the election.
Premier Kingston has written the Colonial
Office very plainly re vice-regal salaries and
emoluments. ,£5OOO a year, he remarks, is too
much to pay the Governor of S. A., who, even if
he is lavishly hospitable, only entertains “dis-
tinguished visitors,” who don’t need attention,
and the so-called “Society” of Adelaide, which
comprises but a very small section of the general
community. The democracy’s ideal should be to
find and elect from among its own ranks a
Governor who, like (say) George Higinbotham,
would, by reason of his obvious innate nobility,
cub a commanding figure if he lived in a four-
roomed cottage, and who would look dignified
even while publicly buying a sixpenny leg of
mutton and cramming it into a rusty black bag.
Dear Bulletin, —Be “the people and
business.” the alleged failure of democracies
through indifference and soulless negligence just
as much to them living generally in a climate
favorable to development of large and sluggish
livers? I notice that when the new-chum from
Britain, Canada or any of the cold climates takes
hold of any work or business in Australia he is far
and away more energetic and pushing than our
own people, whose character! die attitude, as
Miss Floral Shaw said, is “leaning against
a post. ” Once the cold-country man is properly
acclimatised his energy disappears, and he becomes
“one of us.” Democracy would seem hopelessly
unable to run State or any other business until
some saviour of his country shall “arise,” and,
after making due provisions for the after-effects,
start forth on a mission to the citizens of this
glorious land, armed with many tons of over-
proof liver-tonic. —Source.
If Premier Reid comes back to power, the
Bill for the reformation of N.S. W. Council may,
with energy and good luck, and assuming that the
tail of the fatted nominees is considerably down and
there is very little fight in them, pass by the end
oftheyear. It may takelS months or five years’ hard
fighting,, but it is quite safe to assert that it won’t
pass before the beginning of ’96. Then, assuming
that the existing Councillors stand to their guns
in the matter of the Land and Income Tax, and
that all the newly-appointed Councillors are
favorable to the measure and continue so, the
requisite majority to pass the Land and Income
Tax Bill should be available some time in ’9B,
and, allowing for the time necessary to get the
machinery in working order, the tax should be
collected for the first time during the financial
year ending 30th June, ’99. If there is a long
fight over the Reform Bill, and the old Council-
lors have any real grit in them, it may be a year
later. And if the new democratic nominees
petrify in the atmosphere of Eroperty, as almost
all previous democrats have done, then it may be
at any date which is absolutely and hopelessly
indefinite. However, all these difficulties must be
faced sooner or later, and Reid deserves some
credit for trying to turn later into sooner.

For th « ime son ‘ e stations out m the
far west (N.S. W.) pay under 20s. a hundred for
shearing. For this season, however, they offer
“perks” which will bring the price up to over
a pound. Some of them will do anything to get
men to sign at, say, 17s. ; and they are meeting
with no difficulty—stands are being over-applied
for all round. At Momba and other stations
where local men made (or were given) trouble last
year all the stands are given to imported men.
The first batch brought up came from the Geelong
district. The first shed to start under conference
agreement was Tarella (Quin, Currie & Co.’s).

“ I was swimming in the creek last week,
when I spotted a big black snake, his head out of
one leg of my pants and his tail out of the top
end. When I objected, he took his tail in his
mouth, bowled off like a hoop, and me after him
for miles—but it was no go. I had to wait until
after dark to get into the town.” The latest,
from a Bulletin very special correspondent.
Since Louis Becke’s South Sea stories ap-
peared in The Bulletin, he has received batches
of letters from all sorts of people, who want to go
down to the islands and catch the wild goat by
the beard and investigate the deplorably bad con-
dition of local morals. Some of these letters are
from young men; but most are from old married
men. One man says that he has a new cocoanut-
dessieator, whioh he would either give in return
for passage or show natives how to work ; another
only wants to get to some quiet spot where he can
get some light, agreeable employment (lying in a
hammock, eating squashy fruit, with a native girl
to brush off the flies, is this man’s “dart ”). An-
other man has “ a good deal of household furni-
ture, bed-linen, &c., and a lot of rough experience,
and thinks the life would suit him ” (what has
he done with his wife?). Two brothers in N.Q.
are willing to do “any darned thing”—would
prefer to go in for cattle-breeding there (Becke
says that some of the coral atolls are so narrow
that a cow sniffing the sea breeze on the ocean
side would have her tail hanging over the glassy
surface of the lagoon on the other side); and then
one pious gentleman thinks that, as he once filled
a position of trust in a mercantile house at Per-
nambuco, where the temptations (what ki.nd,_
white or brown ?) were very numerous, he would 1
like similar employment in Tahiti or Samoa
Becke, being unable to answer all these queries, is
referring the writers to the missionary head-
quarters in Sydney.
John Orozier was showing a visitor round
St. AlbanV one day and they came to a paddock
where the Alderney cattle were grazing. “John”
opened the gate and was proceeding to describe
the succulent properties of the prairje grass and
what butter it made, when a low bellow inter
rupted him. On turning round he beheld his bull
coming for him ! How “ John ” ever reached th>
fence he never knew ; but the next day, armec
with a gun, he went down and shot the animal
remarking : “ I’ll show that bull who is th#
proprietor of St. Alban’s !” Bull worth £5O ; Johr
worth £lOO,OOO ; no match !


RE RETURNS.
RETURNS Will Not be allowed for if sent
in after the Month following date of publi-
cation, that is, May returns are to reach this
office not later than June, and those for June, in
their turn, not later than July.
Heading and date over front cartoon will be a
sufficient voucher for each return.
This course is necessary in the interests of the
paper; without a fixed system it is impossible
to control sales.
Please note that no divergence from the above
rule will be allowed.
Unsold copies allowed for to the extent of Ten
per cent, only of the number supplied.
WM. MACLEOD,
Managing Director,
Bulletin Newspaper Co,, Ld.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co.
(Melb.) shows a profit of £305 for the half-year ended
June 30, and pays no div. The profits for the previous
half-year were £2265, and for the one ended June, '94,
£2287, so that the apparent falling-off is something
serious. It is, however, more apparent than real. In the
boom days, when everybody had “confidence,” it was
the company’s custom, when an estate was placed in its
Hands for realisation, to take credit in advance for com-
mission on the entire amount. Realisation, however,
proceeds so slowly nowadays that it has decided to swear
off this unsafe practice, and only take credit for commis-
sion on amounts actually realised. This should put the co.
in a much safer position, though it means a serious reduc-
tion in the visible profitsfor a little while. In other respects
things only look moderately well. Compared to the state
of things a year ago, rents received have declined by
£256, or a fall of 40 per cent., and interest receipts have
fallen off by £77, and against this total expenses have
increased by £42. Office expenses, in particular, have
increased by £145. A year ago the company had
a mortgage of £35,000 on its property, and a similar sum
set aside to pay it off on maturity, and the difference in
the interest paid and that received resulted in a loss of
£320 for the six months. The mortgage is now reduced
to £25,000, but instead of the difference diminishing it
has increased to £462. The investments (£107,253), mostly
in 4 per cent. Govt, stock, debentures,/fixed deposits
and “mortgages and other advances,” only yield 3J per
cent, per annum, which seems to suggest that some of the
advances are paying no interest whatever. There appears
among the investments “set aside to meet mortgage on
freehold property £35,000,” but where this money
is, or what securities there are to represent it, is
not explained. And, though the co. has £65,000 worth
of freehold property, its half-year’s income from “ rent ”
is only .£376, while its outgo for “rent and taxes” is
£577, The £65,000 worth of property thus represents a
dead loss of £2Ol, instead of an income. A somewhat
similar state of things was shown on two previous half-
years. Also, it excites a little passing interest that the
co. should have set aside £3s,ooo—which is invested in
some way totally unexplained—to meet a £35,000 mort-
gage, and should have paid off £lO,OOO of the mortgage
without, so far as the balance-sheet shows, any way
reducing the amount alleged to be set aside. Altogether,
the Trustees, Executors and Agency Co.’s sheet is not so
full of information as it might be.
* * * *
That exceedingly hopeless concern, the Free-
hold Investment and Banking Co. (Melb.), has yet another
reconstruction scheme on hand. Its main features are
that £5 per share of the original co.’s capital, and all its
reserve funds, be written off as lost, which goes without
saying, as the fact that they are lost is already apparent.
A new co. with a nominal capital of £500,000 is to be
formed out of the debris of the old shareholders—those
who are not in the Benevolent Asylum, or sleeping out
beside the Yarra, or gone mad, or suicided, or missing ;
and the creditors are to he paid with the new co.’s deposit-
receipts, bearing interest at 5 per cent., to the amount of
about £1,300,000. As the 00. has no present means of
paying the said interest—the interest would amount
to at least £60,000 a year, .and last year’s income,
apart from calls, did little more than cover ex-
penses—the 5 per cent, is to be provided, not in
cash, but in new deposit-receipts, so that the bank’s
liabilities will multiply alarmingly. The proposal looks
too hopeless to be worth serious consideration. If the
interest on the deposit-receipts were fixed at 1J per cent,
there would at least be a prospect of some good result.
The present suggestion only amounts to this: The
creditors may be induced, by the very remote prospect
of payment in full with 5 per cent, interest, to accept the
arrangement. And, as the said arrangement authorises
the co. to owe all its debts indefinitely, and to pay the
interest on its paper by issuing more paper, and to owe it
indefinitely also, the pressure of calls will be relaxed,
and the bank may keep on owing its debts comfortably
until further notice. There is an atmosphere of calm and
profound rest about the scheme, but that seems to be
nearly all there is about it.

The full text of the City of Melbourne Bank’s
second reconstruction scheme varies in just one particular
from the cabled report, the difference being that both the
31 and the P er cent, stock are redeemable only at the
bank’s option, on six months’ notice. The City of Mel-
bourne thus—if the scheme is adopted—gets its deposit
iponey at 2Jper cent, all round (to be raised to 3 percent,
if. profits admit), and redeemable solely at its own con-
venience. This is something like a reconstruction.

So far as The Bulletin knows, the eminent
accountant, Mr. Young, of Turquands, Young and Co,,
London, has never put foot in the "colonies,”
yet the Scotch and English creditors of the City
of Melbourne Bank are practically asked to rely on
his valuation of that institution’s Yio. assets, and on
that valuation accept much less than they bargained for
when the bank was “ born again,” How on earth can
Mr. Young, or anyone else who has never visited Victoria,
be able to appraise the value of the bank’s securities
against the overdrafts of, say, a big furniture firm, acolossal
land-boomer, a speculative stationer, an engineering
firm, a squatting co., or a huge auctioneering concern?
Amongst clients as various as these, who may or may not
be good for all they owe, it is alleged that the bank has a
huge amount outstanding. Thebank’s envoy to England in
this matter, is not, all things considered, the person
whom a shrewd board of directors would put forward to
plead their cause. Should his and Mr. Young’s scheme
"come off” the British, speculator can hereafter be
regarded as a sheep in wolf’s clothing.

Great Britain’s loans are issued on the simple
basis that the State can repay any time it likes after a
jertain number of years, but the lender can never demand
repayment. Consequently, when times are good, the
mother country does a conversion operation and reduces
the rale of interest, and at other times she simply holds
on. Australasia issues her loans repayable at fixed
dates, and when they f ill due she has to renew, no matter
how disastrous the op -ration may be. The renewal of
one £2,500,000 loan, which fell due at a time of great dis-
tress, cost Queensland just £300.000. Next year, over
£6,000,000 of Australian debts fall due; £8,000,000 fall
due in 1898: 412,000,000 in 1915; £13,000,000 in 1918;
£31,000,000 in 1924 ; and £29,000,000 in 1927, with smaller
sumsiu between. If two or three of these happen to be years
of panic, the mere operation of renewal may cost this
miserable country £5,000,000. M<anvvhile, but for the
fixed date arrangement, these provinces could do a con-
version operation now which would save ±900,000 a year
in interest. Yet the alleged financiers of Australia still
adhere to the old, disastrous system-apparently from
force of habit and mental exhaustion.

The City Mutual Life Assurance Society replies
to some recent "comments : Dear Bulletin, —No bonus-
paying life office in the world, doing a large new business
in proportion to its renewal income, values on a "pure
premium” basis. The “loading” on the pure premium
averages about 25 per cent, It is the almost universal cus-
tom to allow, as commissions alone, 40 to 50 per cent, of
the first year’s premiums. It works out all right, as I have
fully explained to the Record, the next number of whioh
I trust you will have time to glance at. The City Mutual’s
standard of valuation, by the way, is stricter than that of
two of our largest and wealthiest Australian offices.
Because we w#re prudent enough to put by some money
to reserve-fund during the past couple of years, you point
out that our net rate of interest has fallen to under 5 per
cent. Do yoU know that the net rate of one very large
office last year fell to £3 155., yet they valued the bulk of
their policies at this rate? The City Mutual is working on
the safest of safe lines and has one of the soundest connec-
tions in the province.—Qko. Crowlky, Manager.

The Federal Bank, which has already called up
£1 per share, makes another call of 30s. which will
exhaust the unpaid capital. The liabilities are now
£1,254,000, and the assets are vaguely supposed to be
worth £711,000. The call should realise £240,000, but it
is expected to produce only £50,000, so the probable end
of the Federal Bank will be about 10s. or 12s. in the £. The
deficit, roughly speaking, will tot up to £600,000, inde-
pendent of the legal costs and winding-up exes. Once more
the question arises—to what end were those precious costs
incurred ? The ruined bank dissipated a lot of money in
procuring awful revelations, which set a learned Judge
sighing and shaking his head, and afterwards—Nothing.
The depositors have been compelled to sacrifice a morsel
of dividend on the altar of humbug.

• « * *
There are scores of .£5 notes (unnumbered and
unsigned) in circulation in the back-blocks of Queensland,
Westralia, and N.S.W., printed from the plates of the
long-bung Provincial and Suburban Bank, Limited. The
■writer has seen eight of them which have been forwarded
by the remote branches of several good banks for collec-
tion in Melb. Judged by the newness of the notes, and
the fact that they are unnumbered and unsigned, there
is evidently a stock of them which has been overlooked,
or the plates have not been destroyed. The Provincial
and Suburban Bank (Ltd.) was “liquidated” eight years
ago, and there are no assets left. Another reason for a
State paper currency. Nobody has to try to remember
whether the State is still in existence or not.
• * * *
In Victoria, except in Melbourne and Geelong,
all borough and shire councils have to provide for the
redemption of their loans by an annual investment of at
least 2 per cent, of the amount in Vic. Government deben-
tures. Other provinces would do well to follow this wise
policy. Every Government and local loan should have a
sinking fund attached.

An English M.P. states that 70 per cent, of
English hotels are under the thumbs of the brewers. One
hotel in Derby is bound by contract to obtain the very
sawdust for its spittoons through its proprietary brewer.
No such tyranny would be tolerated in free Australia.
N.B.—This is intended for cruel sarcasm.

Maoriland Govt, now issues postal-notes for .£1
and £5, payable at any time at any P.O. in the province.
Being absolutely free from all the usual restrictions these
notes have naturally become a State-guaranteed paper
currency, and the air is full of prophecies from many
lamentable old Tories about the dreadful evils which will
ensue through “flooding the country with paper-money,”
&c. So far, however, none of the alleged evils have pre-
sented themselves.

During the first three months of ’95 Victoria
exported 72,000 gallons of wine ; South Australia, 68,000
gallons ; N.S. Wales, just 7000 gallons. The N.S.W. trade
has doubled sinoe ’81; the Victorian trade has multiplied
about 50 times ; the S.A. trade has multiplied nearly 90
times. In ’Bl, N.S.W. had two-thirds of the export trade ;
now it has less than one-twentieth!
• ♦ * •
The Vic, Govt, is brutally candid in its reduc-
tion of the bottled-beer duties. Minister of Customs
Best says that the result of the high duty has
been decreased consumption and a heavy decline in
the revenue, and the aim of tariff revision in
the matter of malt liquor is to make the popu-
lation more boozy. It will be against the local wine in-
dustry; but what of that? The taste for wholesome wine
is only in its infancy, whereas the belief in British beer is
established, and worth encouraging for the sake of—the
revenue. However, the remission of one penny per quart
is not likely to stimulate the demand for ’Ome-brewedale
and stout. By June next year Victoria will probably
have discovered that the revenue made a stupid mistake
by which a few importers profited.

An authoritative ruling as to what constitutes
fair interest is needed in N.S.W. Justice Manning
ordered the Bank of N.S.W. to pay Mrs. Pieroy (Augusta
Dargon) 5 per cent, interest during the time it withheld
from her the £3OOO odd sweep-money. Now, 5 per cent;,
is neither the bank-rate on deposits nor the rate charged
by the bank on overdrafts. In 1834 the Legislative Coun-
cil fixed 8 per cent, as the legal figure, but right along
from that day Judges determined the rate according to
their private ideas, and now no man can tell whether in
any case the rate will be 4 per cent, orB. M’Farlane.M.P.,
had a Bill before N.S. W. Parliament on the subject, hut little
is expected to come from it. The new Bankruptcy Bill has
a better chance ; but in dealing with interest the Council
has shirked the difficulty by ingeniously devising that
where interest is part of a debt the creditor will receive
8 per cent, interest in the first instance out of the estate
without prejudice to his claiming higher interest after the
other debts proved in the estate have been paid in full.
Moses will thus get 8 per cent, to start with, and if any of
the estate survives the proved debts he may strike in for
the other 92 per cent. The question is a difficult one to
settle, but it is admitted on all hands that there are exor-
bitant rates and fairly common-sense rates, and as most
countries of Europe have fixed a rate which governs legal
decisions there is no reason why N.S.W. judicial awards
should continue to be so various and apparently hap-
hazard as they are.

The loan-bank idea, lately detailed in these
columns, spreads in the Vic. Civil Service; two more
public departments are now launching banks. As a rule,
the greatest opposition comes from the worst spongers.
In every case, the founding of the bank dooms private
borrowing.

the enemy,Jbut it is a spigot or “bung.”

Block 14 (8.H.) is adding to its concentrating
plant, with the object of dealing with the slimes which
it produces so largely. For the week ended July 6, the
mill turned out 323 tons concentrates from 1366 tons of
crude ore, and 137 tons of “first-quality” slimes. The
slimes are sent to the smelters at Port Adelaide, but their
value the reports do not disclose. The South is also
dealing with its slimes by the addition of three huddles ;
so far, it has had but one.
» * ♦ *
South (8.H.) sulphide concentrates are wonder-
fully uniform, the weekly returns, with only fractional
variations, showing 69 per cent. lead, 7 percent, zinc, and
7oz. silver per ton. Last week’s figures show that 75 per
cent, of the lead and 48 per cent, of the silver in the
crude ore was saved—a much better result than any other
local concentrators can show, the low silver grade of the
ore considered.
South concentrators’ output for half-year to
June 30 Sulphides treated, 28,775 tons, producing 9867
tons concentrates, containing 6052 tons lead, 76,8560 z.
silver, and 656 tons zinc. Also 8700 tons crude sulphides
shipped to England.

A respected pioneer of the “ Bushman’s ”

crush d. The contractors make a bare living, competi-

At the 400 ft. level on Smith’s reef, Baker’s
Cre- k mine, there is a splendid chute of gold going into
the Baker’s Creek Extended. An offer from the 8.0. Co.
to work the Extended on a 10 per cent, tribute was
declined.
» • * *
Some people who know their business have taken
a tribute from Baker’s Creek Co. to “ run ” the old slopes
of the upper levels worked in Captain Pleitner’s time.
They have a good thing, and many a plant will probably
be sprung in these slopes. Some day the shareholders
may ask the directors why the co. did not do it.

• * * ♦
The rich yields of the Great Boulder (Hannan’s)
have “knocked ’em silly.” It must have been a terrible
surprise and do as they will some of those connected
with the mine in its poverty-stricken days cannot conceal
their consternation. Listen : “In proof of the immense
richness of the Great Boulder, it has been discovered by
recent visitors who started panning the stuff taken from
an excavation, that it contains from 5 to lOdwts. pen ton.
The whole hill—the largest in the district—is composed of
this material, and is apparently saturated with gold.”

They say the machinery whteze is subterfuge, as the
development of the mine does not warrant the erection of
machinery.

Most unfortunate that the latest report re the
Wealth of Nations (unofficial) is that the reef at the 100 ft.
level is only 21ft. wide, but it is some consolation to learn
that it is rich in gold. A 21-foot reef rich in gold ought
to just about pay.

* * * ♦
Two American mining co.’s have diverted their
capital to work Coolgardie mines. The Bulletin wishes
them luck. It is quite certain they want it, for from their
locations they have poor shows. Imagine a mine whose
chief recommendation is that it is “ near Bayley’s"!
Efforts are being made by some Coolgardie men
to encourage London shareholders with the feeling that
Cashman’s Brilliant Reward is not a duffer, as the
manager for the English 00. recently reported. The fact
that some hundreds of ounces were taken from the
alluvial does not affect the main point—the seeming
barrenness of the reef, which, it was said, would average
several ounces to the ton.
• * • *
The North Coolgardie—a property acquired by a
foreign co., which owns a mine in Mexico—has been passed
out as a duffer. It was first taken on the recommendation
of a gentleman who now says: “Assays made for me were
unreliable. It is a very poor mine and won’t pay to
work.” The assavs in question went from 2J to 350 z.
♦ * * •
The size and value of the gold-bearing formation
found in Reward mine at Hannan’s will be known shortly.
“ It promises,” says the wire, “to be one of the biggest
mines of the district.” Well, there aren’t many there,
and mines sometimes commit breach-of-promise.
• * « •
A sJoz. slug found after the rain at Fly Flat,
Coolgardie. Had this ground not been turned over
again and again, by d( sperately-hard-up dry-blowers, it
would be a great specking-ground after rain. Although
it was one of the richest alluvial patches of the field, no
reefs of anv value were found on or near it.

Reported from Coolgardie that 200 men have
sneaked out of the town tor a new find in the direction of
Israelite Bay. They had not returned, therefore, says the
telegraphic expert “it is certain they are on good gold.”

The contract for the Southern Cross-Cool-
gardie railway has been given to Wilkie Bros., of Maori-
land, at ± 64,i25, or about £560 per mile. This price in-
cludes a 3ft..-6in. permanent way, with ballast, stations,
bridges, culverts and etceteras—everything except the
raiis. The Govt, engineers’ estimate was about double —
sleepers alone are expected to cost £40,000. The cheap-
ness is explained by the hig profits which will accrue to
the contractor from traffic over the sections of the line
as they are opent d up.

One prominent Vic. broker has dropped <£7ooo
over the slump in Ironclads (Marble Bar).
♦ ♦ » •
Broken Hill, situated in an almost timberless
region, is threatened with the failure of its domestic fuel-
supply. The scanty mulga scrub has hitherto kept the
town going, but none now exists within 20 miles. Still,
after being carted this distance, wood is retailed for £1
per ton. A peculiarity of the desert mulga—which differs
from the tree known by that name elsewhere in Australia
—is that it contains so little sap that the wood burns
freely in its “ green ” state.

* * * *
The “Green Harp swindle” is now an almost
forgotten legend of the Coromandel (M.L.). Many years
ago some dreadfully smart persons salted the Green Harp
olaim, and ran shares up to a high figure. When the
swindle was discovered and the awful drop came, many
people were ruined, and one man, who had dropped his
whole pile in the fraudulent hole, put an end to his
troubles by nearly sawing his head off with a carving
knife. And now the old, delusive Green Harp, re-
christened the Hauraki, is one of the richest mines in the
the district, and it seems that these bygone salters by
their smartness fooled away a big fortune.
• * • •
The Westralia Extended G.M. Co., the pros-
pectus of which appeared in Tub Bulletin of July 6, is
another Coolgardie speculation which is being offered to
the public. The co. owns 43 acres about seven miles
north from Coolgardie, alongside the Westralia Proprie-
tary and New Victoria mims. The former of these has
been sold to an English syndicate on terms that are <qual
to £32,000, and the latter has been bought by Dr. Simo i
for £40,000. Several reefs exist on the Westralia Ex-
tended property, and one, estimated to go 20z., has been
tested to a depth of 60 feet and proved so far permanent.
Others look well on the surface, but have not yet been
sunk upon. The distinctly good points about the Wes-
tralia Extended are that the owners take payment prin-
cipally in contributing shares, and that the capital
(£27,000) is very moderate.

The cold weather doesn’t seem to improve
the temper of Sydney Harbor sharks. The other
day the Sydney Customs launch dropped across a
big fellow worrying a bag containing a dead dog.
The launch steamed up to the spot, but the shark
wouldn’t quit, and when jabbed with a boat-hook
he seized it and tore it out of the coxswain’s
hands.

Dear Bulletin,—Here’s how one R.C.
missionary, at all events, made a big strike in
converts. Certain place in Malay Peninsula;
soul-competition hot between Baptist parson and
Jesuit father. Natives catch half-grown tiger-
cub, put him in pit, and pelt him till liis remon-
strances bring angry mother from jungle. Tigress
riddled; but, in excitement, Baptist native falls
into pit, and the cub gets him down and lies on
him so that to shoot one is to shoot the other.
Rev. Hardshell summoned ; but, while he is be-
seeching Gawd, at a safe distance, to help our
dear ber-ruther, Father Blank conies along, bor-
rows a kris, has himself lowered into the pit, and
gives young tiger beans—receiving in return six
weeks’ broad-of-his-hack and the accession to his
flock of all the yellow Baptists, except—the man
whose life he had saved !— Macassab.

Previous to his departure for Johannes-
burg, the other day, W. A. Alexander, a Mel-
bourne tobacconist, was entertained by the Tobac-
conists Association. The President, Harry Levy,
after exhausting his stock of eulogistic phrases,
came to a stop for a moment, and then, in a burst
of broken-voiced enthusiasm, wound up with the
statement that Mr. Alexander was “in every
respect an ideal tobacconist.” Which recalls the
deceased person over whose remains nobody could
be found to pronounce a funeral eulogium until a
German in the crowd stepped forth, and looking
down on the coffin, observed briefly—“ Ach veil 1
he vos a goot schmoker.”
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
A London correspondent of the Akgus
comments unfavorably on the Metropolitan School
Board’s desire to encourage music among the
lowah ordahs. It seems that the reckless board
intends to introduce fiddle tuition into the evening
schools, with a view to “brightening the homes of
the people,” and the Abgus man fears the worst.
“ Already, ” he says, “the experiment of teaching
the piano has proved a costly item. On the
strength of the training obtained in the board
schools, some of the elder girls, instead of continu-
ing the honest drudgery of their parents, are start-
ing in the suburbs small music classes at sixpence
a lesson.” Mark the Tory indisposition to allow
a choice of honest drudgery to these inhabitants
of a lowah sphere ! “Genteel” people may strive
to pick, up a meagre living in a field of “genteel ”
industry, but the daughters of charwomen must
“char” or follow their natural avocation of
domestic servants, otherwise the classes become
mixed and the glorious British constitution goes
to the devil. But how about the Havveisian theory
of music and morals?
The Yic. wine and spirit trade expands
despite itself. Joshua Bros., Melb. distillers,
have just secured a British army brandy contract.
A French wine expert, engaged in examining
Australian vineyards, tells The Bulletin a few
of his experiences, and expresses astonishment at
the quality of the wines, considering the amount
of ignorance expended in their preparation. His
visit to a leading Dookie (Vic.) cellar is men-
tioned. On entering he enquired the temperature,
but was told by the proprietor and manager that
“ they did not know.” “Don’t you keep a ther-
mometer?” asked the expert. “Oh, we have
plenty at the homestead,’’replied they, “but none
down here.” “Well, how do you tell when it is
the right temperature for fermentation ?’’ “Oh,”
they replied, “we put our arms in, and when the
air in the cellar is hotter than the blood we know
it is right for fermentation.” Another man, asked
for samples of his wine, sent back several bottles
half filled. When asked why they were not full
he said “the wine was so strong ho thought it
would burst the bottles.” In another place was
found a kerosene-tin, with a perforated bottom,
and a man sitting on top to squeeze juice out!
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
Dear Bulletin, — On one of the W.A.
fields the other day the miners complained to a
visiting warden that the blacks had been stealing
tucker. The warden answered thus :—“ If the
niggers steal anything from you, follow them out
to their camps, and when you find them do not be
afraid to use your firearms.” That such advice
should be given to a body of men by a warden
and magistrate when acting officially is, to say
the least of it, a bit rough on the unfortunate
niggers—when considered in connection with the
facts that they are more than half-starved, conse-
quent on their hunting-grounds being over-run by
whites ; that there was a special clause relating to
the treatment of the aborigines inserted in the
Constitution Act which gave Home Rule to
W.A. ; that a large sum of money is voted
annually for the especial benefit of the natives;
and that an officer (Imperial, I think) is paid a
good round salary to see that they get fair treat-
men t. —Republican.

Notice is hereby given that the THIRTY-FIFTH ORDINARY HALF-YEARLY MEETING
of the Shareholders of the above Company will be held in the Board-room, at the Company s Offices,
No. 412 Collins-street, on THURSDAY, 11th JULY, at 11 o’clock.
BUSINESS:
1 To receive the Directors’ Report and Balance-sheet for the Half-year ending 30th June, 1895-
2. To consider any other business that may be brought before the meeting, of which due notice
shall have been given.
REPORT.
The amount remaining at credit of profit and loss account is £3477 3s. Id., which the directors
'recommend should be carried forward to next , , , - *
The absence of a dividend calls for a few explanatory remarks Our experience heretofore has
shown that the commission received on income collected in estates, trusts, and for absentees, and the
interest on our investments, have met our expenditure, leaving the commission on the corpus of estates
to pay the dividend. In anticipation of the number and value of estates being maintained, the prac-
tice of exercising our legal "right by at once taking our profit upon the sworn value of the corpus was
instituted many years back and maintained until 1892, when the diminishing revenue from this source
Lnrl the increased difficulty of winding-up estates demonstrated the undesirability of its continuance.
By iTS di.itol it w» apprehended we would gradually be enabled, without an undue
sacrifice by the shareholders, to institute the safer practice of only carrying to credit of profit a fair
proportion of the commission, taking into consideration the time the estate would be in hand , but
though the number of estates coming into our hands has been well maintained, the further decreases in
the value of corpus and the practical difficulties in the way of early realisation have impressed upon
the Board the necessity of working on closer lines, and we decided only to take to credit in the half-
year’s profits such commission on corpus as the process of realisation absolutely justifies.
By Order of the Board.
412 Oollins-streeb, Melbourne, sth July, 1895.
JAS. BORROWMAN, Manager,
The Trustees, Executors and Agency Company Limited.

When the Duke and Duchess of York
drive out in London the crowd always yell,
“What cheer —where’s the baby?” Piinctss
May’s indifference to the Royal infant is well-
known*
The Sydney-owned whaler, Phyllis, has
been reported several times off the south coast of
Tasmania, hut so far does not appear to have
bagged many fish. Through a boat-accident at
Port Davey she lost two of her crew—colored men.
The Hobart Waterwitoh has so far taken 46 tons
on her present cruise. The Helen, the best-
equipped vessel in these seas, whose “take” for
12 months reached the respectable total of 151
tons, has not re-fitted for another cruise, owing to
some dispute between A. M'Gregor and Co., the
owners, and Capt. Folder. The latter has had
command of the barque since she was converted
into a whaler.
Vic. Works Dept, returns show that the
adoption of the minimum-wage principle on con-
tracts has not resulted in any loss to the State.
Twenty minimum-wage contracts have been let
since March, the estimate for which was .£8270,
and the actual cost, .£7660 the department saving
,£6lO on calculated expenditure.
A statement by Beazley, M.L. A., that hunt-
ing-saddles were made in Victoria, fetched the re-
tort —“They cannot make a hunting-saddle
here” —meaning the kind most Jear to hunting-
men. Then Beazley protested that he spoke with
some experience, having spent 26 years in the
saddlery trade. “But not in the saddle /” said
Murray Smith.

Joshua Bros., the big Melb. brandy-
people, are trying hard to show that good,
marketable brandy cannot be made solely from
grapes, and that if differential duties are imposed
their trade will be ruined. It is not the first time
Joshua has had a difficulty about grapes. The
historic Joshua, assisted by Caleb, tried to carry
Oanaanite brandy to the Israelites, and found the
bunch so heavy “that the pole brake.”
A Tennyson story related by Henry Lucy.
The bard was on a yachting-voyage, and pub
in at Copenhagen, where the King of Den-
mark boarded the Pembroke Castle and
stayed to lunch, bringing with him his
daughter, the Princess of Wales. It was the
habit of Mr. Gladstone and the other guests
to assemble in the smoking-room on deck after
luncheon or dinner, and there Tennyson read, or
rather recited, some of his verse. Amongst the
guests was Miss Margot Tennant, with whose
bright personality the Laureate was much
attracted, and as she generally sat near him whilst
he read he (amiable old gentleman !) fell into the
habit of holding her hand. On the occasion of
the Copenhagen luncheon the Princess sat at the
right hand of Tennyson, who selected “ Maud ”
for his reading, and, as usual, became absolutely
absorbed in his work. When he reached the well-
known lyric, “Come into the garden, Maud,” his
right hand drooped at his side, and, finding
another there, he, accustomed bo the companion-
ship of the fascinating Margot, seized it and, it
was obvious to the shocked lookers-on, squeezed
it. Bub the Princess stood it, and did nothing to
discompose the aged poet.
Dear Bulletin, — A sample of the value
and dignity of labor in Melbourne. lam agent
for an express messenger co. which inter alia
addresses and delivers circulars. To-day I called
on a firm advertising thus ;
"WANTED, 8000 prospectuses addressed, type or
’ * handwriting. Quote rate.
I quoted 4s. a thousand, for addressing by hand,
and was told that I was “ miles out of it,” even at
typewriting rates ! Now, it takes a man all his
time to average 100 envelopes per hour, and had
I obtained the job its value per hour would have
panned out just 4fd. As the addresses had to be
picked, and not taken in rotation from a list in
the directory, the job would actually have realised
3d. Typewriting taking about 20 per cent, longer
time, it follows that the would-be successful
tenderer must have been satisfied with just about
2d. per hour, working dawn to dark tor a fort-
night ! Needless to say, the female tenderer was
well to the fore at these rates. Our company
delivers addressed envelopes, within a mile of
the G.P.0., at 3s. 6d. per 100, obtaining receipts
from the addressees, that is to say at less than
half the Post’ Office rates. Even at the figure we
rarely get jobs, seeing that plenty of firms can
put them through for considerably less. The
Paradise of the Workers? The price in London
for addressing circulars is about ss. per 1000.—
Yours, &c., Phulldp.
THE BIRDS WHISTLED “THE DEAD
MARCH.”
u Even the birds on the trees learned to whistle the
‘Dead March.’ ”
It was an old soldier who was talking. “We
were in camp;” he said “ in a flat, malarious part
of the country. Our Colonel was a splendid
tighter, but didn’t • aprear to have-any idea of
sanitary matters. Just then we were in more
dkuger from disease than from the enemy. Pre-
sently fever broke out and men died by the dozen.
Hardly a day but we buried some of them in the
swamp. In fact, we played the ‘ Dea f March ’
so often that I used to fancy the birds in the trees
niftd learned to whistle it. ‘ Awful sorry, you
know, boys,’ said our Colonel, ‘ but ,-o long as we
have to stay here, we can’t help having the fever.’
Vet the Colonel was wrong, as another regiment
camped near.us almost wholly escaped. But their
commander fought the malaria with sanitation
and preventive medicine. That made the differ-
ence.’’
In January, 1892, the influenza was epidemic at
Stubbing, near Chelmsford. Among the persons
attacked were Mrs. Abram Thorogood, of White
House farm, her daughter Annie, and her sons
William and Ernest. They had terrible pains in
the head, sore muscles and joints, and were very
feverish. The whole four—mother and three
children —were confined to their beds.
In a letter bn the subject Mr. Thorogood says :
“My wife became quite delirious; she did not
know where she was, and could neither get in nor
dut of bed. I gave Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup to all of them with excellent results ; the
fever soon left them, and shortly they were well
and strong as ever, and have since remained so.
I may mention that many neighbours and friends
had the same complaint as my wife and family,
but although the others had doctors and the best
attention and advice, none recovered so rapidly as
rfty people did. I thank God that I came to hear
of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, for although
we are a family of seven, living at home, during
the eight years I have kept it in the house we
have not needed a doctor, thus saving many
pounds in doctors’ bills and costly prescriptions.
“ The way I first used Seigel’s Syrup was this :
In the spring of 1883 I began to feel ill and out of
sorts. My tongue was dreadfully coated and a
thick phlegm covered my gums and teeth. After
eating I suffered from pain at the chest and
stomach. I had had nights, and sweat so much
that in the morning my underclothing was soaked
with moisture. I n the following August carbuncles
came on the hack .uy neck, on my nose, and on
my cheek-hone. What I suffered I cannot des-
cribe. I got so low and weak that I could barely
crawl about. The doctors did me no good. And
as for their physic I might as well have taken tea
or water. In pain and suffering I lingered on
until I heard, through a neighbour, of Mother
Scigel’s Curative Syrup and got a bottle from Mr.
W. Linsell, grocer, of Stehhing. A few doses
relieved me, and soon the carbuncles disappeared,
and I was as well as ever. Yours truly, (Signed)
Abram Thorogood, White House Farm, Steh-
bing, by Chelmsford, August 19th, 1892.”
In Mr. Thorogood’s own case the disease was
indigestion and dyspepsia, which poisoned his
blood and caused the carbuncles, which are
growths on the underlying layers of the skin,
After the Syrup had purified his blood they were
absorbed and expelled from the system. The
effect of the remedy in the cases of the other
members of his family shows (what we have often
stated), viz., that influenza attacks those whose
Wood is filled with the poisonous acids thrown into
the system by fermented food in the stomach. Mr.
Thorogood says his wife had been troubled with
indigestion and dyspepsia, which was no doubt
the case with the children also. Don’t allow your
blood to become a breeding-ground of disease.
On the first signs of Indigestion take Mother
.Seigel’s Curative Syrup. Vou know the proverb
about the ounce of prevention.
The English sisters of a Bulletin repre-
sentative are preserving two dingy, decomposed
sprays of flowers under a glass case. The treasures
in question were originally pitched at them by
their gracious Queen and Princess Beatrice during
a Battle of Flowers at Nice, and the misguided
young women (who are said—by themselves —to
have been singled out for these marks of royal
favor on account of their good looks), have just
forwarded a shred of rose-leaf to their relative in
Australia, hoping that the sacred relic will convert
him to “loyalty.”
A poor, crazy Englishwoman, with a history
of misfortune, comes constantly into a Sydney
bank, where she has no account, and on a credit-
form, or blank paper, scribbles her name and the
figures of a large sum of money. Then she calls
the teller and demands payment, and, when her
request is refused, upbraids the bank and its
officials. Another bank-crank is a Chinaman.
Periodically he comes to one Sydney bank and
draws a cheque for =6lOOO or £1,000,100. The
clerks give him some excuse, tell him he lias made
a mistake, or to call again. He goes off satisfied.
Dear Bulletin, — Apropos barter in Aus-
tralian bush townships, I visited many towns in
the “good days” in the now down-trodden occu-
pation of a “ book-fiend.” In each town it was
my custom to advertise largely, and I found the
custom pay, but in many cases I found a difficulty
in fixing upon the proper person to whom to make
payment. Here is an instance : At (in
N.S. Wales) on my first Saturday I was waited
upon by a gentleman from the local newspaper
office, who blandly presented me with a bill for
the one insertion already published. I explained
that my custom was to keep the advt. in while I
remained in the town and square up when leaving.
“Very glad to hear it, but the proprietor was
absent and wages, &c., had to be paid, &c., &c. ”
I paid. In half-an-hour another gentleman from
the same office called with a bill for the same
insertion. I expressed surprise and showed the
previous caller’s receipt. “Ah! ah ! beg pardon,
a mistake,” &c. A few minutes later another man
called with the same bill. Much riled, I roundly
charged the lot with being either lunatics or
swindlers. Gent No. 3 was then kind enough to
explain that the treasury was empty, the proprie-
tor’s pockets ditto, and each in the office “ had to
look out for himself.” The third collector was
the editor, who expressed his regret that the book-
keeper should have taken such a mean advantage
of his colleagues as to “snavel ” the whole of my
account. But the climax was reached when on
Sunday morning the God-fearing proprietor him-
self waited upon me to know if I could not
accommodate him with that little amount: and
House and Windmill. ST. A LLAN’S ESTATE (Geelong)
Photo, by Talma.
his indignation knew no bounds when I produced
the tell-tale receipt. The oomps. sent an envoy to
know if I wouldn’t kindly keep next week’s
amount for them, but I was so disgusted that I
withdrew the advt. altogether.—F.
A paper called the Melbourne Leader
has just discovered Phil May, and summarises his
career so : “After all sorts of ups and downs, and
plenty of globe-trotting in a figurative sense, suc-
cess was won on St. Stephen’s Review,’’ Practi-
cally, Phil May’s success was won in Australia.
His advances in his art after his arrival in Sydney
were speedy, and obvious even to the least critical
Bulletin readers. He attained his present excel-
lence before leaving Australia, and found his
market ready for him as soon as he chose to spread
himself in London. A carefully-selected store of
ignorance is essential in the respectable editor on
the Australian sup-press, but he has not always
sense enough to use his ignorance judiciously.
A Bulletin correspondent :—Auckland—
a city of saints, largely of Scotch origin, who won’t
let either trains or ’buses run on the Saw bath.
A howling desert of dull monotony set in the
midst of lovely scenery—no life, no gaiety, save
awful mockeries called “ high-class concerts ”
where Scotch people pay from 6d. to 18d. to hear
Scotch persons rasp out Scotch “music.” At
10 p. in. a funereal gloom settles down upon the
streets, deserted save for small bands of childish
nymphes du pave ranging from 10 to 15 years ; pro-
bably the predominating Scotch element among
the males has driven the older nymphs to other
cities for a living. No restaurants where one
can obtain a decent meal—not one. No hotels
where there is a first-class bar, such as you
would meet with even in a N.S.W. country town.
Some of us asked for vermouth, and at each place
the melancholy-looking barmaid didn’t know what
it was ; in one case the landlord was summoned,
and in a raucous Highland accent said, with a stern
manner, that there “ was nae ca’ for siccan leeker
in Auoklan’.” We went to an office to use a
telephone ; a tall young person in shirt-sleeves
and violent red-hair seized hold of the handle of
a machine like an old-fashioned ship’s pump, and
turned it with furious energy for seven minutes.
He told us to come in again and he would “get
on.” We got out. From 3to 5 Queen-street is
filled with women—some of them very pretty, but
all dressed badly, very badly. A good many
Maori half-caste women among them, often better-
looking and healthier than their pakeha sisters,
who affect to despise them. But, for all its dole-
fulness, there is not to be seen in Auckland the
same grim poverty one sees daily in Sydney—in
fact, the only things that go a-begging are fine
fish (kawai), three for 6d., and the aforesaid youth-
ful street-walkers.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Damuo’s second win in the G.N. Steeplechase
proves him a real good one, but the enthusiasts who
would place the new idol on a pedestal higher than Red-
leap’s are not reasoning mortals. Redleap ran the course
in practically the same time, with 21lh. more in the
saddle and heavier ground to travel, also Mr. Miller’s
great horse won quite as easily as did Daimio on Satur-
day. When the ex-hunter has carried 13st. 31b. to
victory on a sticky day, after annexing the big hurdle
race at the same meeting, he may then be fairly called
“another Red leap," but until then This Bulletin will
swear by its old champion.
♦ » * ♦
Sage Fred. Swindells, who “ didn’t care to see
his money joomping oop in the air," spoke from long
experience of other people's steeplechase disappoint-
ments. Baltoro, the Ballarat horse that couldn t fall,
managed to tumble early on Saturday, and his clever
rider, A. Williams, was seriously injured. For the sake
of argument, The Bulletin will assume that Baltoro
“ must have won ” if tire circumstance which caused his
downfall had happened to his co-favorite, Daimio,
instead. Anyway, the betting at the start gave some
excuse for this wild hypothesis. Of tile eignt leading
favorites three came to grief, whilst the remaining five
filled tlie first live places. None of the outside division
got a look-in at the finish.
* * * *
Although the Sydney gelding Dart ran a legiti-
mate second to Daimio there was nothing brilliant about
his performance, exc pb at the jumps. He was to have
darted past every tiling at the finish, if he stood up, but
the winner always held him safe,
♦ * * »
It was refreshing to find The Admiral winning
a couple of races at the V.R.O. meeting. Cook’s old
Derby winner was a racehorse among a lot of commoners,
therefore a bit of extra weight didn’t stop him. Good
performances by good animals were what the public used
to look for in less dishonest days, and the fact that a
Derby hero surprised them by running up to form is a
sufficient explanation of tire turf’s decline. As regards
The Admiral, there have been teething troubles to
account for much of his inferior work. The horse is really
well now, and if lie he as gay and muscular in November
he will bid high for the Melb. Oup, and perhaps get the
stake knocked down to him. As a three-year-old he
finished sixth in Carbine’s race, when the going was iron
hard. As a five-year-old he narrovyly escaped getting “ a
place” behind Glenloth in the muddiest Oup ever con-
tested. There is a chance of the well-preserved ancient
distinguishing himself this year in any sort of weather.

Generally conceded that the Y.A.T.C. secre-
taryship, annually yielding £BOO to Harrie Smith, is
about the snuggest thing in Melb. billetdom. For bossing
the Y.R.C. employes Byron Moore only draws £7OO. As

Judge M‘Culloch’s slump from £3OO to £lO6 for 15 days’
work per year is notable.
Since the above was written Mr. Harrie Smith
has died. It seems fatal to a man to envy him. The
reason, perhaps, is that to envy a man it is necessary to
pay a certain amount of attention to his doings. To be
happy it is necessary to enquire very little and pay very
little attention to what goes on around you. Otherwise,
you will discover that this world is a mere slaughter-
house.

the hounds, huntsmen, and hunting ladies, going round
the Steeplechase course, before the public.

A startled bookmaker points out in the Argus
that there are 400 bookmakers round Melb. each employ-
ing a clerk, and, should the totalisator be legalised, these
will be all thrown out of employment. The next com-
plaint will possibly he from the police—that the scarcity
of crime is to lie deplored, creating as it does a falling-oil
in the constable industry.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—You include Donald Wallace
in a list of owners whose luck deserted them after a Melb.
Cup win. Wallace’s boom didn’t set in until he had won
the Melb. Cup with Mentor. A week later he bought
Carbine with some of his Mentor money. And your list
omits the name of perhaps the unluckiest Cup-taker of
them all—Redfearn, who has had a bad time since
Malvolio’s year.—Yours, L.M.Q.
’» » * *
Tim Swiveller Davis, M.L.C., professes friend-
ship for the tote, and has promised to support it in the
Vic. Council, but says he is convinced that 'lie bill has no
chance of passing, “owing principally to the great influ-
ence of the Presbyterian Church.” The Presbyterian
Church is largely represented by fat little men, with
rings on their fingers and hooks to their noses.
Good old Mossy Isaacs, the genial Melbourne
bookie, who diligently lays the double and eschews bad
language, is very severe on Daviesian banks. Mossy, who
mostly speaks truth, although he is disposed to stretch it
a little, declares that £lO,OOO of his hard stuff has gone
down the Gentile sink since the Mercantile failed. “Talk
about bookmakers , ” says Mossy, with his eyes rolling
wildly, and Ids hat tilted back at an angle of 45°, “talk
about bookmakers— they're honorable men, they are,
although this with splendid scorn—“you don't see'em
elbowing their creditors at Government House balls.’’
Mossy is somewhat mollified when you remind him that
the bank-busters to a man are opponents of the tote.
* ♦ ♦ *
‘Old ’ Bradshaw (“Braddy’’) raves against
the tote in a Melb. sporting weekly. Braddy, judging
from his style, must be first cousin to a duke and uncle to
the British constitution. Hear him! “Of the moral
aspect of the totalisator I will say little ; but as this bill
at present stands it makes Communism in its worst form
the ruling spirit of the turf. It is Socialism carried to its
extreme—it places the man who expends thousands to
produce a Carbine or a Commotion on exactly the same
level as the three-card man, and inculcates the immor-
ality which sways the latter. This bill would, indeed,
leiel down and make true the fallacious adage that on the
turt and under it all men are equal.” Evidently a great
believer in caste and “blood” is Braddy. Let arts and
commerce, laws and learning go, but leave him still his

Charters Towers recently, and ordered to enter into £lOO
recognizances to run no more sweeps. Judge Noel said
sweeps were clearly illegal; and the defence asked why.
in that case Q. Govt, didn’t prosecute “Tattersall”
instead of picking out the small marks ; and proceeded Jo
make nasty comments on the attendance at sw'eep
banquets of Q. Postmaster-General and other notables.
If the Judge is right, of which there seems no doubt,
there was no need for the Govt, to bring in an elaborate
Anti-Gambling Bill to make fish of sweep-promoters and
fowl of church-bazaar swindlers. The Bill promises to
pass, anyway ; and where then will George A. flee to?
Not to Maonland where the House of Representatives
has just summarily thrown out a sweep - legalising
measure. New Caledonia, perhaps ? The Bulletin told
mm to go there over a year ago.

Unpleasant stories are told in Melbourne about
direct money bribes given by local bookmakers to M.’sP.
as the price of their opposition to the Totalisator Bill.
Several members, it is averred, confess that they have
been asked to name their price ; ” and the most circum-
stantial stories are related of members whose attitude
with regard to the bill has changed after interviews with
representative members of the Ring. All the svmntoma
point to the brewing of a serious political scandal P

Pastor Sevan, Melb. (£3O a week), to a deputa-
tion of horse-owners and trainers: “Could they not
gradually reduce gambling till it was done by means of
the totalisator only, and then gradually do away with the
totalisator ? Then they would be a happy people ’’ The
pastor has a notion that human nature can be revolu-
tionised on the sly. He would sneak away all our little
sms whilst our attention is otherwise engaged, and non
us into heaven before we have quite recovered ourseKes.
But, unfortunately, the sportsman’s interest cannot be
concentrated upon the collection-plate.

“ The Duke of Portland is highly pleased with
Carbine says the cable. A fat lot Australians care
about that. What they really want to know is what
Carbine thinks of the Duke of Portland.
• * * *
The Central Queensland team, which had been
paralysed the Saturday before by representative NSW
met an outside team of Sydney players last Saturday’
The uninteresting scramble terminated at 3.15 • Central
Q. winning by 13 points to 5. The second match of the
afternoon, between the sturdy and confident Randwicks
and Newcastle Carlton, a neat and neatly-dressed lot was
much livelier for a while, but at half-time the score stood
Randwick 14, Newcastle 0. After the interval, Newcastle
played with a dash that deserved victory, but she lost all
her opportunities, and when the umpire’s last whistle
went Randwick had won by 20 to 0.
• ♦ • •
Geelong football club is now the only unbeaten
Vic. senior team. On Saturday it put up 14 goals
19 behinds to Richmond’s 4 goals 3 behinds • 7 goals
9 behinds to 1 behind being kicked in the last quarter.
* » » ♦
Essendon football team has been defeated four
times this season.
* * » *
Miss “Concord” Walker has presented the
N.S.W. Rowing Association with a racing eight-oar boat
complete. And, by the way, the press invariably calls
Australia’s millionairess “Edith.” Miss Walker’s spon-
sors christened her “ Eadith,” with an a.
• * * *
Gov. designate Buxton (S.A.), a strong sup-
porter of the British anti-gambling league, will not it
seems to be certain, attend any S.A. race-meetings. Pre-
sumably, also, H.E. will decline to attend cricket matches
where the choice of innings depends upon the spin of a
coin. The Bulletin hereby points out to Mr. Buxton the
danger to the morals of the community contained in this
insidious custom.
♦ * * *
At Coolgardie they play cricket on Saturday
and football on Sunday. An Adelaide boy, Hope Nichol-
son, made the first local century in a recent cricket-
match.
* * * »
The Austral god of the future. In Centennial
Park, Sydney, a bronze statue of an idyllic footballer has
just been erected. More interest is taken in this figure
than in all the other statuary in the park—great states-
men, warriors, goddesses in undress uniform, and all. In
unborn years Australians will make annual pilgrimage to
this shrine, and will keep a sacred lamp ever burning
before it.

Thirty years ago (3rd July, 1865) W. G. Grace
first appeared for the Gentlemen of England against the
Players.
* ♦ * *
Of W. G. Grace’s 100 centuries nine have been
scored against Australia.

To prevent unfairness at glove-fights, why not
have a vacant space round the ring, so that w hen a man
is forced on to the ropes there would be no possibility of
9ly punch in g, kicking, or holding by barrackers?
♦ * ♦ •
Australian professional rowing is dead. The
regatta glories of the past 1 In ’72 Balmain Regatta blub
offered £5OO for an interprovinoial four-oared race, and
negotiated with England and America with a view to
having an international fours race in ’73, guaranteeing
£2OO worth of trophies and £5OO to each crew from those
foreign parts tor expenses. Now 1! ! In ’74 the Clarence
Riverites gave £355 in cash for prizes at their annual
regatta on Queen's Birthday. There was no entrance-fee
for the Champion Out-rigger Race of £2OO, and it was
won by Mick Rush, with Ned Trickett second.
» ♦ * ♦
The English bowler of this season is Pougher,
the Leicestershire man. Pougher visited Australia with
the last Shaw-Shrewsbury team, which ran in opposition
to Walter Read’s Eleven (brought out by the M.G.C.) and
had such a bad financial time.
(“ Sporting Notions ” Continued on page 22.)
Talk about the privileges of royalty. In
one thing common or kitchen womankind has
a big start of the royal woman. The latter can t
lie about her age !
The eagle flies high and roosts high-
“ Eagle ” cigarettes go high and cost lew. That’
all the difference.*
With the beginning of the really cold
weather in Melbourne it is strange to see the lead-
ing drapery houses sacrificing their winter goods
to make room for an early show of Spring fashions.
Such is the case, however; and it is marvellous
this winter to note the extreme manner in which
the prices of articles which one would have
thought would just be in the greatest demand-
such as furs, sealskin jackets, winter mantles,
dress stuffs, &c.—are reduced at Messrs. Hicks,
Atkinson & Sons’ establishment in Collins-street,
during their existing stock-taking sale.*
One of the handsomest colts of the season
appeared at the late Caulfield meeting. This was
Prentham, by Trenton out of Nellie Moore. He is
a good bay, of average height, but wonderfully
strong and well put together. Nellie has never
thrown a bad one, and Lochiel, Corunna, Gulloden
and Steadfast have testified to her capabilities as
a stud matron. More than one good judge re-
marked of Trentham, “ he’s a regular Derby
horse and he will be a lucky man who gets him
in'the St. Alban’s distribution.*
Bernard's Fine Art Gallery, at 430 George-
street, Sydney, contains just now an unusually
fine collection of works of art, and all connoisseurs,
as well as that much larger class of persons who
are not connoisseurs but desire to look as if they
were, will find this establishment well worth a
visit. A particularly fine collection of water
color views is now to be seen at One
specially beautiful work is a view of Lake, Ala-
baster (Canterbury, M.L.I, and, among a large
number of others, almost equally charming, are
a view of Shoreham (England), a moonlight
English landscape, a picture of an old English
church in Sussex, and many more which cannot
be particularised.*
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of G6oft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic ; 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. Ihe
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long.

Vandyke and Co., the George-st. (Sydney)
photographers, have introduced what they claim
to be a new enlargement process, by which pictures
are finished with almost the delicacy of steel-
engravings. People vho have jumped over the
precipice of matrimony, and people about to
jump, and people who welter with a small, un-
couth infant in the shadow of the valley, are par-
ticularly requested to call and see how beautiful
the new process will make them.*
