Bravo Jones and Mr. “ Seppy ” Stephen were
conspicuous figures in the club-stand and enclosure at
Epsom Derby meeting. I suppose, writes Tub Bulletin’s
correspondent, they got there owing to their connection
with the V. R.C. and A.J.O. Stephen, who followed Jones
In backing Sir Visto, but in a small way, is industriously
booming the starting-machine, which is to have a trial at
tandown. Mountmorganaire D’Arcy had bis usual big
party in his private stand at the Derby. He is most
iberal in his invitations to Australians, and most men of
iny note “ down under ” may be found partaking of his
hvish hospitality on Derby and Oak days.
‘** * ♦
“ Bravo,” or “lucky ” Jones, as they call him
In E igland, won over £2OOO on Sir Visto in the Derby. ■
He backed the horse both for win and place on his own
Judgment, as he thought he was the best-looking of a
very bad lot. Of course, writes The Bulletin’s corres-
pondent, the books have only lent the money for a few
days, as “ Bnvo” plunges, and they are bound to get it
back. Jo > Thompson had to pay him a bit.
A familiar sight at any suburban race-meeting
just now is the min leaning against the fence working
out deep and mysterious calculations on his “ card.” He
is the system-bettor, and is figuring how ranch he’ll need
to pul on Bullswool for the next race in order to get back
the £1 he lost on Nostril for the previous one, and also
the £5 he ought to have won on the aforesaid Nostril. If
Bullswool loses you’ll see him, after the race, figuring out
what amount he will require to back Flyblow' to win him
back the £2 he has lost, and also the £lO he expected to win
over the two previous races. And so on through the day.
Of course when he does back a winner he is safe ; but few
men have a pocket long enough and a will strong enough
to keep going against a run of bad luck.
* * * *
The number of jockeys killed on the flat in Aus-
tralia during the past season is appalling. At the present
rate of going, the old-time gladiator or toreador had “ a
woman’s billet ” as compared to the modern jockey.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Mr. W. Pridham, a Vic. racing enthusiast, says
that the majority of Vic. horse-owners belong to a class
who should not owm racehorses. The totalisalor wmuld,
he says, if introduced, wipe them out in one act. That is
the best answer to the loud squawk of the owners who
live on the game.
* * * *
Private “totes” still flourish in Melb. suburbs.
At one shop, the other Saturday, 4000 shillings were
invested on a small meeting.
* * ♦ ♦
“ Bookmaker ” writes in a Melb. paper on his
trade, and, being of very long experience, both in Eng-
land and Australia, doubtless many can spot him. Except
in a couple of good years, he has never been worth more
than £2OOO, though occupying an esteemed position. He
argues, in short, that bookmaking is the spider-wheel
buggy-trade that the punters and preachers believe it to
be. There is some confusion, however, between makings
and keepings.
♦ ♦ • *
A St. Arnaud man sues for damages from the
local race-club for a broken-leg mishap to his son while
riding at a recent meeting. Plaintiff bases his claim on
the alleged bad state of the course. If he succeeds, many
clubs will have to put their courses in order or go out of
the business.
N.S.W. postal revenue last year is estimated
as having lost £lO,OOO through the interdiction of Tatter-
sail’s sweep. Yet Queensland is going to face the music.
* » » »
In England it is the rage to mate the St. Simon
mares with the Musket blood. Why not here cross the
Musket blood mares with the St. Simon horse, Bill of Port-
land ?
* * * »
Scene : Melbourne suburban racecourse. Female
trainer, two boys, and two ponies. The boy mounting.
Female trainer ( loq .): “ Take him round twice at a sharp
canter and sweat that gut off him.” The New Woman.
* * * *
In Victoria, now, the New Woman owns, trains
and backs—and gets put off the track just like a man. It
is quite on the cards that she will yet steer her own
neddies, as men are deceitful creatures.
* * * »
As with monkeys, babies and green peas,
Chinamen are all alike. Lottery-keepers seldom pay
fines, but ask for time. When time is up, another Chow
walks up to gaol, “does his bit,” and draws a few pounds
when he comes out. The Law is satisfied, and the police
wink the other eye.
• • * *
“Pastor George Soltau (the Tas. earthquake-
man?) at Gore, Maoriland: “I do not know of this
colony, but I do know of the Australian colonies, that
they worship a god of leather, the Football, and a smaller
god of leather, the Cricket-ball ; and when 2000 men go
to see Carbine off to England, and you can’t get 200 men
to a prayer-meeting, no wonder God has smitten Victoria
with a depression such as that colony, has been experi-
encing.”
• * * •
One argument urged against the tote is that a
trainer cannot back his horse for any amount—that as
soon as he puts a few pounds on the public rush and spoil
his price. Yet owners and trainers aver that the
“ books” have so combined as to make it almost impos-
sible to get a fair price about anything. So many runners
and telegraphists are employed by them that the instant
a horse is genuinely backed in the right quarter every
penciller within a quarter-mile radius knows it; and down
go the odds.
♦ * ♦ ♦
It is calculated that cyclist Zimmerman, in a
red-hot go, maintains for 2min. a speed requiring the
expenditure of energy representing two-thirds of 1 h.p.,
while for 6sec., it is alleged, he has exerted the remark-
able force of l| h.p. in driving his bike. This feat is con-
sidered equal to raising a 1881 b. weight Iyd. high in Isec.,
yet in ordinary riding over a specified distance a cyclist
would not labor a quarter as much as a runner.
• * * *
Young George Martin, son of N.S.W.’s late
Chief-Justice, is distinguishing himself as an oarsman at
Oxford, writes a Bulletin correspondent from England.
For years his college, St. John’s, has been trying in vain
to work up towards the “head of the river,” but until
this year have always stuck behind Christ Church. This
season young Marlin his gone to the stroke thwart, with
the result that John’s has gone up four places and now
Stands sixth. The cornstalk pulls a very good oar, and it
is predicted that he will, in all probability, be tried for
the ’Varsity eight at biw, with a good chance of his Blue,
Pioreat Australia !
* * ♦ *
Marvellous W. G. Grace (writes The Bulle-
tin’s correspondent) has just scored his 1000 runs in first-
class matches within a month of the opening of the
season. For years he has not batted in such form as the
following record will show: —
Runs.
At Lord’s, M. 0.0. v. Sussex (13 and 103) .. 116
At Lord’s, M.C.C. v. Yorkshire (18 and 25) .. 43
At Bristol, Gloucester v. Somerset .. 288
At Cambridge, A. J. VVebbe’s Eleven v. Cam-
bridge University 52
At Gravesend, Gloucester v. Kent (257 and 73
not out) 380
At the Oval, England v. Surrey 18
At Lord’s, Gloucester v. Middlesex .. .. 169
Total for nine innings 1016
He is quite the hero of the hour, and crow'ds follow him,
cheering as they did his performances of 30 years ago.
The W. W. Read benefit (writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent) was a frost. They expected
large gates, but owing to the miserable show made by
Surrey in the first innings (did the pros, play badly on
purpose?)public interest did not rise to the occasion, and
a few hundreds only resulted. It is time that bastard
amateurism should be knocked on the head. Read has
been for years supposed to be an auctioneer, but he has in
reality been a paid professional for the Surrey County
under the guise of “ Assistant Secretary ” —though he has
hardly ever done work in that capacity.
* * ♦ •
South Africa is the Australian boxer’s new
Mecca. Joe Goddard, Tut Ryan, and Artie Tully are the
latest departures for a cut at the shekels of the gold and
diamond men.
• * * *
Footballers are a thirsty crowd. Two or three
of the players in a southern suburban first 20 got hold of
a club ball and sold it for 25., which they immediately
converted into beer.
* • • •
Football gates spell professionalism ; no gates—
amateurism. The only cure.
* » » *
At Ararat (Vic.) last week a man died during
the progress of a football-match. Oddly enough, he was a
spectator, not a player, and he succumbed to perfectly
natural causes.
* * * *
H. Do Maus, one of Maoriland’s best cricketers,
deemed good enough for an Australian eleven, is coming
to Sydney to endeavor to achieve his ambition.
*■• * *
Alphonso Brown, who won the three-miles
bicycle handicap off scratch, was the only Sydneyite to do
any good at the Brisbane Hospital Carnival on Saturday.
Megson, Williams, Wootten, and Fowles started without
success in the three miles cash handicap ; and Fitzpatrick
fell and broke his collar-bone.

Editor Sydney Bulletin.
Sir,
Wil{ you please publish, at my expense, the
following correspondence for the benefit of
theatrical managers, and touring professionals. I
recently signed a contract to Blight, manager of
the Bill Posting Advertising 00., of Ballarat,
for the posting of 20 three-sheet lithograph
posters, for the sum of £2 10s. per week for one
week or more. The contract remained in force
four weeks. But, as I had only 12 posters with
me, I wanted to pay for one week only, and the
following experience and correspondence speaks
for itself.
Yours faithfully,
ALBERT RICHARD, M.E.
Milburn’s Coffee Palace, Bendigo,
13th July, ’95.
Lydiard Street.
n . _ Ballarat, 6th July, 1895.
Professor Richard,
Milburn’s Coffee Palace,
Bendigo.
Dear Sir, —I have received your letter with
summons enclosed. will obey your instructions
and attend court, but it is very difficult to do any-
thing without your being present as a witness to
give evidence; and I greatly fear unless you come
to Ballarat for the Tuesday there will be a verdict
given against you. Ido not think the parties are
entitled to the money claimed, but it depends on
the evidence given by you, and it is a pity if you
cannot come. If you do not come, I will let you
know result by letter on Tuesday.
I am, yours truly,
F. H. TUTHILL.
Per E. H. 0.
Lydiard Street,
Ballarat, 21st June, 1895.
Professor Richards,
Kelsall’s Buildings,
_ Lydiard Street, Ballarat.
Dear Sir, —There was an order made this morn-
ing against you for .£6 6s. Mr. Blight in the box
swore that there was no number of posters men-
tioned when the contract was made between you
and him, and that all the hoardings he had avail-
able for posters was 12. He swore there was no
agreement to charge you anything less than the
£2 10s. a week on account of his having 12 posters
to exhibit. On that evidence the Court found for
him for .£5 and £1 6s. costs, making £6 6s. in all,
which with £1 Is., my costs, makes £7 75., less
£5, which you paid me the other day, leaving
£2 75,, which kindly forward. Prior to the hear-
ts' I paid .£3 2s. 6d. into court, so as to protect
you in the event of Mr. Blight admitting that
there were only 12 posters to be paid for.
I am, yours truly,
F. H. TUTHILL.
Lydiard Street,
Ballarat, 9th J uly, 1895.
Professor Richard,
Milburn’s Coffee Palace,
Bendigo,
Dear Sir, —Blight and others v. You. As I
feared, an order was made against you this morn-
ing for £6 65., being .£5 debt and £1 6s. costs.
Mr. Blight went into the box and proved that
you signed the contract, and that the posters were
duly exhibited for the month, and as I could give
no evidence to the contrary, the court made an
order against you for the above amount. I have
got a stay of execution until Thursday, to allow
time for me to write to you and get a reply with
the money. Kindly forward me the £6 65., and
£1 Is. for my costs, by return post.
It was a great pity you could not have attended
the court, because your evidence would, I think,
have gained you the verdict.
Yours truly,
F. H. TUTHILL.

Election Notices.
TO THE ELECTORS OF THE KING
DIVISION OF SYDNEY.
Gentlemen,
More than 40 years ago the undivided city of
Sydney, of which you now form so important a
part, opened the door for my entrance into Par-
liamentary life by electing me by a majority of
two to one to the seat just vacated by Mr. Went-
worth, the author of our Constitution. Events
were then rapidly giving life and form to liberal
thought and opinion, and the dawn of freedom
was shedding its first invigorating light upon the
land. Throughout the long period which has since
elapsed, I have labored side by side with many of
the best men who ever sat in Parliament for the
enactment of sound laws and the honest adminis-
tration of Government. If my many Acts were
withdrawn from it, the very Statute Book of to-
day would present a barren record.
To all who know me, and to all fair-minded
men, it may be well understood that I am not a
seeker of a seat in Parliament. I could easily
have obtained that object from my late con-
stituents. But a momentous crisis in the history
of our country has arisen, when a knot of unscrupu-
lous men, by arts and artifices that would ill bear
examination, have obtained possession of the
offices of State, and are bent upon securing their
own personal ends, even by carrying disaster and
ruin into the industrial pursuits and productive
interests of all classes of the population. On the
invitation of a large body of your fellow electors,
I have consented to enter into the contest for your
suffrages as a direct and determined opponent of
the Prime Minister. By his advice to the Lieu-
tenant-Governor, Mr. Reid has brought about
what was described by himself in the Legislative
Assembly as a “penal dissolution.” He had a
large working majority, which would have satis-
fied the greatest English Minister. But Mr.
Reid, like most small-witted men, is fond of new
projects and new methods, without the power of
brain to discern their fitness or unfitness, their
wisdom or unwisdom. Having wantonly em-
broiled himself with the Upper Chamber, he has,
at the bidding of the Daily Telegkaph, dissolved
Parliament, by which he is certain to banish
many of his pliant followers from the Assembly,
but by which he cannot bring a feather’s
weight to bear upon the Council. It is the mere
childish menace of a feeble boaster. But has not
Mr. Reid to thank.his own arrogance and his own
abusive tongue, and his notorious incapacity to
wisely frame his measures, for most of his troubles
with the Legislative Council ? Forgetting what
is due to the great office lie holds, he began his
conciliatory course by describing the members of
the Council afcujiore reckless slaughterers than
any to be found at the Abattoirs; he next held
them up as mummies, only fit to be stowed away
the museum'; then he spoke of them as men of
sinister eyes and bull necks (not keeping in view
his own symmetrical proportions); and finally, at
the last sitting of Parliament, he called upon some
imaginary power to “sweep the old fossils ’’away.
Did ever Prime Minister before speak of his equals
in this sort of language?
I now beg of you to examine Mr. Reid’s legisla-
tive measures. His Local Government Bill was
so crude and ill-drawn, and so wanting in provi-
sions to meet the wants of the people, that it is
fortunate that he himself laid it aside in the
Assembly. But that Bill never reached the
Upper House ; it was killed by the extreme men
of the Labor-party—Mr. Reid’s present allies.
You will next look at the Land and Income-tax
Assessment Bill. That measure contained exemp-
tions from the operation of the proposed tax of all
incomes of <£3oo downwards, which was nothing
short of an unblushing attempt to bribe the
members of the Assembly. The Council is denied
the right of amending a taxation bill, and the
members of that body had to choose between
giving their sanction to an iniquitous proposal and
rejecting the measure. An intelligent examina-
tion of the defective character of Mr. Reid’s
other bills as sent to the Legislative Council, will
disclose grounds of objection similar to those I
have pointed out. But I invite your attention to
Mr. Reid’s plan of reforming the Upper House.
It is a notable thing that the bogus democrats who
have floated to the surface shrink from trusting
the creation of the Upper House to the votes of
the true democracy. But Mr. Reid’s plan is,
beyond example, the silliest ever submitted to an j
intelligent people, and if it were possible for it to i
be carried into effect, no man of self-respect and
independence would accept a seat in a House so
formed; it would be debased to a Chamber of
Flunkeys, and would soon become the corrupt
instrument of any profligate Minister of the day.
When seeking election 12 months ago, Mr. Reid
made all manner of wild promises. He would im-
mediately abolish the Dibbs tariff and bring in
freetrade. Has he done it ? He would manage
things so well that, within a month, there would
be no “unemployed ”in the country. Have they
not increased_ by thousands? Has he redeemed
any one of his boastful promises? On the con-
trary, has he not sent starving men into the public
parks to work for their food without wages ? Has
not his criminal ignorance and folly paralysed
every branch of trade and industry, and brought
ruin upon countless families ?
In asking you to elect me as your representa-
tive, I desire to state clearly and boldly what, in
my judgment, is the work which Parliament ought
to be immediately invited to do. In view of the
early federation of the colonies, nothing whatever
ought to be done, which would create obstacles to
Union. After the Colonies become united and
occupy the status of a nation, no provincial Cus-
toms’ tariff can possibly exist. The very act of
union will lodge the sole power of imposing Cus-
toms duties in the Federal Parliament. There-
fore, as I have often stated in public, no man can
be a sincere advocate of Federation and, at the
same time, a champion of provincial tariffs. The
two positions are absolutely irreconcilable. But
Federation would open all the markets of wide
Australia to all Australian producers and manu-
facturers, without let or hindrance. All men of
patriotic spirit will unite, irrespective of differ-
ences of opinion on smaller questions, to hasten
the advent of Australian Union. Then will come
the _ great struggle for the settlement of the
National Tariff, when men can take sides accord-
ing to their convictions in the election of the
Federal Parliament.
But the cause of Federation does not stand in
the way of advancing the interests of New South
Wales as a separate colony or state. Undoubtedly
one of the measures moat urgently wanted is a
comprehensive and carefully-framed bill to confer
upon the country districts the blessings of Local
Self-government. Such a bill was introduced by j
me in 1891, and was lost largely through the
machinations of Mr. Reid, who joined the Pro- I
teotionist Opposition of the day to destroy it.
Any such bill must provide for a liberal endow-
ment to the self-governing bodies, with ample
powers of taxation for local improvements. In
my judgment, if we inaugurated a wise policy of
improving our inland navigable waters, our coastal
harbors, and of enhancing the value of the public
estate by irrigation works, the preservation of
the rain-fall, and by carrying out cheap cross-
country railways, fields of reproductive labor
could be opened which would absorb every able-
bodied man now out of employment, while our
national assets would be largely increased in
value.
These are some of the questions which ought to
engage the attention of Parliament, instead of
wasting time by tinkering with impossible provin-
cial tariffs which cannot feed hungry men and
women, or serve the national cause of Australia.
I remain, Gentlemen, your faithful servant,
HENRY PARKES.
July 12, 1895. Annandale.

One of the best races
ever run in Australia was
the Canterbury Plate at
Elemington in November,
1886. There were four
starters, Trident, Trenton,
Cyclops, and Tamarisk, the
latter of whom went to
make the pace for Trident.
This she did for a mile, when
Trenton and Trident singled
themselves out and ran a
most desperate race to the
post, Trident just winning
nt the last. Although Alec.
Robertson, who was on
Trenton, had out him up,
he remarked to a by-
stander, “ He’s the best and
gamest horse I ever rode,
and I was more done than
him at the finish.” The
time was omin. 25fsecs.,
which was the record for
years. Trenton, 5 years,
9st. 61b. ; Trident, 3 years,
7st. 31b. Trenton at the
same meeting, carrying 9st.
51b., ran Arsenal to a neck
for the Melbourne Cup.
A Maoriland man was drowned recently,
in a lake where the maximum depth was under
5ft., through the capsizing of his canoe. It never
occurred to him that the bottom was within easy
walking distance, so he swam until he became
exhausted, and then he went down horizontally,
and died on his stomach on the sand with just 48
inches of water above him.

Rev. S. Gr. M‘Laren, of Melbourne, pro-
tests against free scholarships for State-school
children. “ The country wants thews and sinews,”
says the rev. shepherd :—
Tough sinews and muscles for tilling the soil
Are what is most wanted, the parsons avow ;
But the parson himself holds aloof from the toil,
And he lives by the sweat of the other man’s brow.
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane

A London newspaper-vendor says that
during the Wilde trials he sold twice as many
evening papers to women as to men. The idea of
woman’s “native modesty ” is by this time pretty
well exploded. Women are no more modest than
men—perhaps not so much so. But they sham
modesty better and oftener than men, because
m m expect it from them, and heredity and social
conventions make the pleasing of men their first
object in life.
The sale of old Bulletins at Melbourne
wharves, and other parts of that swindlers’ para-
dise, seems to have inspired a new reading of the
law by policemen on the spot. A victimised pur-
chaser of an old Bulletin is informed that he
can’t give the fraudulent newsboy into custody
unless he can produce witnesses to prove that he
specially asked for “this week’s Bulletin,”
which, of course, the victim can’t do, inasmuch as
he took the date, of the paper for granted, and had
no witnesses to the transaction, anyway. More-
over, the boys have been educated in the police
doctrine as to what constitutes felony in news-
paper dealing, therefore a customer who mentions
“this week’s Bulletin” is supplied with it,
whilst the customer who merely says “ Give me a
Bulletin,’’ gets an old copy shoved into him, and
has no redress. That is to say, the police stand
by and smile upon the swindle.

Melb. City Council now prohibits city
hairdressers from painting their verandah-posts
with the traditional tricolor trade sign. The
erection of the barbers’ poles within the city boun-
daries also infringes the bye-law.
An engineer on board a China steamer
recently had a chilly experience between Sydney
and Newcastle. The refrigerating - room lock
having gone wrong, he was unable to make him-
self heard or to get out for two hours, when, by
means of some tools fortunately at hand, he man-
aged to make his exit. It was a narrow escape.

If Queen Victoria lives another 15 months
she will have reigned longer than George the
Third, whose record was 591 years. Moreover,
George wasn’t counted as king during the last 10
years of his miserable career. He was a goggle-
eyed lunatic, whose beloved sons—V.R’s. father
and uncles—used to tie him up to the bed-post and
call him bad names.
Five Castlemaine (Vic.) publicans lately
helped Turner’s revenue estimate to the tune of
about .£3O by Sunday trading. With 44 “ hotels”
(courtesy title) in Castlemaine, competition must
kill either thtrpublican or his customer. Within
a radius of about half-a-dozen miles of Castlemaine
P.O. exist 85 tanglefoot depdts, to serve an
estimated population of 8500 : one house to 100
throats.

Marshall’s Mate .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You almost heard the surface bake, and saw
the gum-leaves turn—
You could have watched the grass scorch
brown had there been grass to burn.
In such a drought the strongest heart might
well grow faint and weak—
’T would frighten Satan back to—well—-not
far from Dingo Creek.
The tanks went dry on Ninety Mile, as
tanks go dry out-back,
The Half Way Spring had failed at last when
Marshall missed the track ;
Beneath a dead tree on the plain we saw a
packhorse reel—
Too blind to see there was no shade, and too
done-up to feel.
And charcoaled on the canvas bag (’twas
written pretty clear)
We read the message Marshall wrote. It
said, ‘ ‘ I’m taken queer—
I’m somewhere off of Headman’s track, half
blind and nearly dead ;
Find Crowbar, get him sobered up, and
follow back,” it said.
“ Let Mitchell go to Bandicoot. You’ll find
him there,” said Mack.
“ I’ll start the chaps from Starving Steers,
and take the dry-holes back.”
We tramped till dark and tried to track the
pack-horse on the sands,
And just at daylight Crowbar came with
Milroy’s station hands.
“ Spread out as widely as you can each side
the track,” said he ;
“ The first to find him make a smoke that
all the rest can see.”
We took the track and followed back where
Crowbar follow’d fate,
We found a dead man in the scrub —but
’twas not Crowbar’s mate.
The station-hands from Starving Steers were
searching all the week—
But never news of Marshall’s fate came back
to Dingo Creek ;
And no one save the Spirit of the Sand-
waste, fierce and lone,
Knows where Jack Marshall crawled to die—
hit Crowbar might have known.
He’d scarcely closed his quiet eyes or drawn
a sleeping breath—
They say that Crowbar slept no more until
he slept in death.
A careless, roving scamp, that loved to laugh
and drink and joke,
But no man saw him smile again (and no one
saw him smoke),
And, when we spelled at night, he’d lie with
eyes still open wide
And watch the stars as if they’d point the
place where Marshall died.
The search was made as searches are (and
often made in vain),
And on the seventh day we saw a smoke
across the plain ;
We left the track and followed back—’twas
Crowbar still that led, ■ -
And when his horse gave out at last he
walked and ran ahead.
We reached the place and turned again—
dragged back and no man spoke— :
It was a bush-fire in the scrubs that made
the cursed smoke.
And when we gave it best at last, he said
a Til see it through,”
Although he knew we’d done as much as
mortal men could do.
“By God, I won’t give up !” he said—his
hand pressed to his brow— .
“ The cursed flies and ants and things, they
might be at him now.
Good-bye, boys ; tell the folks I’ve gone to
find my mate,” he said,
‘ ‘ I’m going back to find him, and I will—
alive or dead !”
He packed his horse with water and provi-
sions for a week,
And then, at sunset, crossed the plain, away
from Dingo Creek.
We watched him tramp beside the horse till
we, as it grew late,
Could not tell which was Bonypart, and
which was Marshall’s mate.
The dam went dry at Dingo Creek and we
were driven back,
And none dared face the Ninety Mile when
Crowbar took the track.
They saw him at “Dead Camel” and up the
Dry Hole Creeks—
There came a day when none had heard of
Marshall’s mate for weeks ;
They’d seen him at No Sunday, he called at
Starving Steers —
There came a time when none had heard of
Marshall’s mate for years.
They found old Bonypart at last, picked
clean by hungry crows,
But how his master died none knew—the
soul of Marshall knows !
And now, way out on Dingo Creek, when
winter days are late,
The bushmen talk of Crowbar’s ghost
“ what’s looking for his mate
For let the cynics scoff and sneer, and let
the wise men doubt—
The soul of Crowbar and his mate have
travelled further out.
Beyond the. furthest two-rail fence, Oolanne
and Nevertire—
Beyond - the furthest rabbit-proof, barbed
wire and common wire—
Beyond the furthest “ Gov’ment ” tank, and
past the furthest bore—
The Never Never, No Man’s Land, No-
more and Nevermore—
Beyond the Land o’ Break-o’-Day, and sun-
set and the dawn,
The soul of Marshall and the soul of Mar-
shall’s mate have gone.
Sydney, ’94.
JOE SWALLOW.
America still leads. In Kansas a man has
been tried and convicted for hypnotising another
in order to make him commit a murder. The
actual slayer was, tried and acquitted, and now
the hypnotic murderer has been sentenced to bd
hanged. The sentence has been confirmed by the
Supreme Court.
Emanuel Baptist Church, San Francisco,
has a bad record. Oneofitspastorssuicided, another
was a murderer —he committed the unpardonable
crime of shooting an editor. Now two young
girls have been outraged and strangled in the
precincts which it would be grotesque mockery to
call sacred. On Saturday, April 13, some ladies
entered the church to decorate it for Easter.
They found in a small room off the library the
body of Marian Williams, aged 18, stiff and cold.
The church was searched, and on the following
day, Easter Sunday, while the bells of the
city were jangling a joyous hymn for the
risen Christ, the nude body of Blanche Lamont,
a young school-teacher, was found high up in the
belfry tower of Emmanuel Church. Suspicion
points to the church librarian and Sunday-school
superintendent, Theodore Durrant, the last person
seen with both the dead girls. Durrant is a
medical student; and it is said that he was a
sexual pervert, and had been brooding over a
medical work on abnormal sexual crimes, and that
he had made improper proposals to other girls
whom he had endeavored to inveigle into the
church (of which he had a key) on various pre-
texts. Durrant was also met in a suspicious
state of undress and excitement in or
near the church about the time when the
crimes were committed; and the case against
him, though wholly circumstantial, seems strong.
A cruel commentary upon the facts comes as
a petition from the city publicans, against
whom the churches not long ago led a crusade.
The churches wanted the private doors of public-
houses closed, and liquor-licenses to be issued only
to parsons vouched for by twelve citizens. The
publicans now ask that private doors in churches
be closed ; that no separate rooms, bedrooms, or
lounges be permitted in churches; and that no
preacher be licensed except on the recommenda-
tion of twelve citizens. “This petition,” it is
said, “is not conceived in a spirit of malice, but
with the highest and loftiest and most honorable
object of saving the young girls of San Francisco
from murderers, and preventing the debasement
of churches as houses of ill-fame.” The satire is
keen and just. For, as says the Akgonaut, which
heads its article on the subject :
There is a fountain filled with blood
Drawn from Emmanuel’s veins—
“ there is no further field for Emmanuel Baptist
Church as a temple of the Most High. Whatever
may be the fate of Durrant, Durrant ? s church
should die.”
Old Mrs. Spurling, of Auburn (Vic.),
stumbled and broke her arm the other day. She
died of the shook a week aftetwards—at the age
of 104. It was her boast that she had held in, her
hand the acorn from which'grew the finest oak-
tree in Victoria.
S. Australia is losing the Queensland trade
owing to the indiscriminate collection of the
stock-tax in the former province. Formerly, sup-
plies for North and N.W. Queensland were largely
drawn from South Australia, wool being sent in
exchange. Now, the Longreach and Charleville
teamsters are scooping in the trade. Teamsters
with Queensland wool to Horgott (S.A.) have
been worried for the tax on working(!)bullocks, and
only allowed to load for the back journey on con-
dition they return in six months —tax guaranteed.
The Melbourne city market, which the
Municipal Council built, with .£250,000 of bor-
rowed money, on the yielding sand of the Yarra
bank, is sinking in various directions. Part of the
building has moved several inches from its original
location, and the rest is cracking and bursting
asunder. It is an enormous pile, erected in the
boom days, when money was splashed around like
water. No one really wanted the market, or had
any use for it, so it has never even paid interest
on its cost. Now it would seem that the famous
white elephant is bogged, and .£250,000 is going
down to the corpse-fed crab and the ghoul-like
Yarra eel.
A lugubrious letter from a Queensland
paper:—
1 wish no harm to anyone, but we want the dry seasons
back again. What we had to sell then we got some-
thing for, now we get nothing. Just fancy butter at Id.
per lb., and maize at lOd. per bushel! How are we to
live on it? Your Forest Hill correspondent quoted an
instance in his last where a neighbor sent tw o tons of
potatoes to Brisbane and got 6d. in return, and lost his
bags into the bargain. Well, 1 sent five tons of really
good potatoes to Brisbane last season, and got back
17s. fid., and I also lost my bags, which cost me £l, so
after all I was 2s. 7d. to the bad, with all my labor lost.
And this is how we live and are expected to pay our way.
“ Go on the land, young man, go on the land,”
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
The Odontological Society of Vic. was
registered the other week under humorous cir-
cumstances connected with the civil war in Mel-
bourne’s little world of dentistry. Ever since
E. L. Oldfield started his dental college and
hospital, a number of furious rivals have been
trying to scuttle his membership in certain useless,
decrepit institutions which a representative tooth-
doctor is expected to belong to. It seems that an
odontological society existed in Melbourne for 10
years. There was no tangible idea in the thing,
except “ prestige,” and it had dwindled down to
a body of 13 financial members (including Old-
field), when he found that it was making secret
arrangements to go bung and form itself anew,
without including him in the reconstructed- con-
cern. Thereupon, Oldfield lured a “ high official”
into an official admission that the society was
defunct, and immediately called a meeting of
friendly dentists to form the O.S. of Vic. as afore-
said. A day later, when the dead society recon-
structed and tried to register as an 0.5., Limited,
they found that their title had been appropri-
ated by Oldfield for his new-born institution.
Then the corpse of the old society arose from its
winding-up sheet and swore that it hadn’t died at
all, and passed a cold, clammy resolution to the
effect that E. L. Oldfield ought to be expelled
front its decomposing remains.

A Case by the Way.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The road from Dargo to Ghost Creek is one of
the worst in Victoria. It twines through heavily-
timbered country, and is so narrow that the coach
passengers can touch the branches of ti-tree and
dog-wood on each side as they drive along.
1 was sitting on the box-seat, and dreamily
watching the narrow pathway twisting itself like
a long grey boa-constrictor into numerous S’s on
ahead. The driver’s skill was admirable, and our
four horses were the most sagacious beasts that ever
wore harness. One minute the near wheels would
take the bark off a gum-tree to avoid a deep rut,
and the next minute the off wheels would plunge
boldly into a deep rut to avoid steeplechasing over
a fallen log.
“What would be done if two vehicles met on
the road ? ” I asked.
“Traps never do meet on this road,” the driver
answered sententiously.
He was a silent man, and seemed to consider
further expression of opinion needless. Suddenly
two dark spectres showed up in the distance.
“Man ridiu’ and leadin’ a pack-horse,” said the
driver. I was deeply interested.
We stopped, and the man and horses came up
beside us. It was not a pack-horse, but a great,
queerly-eolored saddle-horse that would have
adorned a circus.
The man peered into the coach-windows and
then addressed the driver, who was leisurely
fixing his whip lash.
“ Look here, have you got that young woman
wot’s bin nursin’ Jim MTutyre on board?”
This startled me. I was the young woman who
had nursed Jim MTntyre. The coach-driver
jerked his thumb at me.
“Kin yer ride a saddle-horse?” inquired the
new-comer.
I had kept my seat on the coach, and, after that,
I felt I could ride anything. The man eyed me
all over, and then said :
“ There’s a female took very bad suddink-like.
She heard about you last week, and now she’s
set on having you come and cure her.”
Jim MTntyre had died, so why the “female”
had faith in me, I did not know ; but, after some
further talk, I agreed to break my journey.
The coach-driver remarked : “If yer jine me
somewhere about here next trip, yer can ride out
yer passidge-money.”
He could not leave his horses, but he issued
large orders to the other man as to the best means
of hoisting me on to the roan creature’s back. I
draped my skirts as decently as circumstances
permitted. The man’s saddle was an awkward
thing to balance one’s-self on. The coach trundled
off down the winding road ; I jogged along un-
comfortably after it; and, as it finally disappeared,
I felt as if I had lost a friend. The bearded man
beside me proved to be even more taciturn than
the coach-driver. The only remark he made to
me during the three-mile ride was :
“ Wouldn’t it be better if yer straddled him?”
I privately thought it would; but modestly re-
frained.
• * *
I found that the “ female ” owned a roadside
hotel; her only son helping in the management.
She was a queer-looking woman, evidently suffer-
ing from a chronic complaint. I did what I could
to relieve her pain, and it was dark before I
finished my labors. The red-headed son came
into the room at intervals. He did not trouble to
knock, and seemed uncommonly interested in all
my proceedings. I was embarrassed ; but he and
his mother were not.
He suggested some tea for me, and said “Wot’ll
yer take ?”
“ Oh, anything !” I said.
He brought in several bottles of spirits, and
ranged them on the low mantelshelf; also some
ale, porter, and raspberry-vinegar. I begged for
some tea, and watched the son make it for me.
He made it in a jug, and .poured it into a tin
mug through the strainer of a soap-dish. I took
it with a thankful heart, and refused the stimu-
lants, but they were left on the shelf all night,
It was a night to be remembered. About nine
o’clock the population of the neighborhood in-
creased, and there were sounds of revelry in the
bar.
I was very tired* and lay down on the bed
beside my patient, turning the lamp low. The
woman’s pain had eased by this time, and she
was inclined to be talkative. The walls were
covered with newspapers, and in a little while the
newspapers were covered with perambulating
illustrations. Cockroaches, and beetles, and
spiders, and great, long, straw-colored things,
walked round about each other and made little
clicks against the paper. Mosquitos and flies
filled the room with a buzzing sound, and then I
felt sundry nips and bites which seemed beyond
the power even of Australian mosquitos. I turned
up the lamp. There were specimens of the
insect world on the bedclothes. They were very
lively. Some of them took exercise in erratic
jumps one after the other ; some preferred short
sprints on the flat. The latter looked as if they
were not built for the hurdles.
“I heard tell a lot of you while young MTntyre
was bad,” said my patient.
“Yes!” said I abstractedly, as I captured a
large, flat, black insect and put it in a watery
fl r S,Y e * , , , , , , ,
The noises on the verandah waxed louder, and
I looked at my patient wearily. I felt there was
little chance of sleep for her while she was worried
by such sounds.
Suddenly above the din a voice said, in tones
of bibulous determination—“ Fll shout for the
crowd I”
My patient opened her eyes and murmured
softly, “ Ned’s havin’ a grand night, thank God !”
Then it dawned on me that loud voices in a bar-
room might have a soothing effect on the licensee,
* * •
Daylight dawned, and the red-headed boy
] oo med into the room. He offered to “draw a
cork ” for me ; but I felt that nothing but chain-
pagne W ould suit my depressed condition, and
tbey bad none .
T be boy and Davis (the man who escorted me
f rom t be coach) spent most of the day asleep, and
j rummaged round for eatables, and fed on salt
mea t a nd badly-made bread.
There was an old nanny-goat, with a natural
] ong j ng to be milked; but milking is not an
accomplishment of mine, though I might have
done something with a breast-pump had nanny
submitted. Yet I was yearning for milk, and said
so.
When Nanny heard my patient calling to me,
she trotted into the bedroom and seemed to take
in the situation at a glance.
“ Would you be after bringing the dipper,
please, mum ? ”
When I brought the dipper Nanny was on the
bed, and she amused herself by nibbling the bed_
clothes while her overflowing udder was relieved'
It was a funny scene. But the invalid was not
hurt, and the jug-made tea was much improved.
I was given a huge dried snake-skin, and the
snake’s vertebral column, with a-box of birds’-
eggs and a dingo-skin, as “ presents.” There was
also a boomerang. I felt the gifts to be appro-
priate mementoes : and they adorn the walls of
our Nurses’ Home in the city.
* # #
A few days later, Davis took me to the coach.
The driver made room for me, beside him, in
silence. He said nothing until we had travelled
over a mile. Then :
“ Yer spiled yer looks that last shot! ” and he
eyed me critically.
“I did not sleep much for the insects,” I re-
marked.
“Nobody ever thinks of taking a bed there,”
he said.
After a pause of a quarter-mile, he added, “ It’s
a damn buggy shop ! ”
I quite agreed with him.
Melb.

“Mister” Gladstone has discovered that
the English language is in extensive use amongst
Germans, and regards this blessed fact—the com-
mercial reason for which is squalidly obvious to
ordinary persons —as an assurance of the strong
fraternal sentiment between the two countries,
which he trusted would long continue to be the
great security for the peace and happiness of the
world. Ghastly cant! At the very time when
France was in arms against Europe, French was
the only language in which the diplomats of the
Holy Alliance could transact their correspondence
with each other.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
Some Vic. justices put up a really good
record the other day. The son of another justice
was charged with embezzlement, and in the
ordinary course of things would have gone to a
higher court, where he might possibly have had a
bad time. But he offered to plead guilty to
larceny, that being a crime within the jurisdiction
of a J.P., and the charge was altered accordingly.
Then the accused pleaded guilty as promised, and
the bench promptly acquitted the, son of their
colleague without a stain on his character, on the
ground, no doubt, that he didn’t know what he
was talking about.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
Dear Bulletin, —Outside of his grandilo-
quence and disposition to drop into “poetry,”
Fitz Gibbon is not a bad sort of ex-Town Clerk.
He has done something for Melbourne. In
former times, Fitz. put himself out of the way to
obtain the reservation of public gardens and parks
for the people. More than that, he took care to
erect innumerable notice-boards, reminding the
Philistines that the gardens were their own pro-
perty, and exhorting them to behave accordingly.
Circumstances have brought all the vanity and
flunkeyism of Fitz Gibbon to the surface of his
character, but the average man might have
enjoyed all the Old Tin’s good luck and perquisites
without preserving half as much public spirit
through the ordeal. Compared with those civic
knights Bingy Benjy and Snowden, the blather-
ski ting C.M.G. pans out well.—Yours, Oilcan.
The Passing of Morrissey .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O’Flynn cloaked the symptoms of Morrissey’s dis-
order under a veil of professional slang. He said
also: “Within twenty-four hours he dies. The
evil he did ye, speculators and turfmen, was
according to our gospel. His hand and brain
made many paupers. To you, Alf., son of Levi,
I shall give thirty in a hundred. Marker, my
cue.”
A tall, red-whiskered man, known as “ Ginger,
the Sloth,” leaned across the baize: “Hith tote
wath a rotten thwindle, anyhow.”
Another gentleman, in a white-buff coat and
pince-nez, requested him, in the name of the
Messiah, to hold his peace. O’Flynn gave Alf. a
miss in baulk.
I was “marker” at the club where these
fashionable sports foregathered. The news of
Morrisey’s approaching end filled me with a
savage gladness. For had he not robbed, cheated,
and tricked me of an inheritance; had he not
reviled and abused me in public ?
Once he offered a piece of jewellery to my starv-
ing wife. My sleep is charged with nightmares
of Morrissey ; for ever my fingers are dabbling in
the fat of his hideous throat. Within 24 hours !
Oh, blessed doctor!
That night I stole to Morrissey’s residence.
They permitted me to sit beside him in his room.
The white heap in the bed moved; the grossness
of his nature defiled the image of God and man.
He peeped at me from his flesh-bagged cheeks;
he houghed, houghed as a sow with litter. I did
not speak ; we watched each other.
Violent reflex action would cause death imme-
diately, O’Flynn had said—O’Flynn the Daniel
of physicians. Violent reflex action. Once he
shifted, but those eyes, those little peeping eyes,
were always looking into mine. A distant clock
struck two. From my pocket I took a tortoise-
shell box. The lid flew back, causing me to sneeze
several times. I approached the door, closing it
very softly. His eyes now followed the box in
my hand. For the first time his lips moved.
“ Christ! If I could only do that, too. Dick,
I’m stuffed up—baked, I want to, to . , . my
head, the box, the box !”
“1n24 hours, Morrissey, less ”
“The box—hough, hough !”
I held the lid slightly open until it dropped in a
thin stream under the pug-nose and waited.
It seemed that he was trying to cough ; the bed
surged and creaked with his struggles ; a sudden
paroxysm bent him. He sneezed, and his was reflex
action : something popped like a cork. From his
eyes, mouth, and finger-ends spurted the crimson
fluid. I stole softly from the room.

Readers of The Bulletin, where-
